The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Second Figure Syllogism: Judgment is Not Perception
Proves via second figure syllogism that judgment is not perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the second figure categorical syllogism that proves judgment is not perception, and verify its validity using Cesare mood conditions.

Core Idea
The second figure syllogism shows that judgment is an act, perception is not an act, so no judgment is perception.

In this passage, the logicians prove that judgment (hukm) is distinct from perception (idrak) by using a categorical syllogism in the second figure. This is a crucial step in establishing that tasdiq (assent) is composed of three tasawwurat (conceptions) plus judgment, not four tasawwurat. The proof relies on the nature of perception as either passivity or quality, neither of which is an act. We will walk through the syllogism step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion being proven
The desired conclusion is that 'no judgment is perception' (i.e., judgment is not perception). This is what the syllogism aims to demonstrate.
2
Extract the major premise
The major premise is 'All judgments are acts'. This comes from the earlier claim that judgment is from the acts of the soul. It is a universal affirmative proposition.
Source: 'الحُكْمُ مِن أفعالِ النَّفْسِ' (judgment is from the acts of the soul)
3
Extract the minor premise
The minor premise is 'No perception is an act'. This is derived from the analysis that perception is either passivity or quality, neither of which is an act. It is a universal negative proposition.
Perception as passivity: 'الإدراك انفعال' ; as quality: 'يكون من مقولة الكيف'
4
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'act' (fi'l). It appears as the predicate in both premises: in the major premise it is predicated of judgment, and in the minor premise it is denied of perception.
In second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
5
Determine the figure and mood
The premises are: major: All judgments are acts (A); minor: No perception is an act (E). The conclusion: No judgment is perception (E). This is the Cesare mood of the second figure (AEE). It is valid.
Second figure conditions: major premise must be universal, one premise must be negative, and the mood must be Cesare (AEE) or Camestres (AEE with major E? actually Cesare is EAE? Wait, check: Cesare is major E, minor A, conclusion E? No, Cesare is: No P is M, All S is M, therefore No S is P. That is EAE. Here our major is A: All J is A, and minor is E: No I is A, so conclusion: No J is I. That's actually Festino? Festino is second figure: No P is M, Some S is M, therefore Some S is not P. So this is not standard. Let's re-evaluate carefully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُقَدِّمَةُ الماتن وإذا لم يكن الحكم إدراكاً؛ لم يكُن تصوراً ؛ الدسوقي والحاصلُ : أَنَّ التَّصديقَ مُركَّبٌ مِنَ التَصوُّراتِ الثَّلاثة، والحكم على هذا القول والذي قبلَهُ، إلا أنَّه على القول المتقدّم الحكم إدراك كيف أو انفعال، وعلى هذا القول ؛ الحكم فعل (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُن الْحُكْمُ ... إلخ) هذا بيان لكون التصديقِ مُركَّباً من تصورات ثلاثة والحكم، لا من أربعة تصورات . المطار وقبول الأثر، ولا يصدقُ أحدهما على ما صدق عليه الآخَرُ بالضَّرورة، وأمَّا أَنَّ الإدراك انفعال؛ فإنَّما يصح إذا فَسَّرْنَا الإدراك بانتعاش النَّفْسِ بالصُّورة الحاصلة مِنَ الشَّيء، وأمَّا إذا فَسَّرْنَاهُ بالصُّورةِ الحَاصِلَةِ في النَّفْسِ ؛ فيكون من مقولة الكيف؛ فلا يكون انفعالاً أيضاً .ا.هـ. قال عبد الحكيم: أي: فَلَا يكون الإدراك على هذا التَّقديرُ فِعلاً، كَمَا لا يكونُ على تقدير كونه انفعالاً، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّ القِياسَ المذكور في الشَّرح ؛ قياس على هيئةِ الشَّكْلِ الثَّاني من الموجبةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ والسَّالبةِ الكُليَّةِ، ينتج أنَّ الإدراكَ لا يكونُ فِعْلاً، وهذه النتيجة إذا ضُمَّتْ إلى الموجبة الكُلِّيَّةِ المستفادةِ مِن قَولِهِ : الحُكْمُ مِن أفعالِ النَّفْسِ ؛ يصيرُ القياسُ هَكَذا : الحُكْمُ فِعْلٌ، ولا شيء مِنَ الإدراكِ بِفعل، فَلا شيء مِنَ الحُكْم بإدراك، وهو المطلوب، وهكذا تقولُ على تقدير كون الإدراكِ كيفاً : الإدراك كيفٌ، والفعلُ لا يكونُ كَيفاً، فالإدراك لا يكون فعلاً، وهو بضمّ قولنا : الحُكْمُ فِعل؛ ينتج المطلوب ا.هـ. ، وقوله : المستفادة من قوله : (الحُكْمُ ... إلخ)؛ يعني قول شارحِ الشَّمسيَّةِ، وهذهِ الكُلِّيَّةُ تُؤخذُ مِن قَولِ شارحِنَا : وإِنْ كانَ فعلاً . (قَوْلُهُ: وَالحُكْمُ الأَوْلَى : والفِعلُ ؛ لأنَّ كونَ الحُكْمِ جزءاً؛ أصل المسألة، وقد يُقالُ : إنَّ المعنى والحُكْمَ الَّذي ثبت أنَّه فِعْل . (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِذَا لَمْ يَكُن الْحُكْمُ إِدْرَاكَاً ... إلخ) ظاهرُ السَّوقِ أَنَّهُ أرادَ مِنَ الإدراك الانفعال؛ لأنَّه الذي استدلَّ على مغايرةِ الحُكْمِ الَّذي هو فِعْل له، وحينئذٍ تمنعُ ملازمةُ الشَّرطيَّةِ بأنَّا لا نُسلّمُ أنه إذا لم يكن الحُكْمُ انفعالاً؛ لم يكن تصوراً، وسند
Introduction of the matn: And if judgment is not perception, then it is not a conception. Al-Dusuqi: The summary is that tasdiq is composed of three tasawwurat and the judgment, in this opinion and the previous one, except that in the previous opinion the judgment is a perception (quality or passivity), and in this opinion the judgment is an act. (His statement: 'and if judgment is not...etc.') This is an explanation that tasdiq is composed of three tasawwurat and the judgment, not of four tasawwurat. Al-Attar: ... and acceptance of the effect, and neither one applies to what the other applies to necessarily. As for perception being passivity, that is only correct if we interpret perception as the soul's receiving the form obtained from the thing. But if we interpret it as the form present in the soul, then it belongs to the category of quality, so it is not passivity either. End. Abd al-Hakim said: i.e., so perception on this interpretation is not an act, just as it is not on the interpretation of being passivity. In it is an indication that the syllogism mentioned in the commentary is a syllogism in the form of the second figure from a universal affirmative and a universal negative, producing that perception is not an act. And if this result is combined with the universal affirmative obtained from his statement 'judgment is from the acts of the soul', the syllogism becomes: Judgment is an act, and nothing from perception is an act, therefore nothing from judgment is perception, which is the desired conclusion. And similarly you say on the interpretation that perception is a quality: Perception is a quality, and an act is not a quality, so perception is not an act, and by adding our statement 'judgment is an act', the desired conclusion results. End. And his statement 'obtained from his statement (judgment... etc.)' means the statement of the commentator of al-Shamsiyya. And this universal is taken from our commentator's statement: 'and if it is an act'. (His statement: 'and the judgment is more appropriate: and the act'; because the fact that judgment is a part is the fundamental issue. And it may be said: the meaning is 'and the judgment which has been established as an act'. (His statement: 'and if judgment is not perception... etc.') The apparent flow is that he meant by 'perception' passivity, because that is what he used to argue for the distinctness of judgment which is an act from it. And at that point the implication of the conditional is blocked by saying: we do not accept that if judgment is not passivity, then it is not a conception. And the support...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents a syllogistic proof that judgment (hukm) is not perception (idrak), reinforcing the view that tasdiq is composed of three tasawwurat plus judgment. The matn states: 'If judgment is not perception, then it is not a tasawwur.' The commentator al-Dusuqi explains that this shows tasdiq has three tasawwurat plus judgment, not four tasawwurat. Al-Attar notes that perception may be interpreted as a quality, not passivity. Abd al-Hakim then explicitly formulates the syllogism in the second figure: Major premise: 'Judgment is an act' (from the earlier discussion). Minor premise: 'No perception is an act' (derived from the argument that perception is not an act, whether interpreted as passivity or quality). Conclusion: 'No judgment is perception.' This is a standard categorical syllogism in the second figure (Cesare mood). The text also addresses a potential objection: if 'perception' means passivity, one might deny the implication that not being passivity means not being a tasawwur. The argument relies on the nature of perception as either passivity or quality, neither of which can be an act, thereby establishing the distinctness of judgment.

Checkpoint
In the second figure syllogism presented, what is the middle term?
A) Judgment (hukm).
B) Perception (idrak).
C) Act (fi'l).
D) Tasdiq.

Key Takeaway
The second figure syllogism (Camestres mood) demonstrates that judgment is not perception, confirming that tasdiq consists of three tasawwurat plus judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the syllogism from this passage: write down the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion, and then convert it into standard categorical form. Check the mood and figure. Is it valid? Why?
?
Using the same second figure form (minor negative), construct a syllogism to prove that 'Qiyas al-ghaib 'ala al-shahid' (inference from the seen to the unseen) is a valid epistemological method in Kalam: Major: All valid methods produce certain knowledge. Minor: No invalid method produces certain knowledge. Conclusion: Therefore, no invalid method is a valid method. (Is this valid? What is the middle term?)

Unpacking the Purpose Clause in Nazar
Commentary on the purpose clause and its implications for the definition of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what the phrase 'li-tahsil al-majhul' in the definition of nazar excludes (single premise, intuition) and includes (syllogisms of equality and implication, reflection on second proof, corrupted thought), and justify each based on the purpose condition.

Core Idea
The purpose clause in a definition actively determines what counts as valid reasoning.

In Islamic logic, nazar is defined as observation for the acquisition of the unknown. But the phrase 'for acquisition' is not ornamental—it carries precise weight. This lesson examines how this purpose clause excludes certain types of reasoning (like a single premise or intuition) and conditionally includes others (like syllogisms of equality and implication). Understanding these exclusions and inclusions sharpens your ability to apply nazar in theological and juristic proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
The phrase 'li-tahsil al-majhul' has the lam of purpose, meaning the observation must be motivated by the desire to acquire the unknown. This is the central condition of nazar.
2
Understand the exclusion of a single premise
A single premise (muqaddima wahida) is excluded because its arrangement is not for acquiring the unknown but for acquiring the premise itself. It fails the purpose condition.
Example: If you already know 'every intoxicant is forbidden', and you observe the premise alone, you are not acquiring an unknown—you are confirming what you already have.
3
Understand the inclusion of syllogisms of equality and implication via 'aks al-naqid
Qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) and qiyas al-istilzam (syllogism of implication) are included when they lead to an unknown through conversion of the contradictory ('aks al-naqid). Even though some logicians exclude them because they lack self-evident implication, the author includes them because they can still serve the purpose of acquiring the unknown.
4
Understand the inclusion of reflection on the second proof
Reflecting on the second proof after the first is included because the goal is to know the mode of indication (wajh al-dalala) of the second proof, which is unknown. Even if the first proof already proved the conclusion, the second proof's specific way of showing it is new knowledge.
5
Understand the exclusion of intuition (hads)
Intuition (hads) is excluded because it is not intentional—it occurs suddenly without choice. Observation (ملاحظة) is defined as intentional orientation towards the known, and it is qualified by a purpose, which only applies to voluntary acts. Thus hads does not qualify as nazar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : لِتَحْصِيْلِ الْمَجْهُولِ اللَّامُ لِلأجل ؛ أي: الملاحظة التي يكونُ البَاعثُ عليها التحصيل ؛ فَخَرجَت المقدّمة الواحدة؛ لأنَّ التَّرتيب فيها ليسَ لِتحصيل المجهول ؛ بل لتحصيل المقدّمة، ودخل قياس المساواة والاستلزام بواسطة عَكْسِ النقيض، وإن أخرجُوهُما عن القياس لتقييدِهِم هناك الاستلزام؛ بأنْ يكونَ لِذاتِ القياس ولا لزوم فيهما بحسبِ الذَّاتِ، ودخل أيضاً النَّظَرُ فِي الدَّليل الثَّاني بعد الدليل الأوَّلِ ؛ لأنَّ المقصودَ مِنهُ العِلْمُ بوجه دلالتِهِ وهو مجهولٌ، وَإِنَّمَا قالَ: لتحصيل ، ولمْ يَقُلْ : بحيثُ يحصلُ مثلاً ؛ ليشمل الفكر الفاسد صورةً أو مادَّةٌ، وأما الحدس - وهو حصول المطلوب مع مباديه دفعةً - فَخَارج بقيدِ الملاحظة، كما قالَ الدَّوَانيُّ : إنَّ المراد بالملاحظة هوَ التَّوجه نحو المعلوم قصداً كما نبه عليه السياق؛ سيَّما وقدْ قُيِّدَ ،بالغاية، فإنَّها لا تكونُ إلَّا لِمَا هو حاصل بالاختيار؛ فلا نقضَ بالحدس ؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ بِقَصْدِ النَّفْسِ واختيارها ؛ بل يسنحُ بغير اختيار؛ إما عقيبَ شَوقٍ
His statement: 'for the acquisition of the unknown' – the lam is for purpose; meaning: the observation whose motive is acquisition. Thus a single premise is excluded because the arrangement therein is not for acquisition of the unknown but for acquisition of the premise. And the syllogism of equality and implication enter via conversion of the contradictory, even though they have excluded them from syllogism because they restrict implication there to being by the essence of the syllogism, and there is no necessary implication in them by essence. Also included is reflection upon the second proof after the first proof because the intended from it is knowledge of its mode of indication, which is unknown. And he only said 'for acquisition' and did not say 'such that acquisition occurs' for example, to include corrupted thought in form or matter. As for intuition (hads) – which is the attainment of the sought together with its principles at once – it is excluded by the condition of observation, as al-Dawwani said: what is meant by observation is the intentional orientation towards the known as the context indicates, especially since it is qualified by the purpose, for it is only for what occurs by choice. So there is no refutation by intuition because it is not by the soul's intention and choice, but rather it occurs without choice, either following a desire...
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the phrase 'لتحصيل المجهول' (for the acquisition of the unknown) in the definition of nazar. The author explains that the lam indicates purpose, so the definition means 'the observation whose motive is acquisition'. This excludes a single premise (muqaddima wahida) because in a single premise, the arrangement is for acquiring the premise itself, not for acquiring an unknown. It includes qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) and qiyas al-istilzam (syllogism of implication) when they use 'aks al-naqid (conversion of the contradictory), even though some logicians exclude these from qiyas because they restrict implication to what is by the essence of the syllogism, and these do not have necessary implication by essence. Also included is reflection on the second proof after the first proof, because the aim is to know its mode of indication, which is unknown. The author chose the phrase 'li-tahsil' (for acquisition) rather than 'bi-haythu yahsal' (such that acquisition occurs) to include corrupted thought (al-fikr al-fasid) in form or matter, which may not actually achieve acquisition. As for intuition (hads), which is the sudden attainment of the sought conclusion along with its principles, it is excluded by the condition of observation because observation is intentional and voluntary. Al-Dawwani states that observation is the intentional orientation towards the known, as the context indicates, and it is qualified by purpose, which only applies to voluntary acts. Intuition occurs without the soul's intention and choice, so it is not considered nazar.

Key Terms
تحصيل المجهول
قياس المساواة
قياس الاستلزام
حدس
عكس النقيض
ملاحظة
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a single premise (al-muqaddima al-wahida) excluded from the definition of nazar according to the passage?
A) Because it is not a valid logical argument.
B) Because its arrangement is not for acquiring the unknown but for acquiring the premise itself.
C) Because it is too simple to be considered observation.
D) Because it is based on intuition rather than reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The purpose clause 'li-tahsil al-majhul' in the definition of nazar is not mere decoration; it actively excludes non-purposeful reasoning (like intuition and self-referential premises) and includes any voluntary reasoning that aims at unknown knowledge, even if it fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a scenario: A scholar reflects on the Qur'anic verse 'Had there been within them gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' (al-Anbiya: 22). He works through the logical form: if multiple gods existed, the cosmos would be corrupted; observation of the cosmos shows no corruption; therefore, there is only one God. Is this nazar? Identify which elements satisfy the purpose condition and why.
?
Compare this with a person who suddenly 'intuits' the oneness of God without reasoning. According to the passage, is this intuition (hads) considered nazar? Explain using al-Dawwani's criterion.

The Syllogism

The Necessity of Knowing the Purpose: A Conditional Proof
Author demonstrates the proof by contradiction for knowing a science's purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (qiyas al-khalf) that proves a student must know the purpose before starting a science.

Core Idea
Starting a science without knowing its purpose is vain; a rational person avoids vain action.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi explains the logical structure underlying the matn's claim: the necessity of knowing the purpose (gharad) before embarking on a science. He reveals it as a proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) using a conditional argument. Understanding this proof sharpens our ability to appreciate why introductions to Islamic sciences always begin with stating the field's purpose and benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (al-matlub)
The conclusion to be proven is that every student of a science knows its purpose before starting. Note the exact phrasing: 'the entrant does not start into the science except if he knows the purpose.'
2
Assume the opposite (naqid)
The proof begins by assuming the negation: the student does not know the purpose. This is the 'if he does not know' in the text.
3
Derive the absurd consequence (al-thali)
From the assumption, the text derives that his pursuit would be 'vain' (عبث). This is because a science without known purpose could be useless, harmful, or not worth the effort.
4
Recognize the absurdity as false
A rational person does not engage in vain actions. Therefore, the statement 'his pursuit is vain' is false for a rational entrant.
5
Conclude by modus tollens
Since the consequent (vain pursuit) is false, the antecedent (not knowing the purpose) must also be false. Thus the desired conclusion is true: he does know the purpose.
This is modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'does not know', Q = 'pursuit is vain'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنَّما كانَ الشُّروعُ في مسائل العِلْم موقوفاً على: بيان الحاجة ; لأنَّ الشَّارعَ في العِلم لو لم يَعلَم الغَرَضَ مِنَ العِلْم؛ لكانَ طَلَيْه عَيّناً . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : لَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَم) أي : لو لم يصدق، وهذا إشارة إلى قياس الخلف، وهو إثبات المطلوبِ بإبطالِ نقيضهِ، فَالمطلوبُ عِلْمُ كل شارع الغرضَ مِنَ العِلْمِ، ونقيضُهُ عَدمُ عِلْمِهِ، لكنَّ الشَّارِحَ حذف الاستثنائية منه، فالأصلُ : لكانَ عبثاً ؛ أي: واللازم باطل، فَكَذا الملزوم . (قَوْلُهُ : الْغَرَضَ) أي : الحاجة. (قَوْلُهُ : عَبَنَا مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ يحتملُ أنَّ ذلك الفنّ لا فائدة له، أو له فائدة مضرّة، أو له فائدة لا تفي بتعبه في ذلك العِلْم ، وقوله : لَكانَ طلبه عبثاً ؛ أي: لكانَ الثَّالي باطل؛ لأنَّ العبثَ لا يليقُ بالعاقل، فبطل المقدَّم، فثبتَ أَنَّ الشَّارِعَ لا يحصلُ منهُ الشُّروعُ في العِلْمِ إلا إذا علم الغرَضَ من العِلْمِ، فيكونُ الشُّروع فيه متوقفاً على العِلْم بالغرض. العطار و غاياتِهَا ؛ لأنَّ كَوْنَ الطَّالبِ على بصيرةٍ مِمَّا ليسَ لَهُ معنِّى مُحصَّلٌ يقتضي الاقتصار على ما قصدوه، وعلى هذا ؛ لا يصلحُ تعريفُ المقدّمةِ بِمَا يتوقَّفُ عليهِ الشُّروعُ على وجهِ البصيرة، ولأنَّ تَمييزَ العِلْم عندَ الطَّالِبِ لا يتوقَّفُ على بيانِ الموضوع، بل يحصلُ بِجهاتٍ أُخَر ، نعم، تمايز العلوم في أنفسِهَا إِنَّما يكون بتمايز الموضوعاتِ، والفَرقُ ظَاهِرُ . (قَوْلُهُ : لَوْ لَمْ يَعْلَم الْغَرَضَ مِنَ الْعِلْم كلُّ مصلحة وحكمةٍ تترتب على فعل يُسمَّى غايةٌ؛ مِن حيثُ إِنَّها على طرفِ الفعل ونهايته، وفائدةً؛ من حيثُ ترتبها عليه، فيختلفان اعتباراً ويعمَّانِ الأفعال الاختياريَّةَ ،وغيرها، وأمَّا الغرضُ فَهو ما لأجلهِ إقدامُ الفاعِل على فعله، ويُسمَّى عِلَّةٌ غائيَّةٌ، ولا يوجد في أفعاله تعالى وإن جمَّتْ فوائدها، وقد يُخالف الغرضُ فائدة الفعل كما إذا أخطأ في اعتقادِهِ، قالَهُ السَّيِّدُ في حواشي الشَّرح العضدي للمختصرِ : فَقَولُ الشَّارِحِ : (لو لم يعلم الغرضَ ... إلخ)؛ أي: يعتقدُ إمَّا جَزماً أو ظنًّا الغرضَ مِنَ العِلْمِ، أي: الفائدة التي لها
And indeed, starting into the issues of a science is dependent upon: the clarification of need; because the one who enters into a science, if he does not know the purpose of the science, his seeking it would be vain. al-Dasuqi: (His statement: 'if he does not know') means: if he is not certain. This is an indication of a proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf), which is the proof of the desired by falsifying its opposite. The desired is that every entrant into a science knows its purpose, and its opposite is his not knowing it. But the commentator omitted the conditional from it, so the original is: 'it would be vain'; i.e., the necessary is false, so likewise the conditional (antecedent) is false. (His statement: 'the purpose') means: the need. (His statement: 'vain') from the perspective that it is possible that that art has no benefit, or has a harmful benefit, or has a benefit that does not compensate his effort in that science. His statement: 'its seeking would be vain' means the consequent is false because vainness is not befitting the rational person, so the antecedent is invalidated. So it is established that the entrant does not start into the science except if he knows the purpose of the science, so starting into it is dependent upon knowledge of the purpose. al-‘Attar: and its ends; because the student being insightful into something that has no obtained meaning necessitates limiting to what they intended. And based on this, the definition of the introduction as that upon which starting is dependent in a insightful manner is not suitable. And because the differentiation of a science for the student does not depend on stating the subject, but rather occurs through other aspects. Yes, the differentiation of sciences in themselves is only by the differentiation of subjects, and the difference is apparent. (His statement: 'if he does not know the purpose of the science'): every maslaha and wisdom that follows from an action is called a goal (ghaya) from the perspective that it is at the end of the action and its limit, and a benefit (fa'ida) from the perspective that it results from it. Thus they differ in consideration and encompass both voluntary and other actions. As for the purpose (gharad), it is that for the sake of which the agent proceeds to his action, and it is called the final cause. It does not exist in the acts of Allah, even if their benefits are many. And the purpose may differ from the benefit of the action, as when one errs in his belief. Said by al-Sayyid in his glosses on Sharh al-‘Adudi li al-Mukhtasar: So the statement of the commentator: 'if he does not know the purpose...etc.', means he believes, either with certainty or with opinion, the purpose of the science, i.e., the benefit that...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's statement that starting a science depends on clarifying the need (or purpose). The commentator al-Dasuqi explains the logical structure: it is a proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf). The desired conclusion is that every student knows the purpose. If we assume the opposite (that he does not know), then his pursuit would be vain (عبث), which is irrational. Since the consequent (vain pursuit) is false for a rational person, the antecedent (not knowing the purpose) must be false, thus establishing that he must know the purpose. Al-Dasuqi notes that the commentator omitted the explicit conditional form for brevity. Then al-‘Attar expands on the terminology: ghaya (end) is the benefit that results from an action, fa'ida (benefit) is the same considered from the perspective of result, while gharad (purpose) is the intention behind the action, which may differ from the actual benefit. The text distinguishes these concepts and notes that Allah's actions have benefits but no purpose in the sense of intention. The passage ends with a note from al-Sayyid that knowledge of the purpose can be certain or probable.

Key Terms
الغرض
قياس الخلف
العبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the qiyas al-khalf argument from the passage, what is the absurd consequence that proves the student must know the purpose?
A) The student is ignorant
B) The student's pursuit is vain (عبث)
C) The student is rational
D) The student does not benefit

Key Takeaway
The necessity of knowing a science's purpose is proven by a conditional proof by contradiction: if one did not know, pursuit would be vain, which is absurd, so one must know.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fiqh claim: 'Alcohol from grapes is forbidden because it is intoxicating.' Construct a qiyas al-khalf argument to prove that any intoxicating drink is forbidden. Assume the opposite: a drink is intoxicating but permissible. Derive an absurd consequence (e.g., contradiction with a clear text).
?
In a kalam debate, a Mu'tazili claims that human acts are not created by Allah. How would you use qiyas al-khalf to refute this? Outline the conditional and the absurdity.

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
Qiyas al-Khalf: Proof by Contradiction in Islamic Logic
Author explains the structure of qiyas al-khalf using the science-purpose example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a valid qiyas al-khalf argument in any Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves truth by showing the falsehood of the opposite.

Qiyas al-khalf is a powerful and frequently used argument form in Islamic logic, theology, and jurisprudence. The passage introduces it explicitly as the method behind the proof that a student must know a science's purpose. In this lesson, we break down its structure and applications so you can use it in your own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired proposition (matlub)
Start by stating what you want to prove. For example, 'this transaction is valid' or 'the world has a creator.'
2
Formulate the negation (naqid)
Assume the opposite of the desired proposition. This is the starting point for the contradiction.
3
Deduce an absurd consequence (al-thali al-batil)
From the assumption, derive a logical consequence that is clearly false, contradictory, or impossible according to established principles (rational, textual, or empirical).
4
Conclude the falsity of the assumption
Since the consequence is absurd, the assumption (negation) must be false. Therefore, the original desired proposition is true.
5
Check the validity of the deduction
The deduction must be valid—if the assumption leads to an absurdity, the reasoning must be sound. Ensure no fallacy is committed.

Distinguishing Gharad, Ghaya, and Fa'ida: Precision in Defining Purpose
Author distinguishes gharad, ghaya, and fa'ida.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between gharad (purpose), ghaya (end), and fa'ida (benefit), and apply these distinctions to analyze Islamic legal and theological concepts.

Core Idea
The purpose (gharad) is the agent's intention, distinct from the end (ghaya) and benefit (fa'ida).

The passage from al-‘Attar clarifies three closely related but distinct terms: gharad (purpose), ghaya (end/goal), and fa'ida (benefit). This precision is critical in Islamic sciences, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam, where the intention behind an action or ruling can change its legal or theological status. In this lesson, we dissect the definitions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Define gharad (purpose)
Al-‘Attar states that gharad is 'that for the sake of which the agent proceeds to his action,' i.e., the final cause. It is the motivating intention. Note that Allah does not have a gharad in His actions, though they have benefits.
Gharad is agent-relative.
2
Define ghaya (end)
Ghaya is 'every maslaha and wisdom that follows from an action' considered as the terminal point (taraf al-fi‘l wa nihayatuhu). It is the end result.
3
Define fa'ida (benefit)
Fa'ida is the same benefit considered as a result (min haythu tarattubiha ‘alayhi). So ghaya and fa'ida are two aspects of the same thing: ghaya as end, fa'ida as benefit.
4
Compare and contrast
Gharad can differ from fa'ida: an agent may intend (gharad) something but get a different benefit, or err in intention. Ghaya and fa'ida are objective consequences, while gharad is subjective intention.
5
Apply to Islamic concepts
In usul al-fiqh, the gharad of the Lawgiver (Allah) is not applicable, but His ahkam have ghaya and fa'ida (masalih). Understanding this prevents anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(His statement: 'the purpose') means: the need. (His statement: 'vain') from the perspective that it is possible that that art has no benefit, or has a harmful benefit, or has a benefit that does not compensate his effort in that science. His statement: 'its seeking would be vain' means the consequent is false because vainness is not befitting the rational person, so the antecedent is invalidated. So it is established that the entrant does not start into the science except if he knows the purpose of the science, so starting into it is dependent upon knowledge of the purpose. al-‘Attar: and its ends; because the student being insightful into something that has no obtained meaning necessitates limiting to what they intended. And based on this, the definition of the introduction as that upon which starting is dependent in a insightful manner is not suitable. And because the differentiation of a science for the student does not depend on stating the subject, but rather occurs through other aspects. Yes, the differentiation of sciences in themselves is only by the differentiation of subjects, and the difference is apparent. (His statement: 'if he does not know the purpose of the science'): every maslaha and wisdom that follows from an action is called a goal (ghaya) from the perspective that it is at the end of the action and its limit, and a benefit (fa'ida) from the perspective that it results from it. Thus they differ in consideration and encompass both voluntary and other actions. As for the purpose (gharad), it is that for the sake of which the agent proceeds to his action, and it is called the final cause. It does not exist in the acts of Allah, even if their benefits are many. And the purpose may differ from the benefit of the action, as when one errs in his belief. Said by al-Sayyid in his glosses on Sharh al-‘Adudi li al-Mukhtasar: So the statement of the commentator: 'if he does not know the purpose...etc.', means he believes, either with certainty or with opinion, the purpose of the science, i.e., the benefit that...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's statement that starting a science depends on clarifying the need (or purpose). The commentator al-Dasuqi explains the logical structure: it is a proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf). The desired conclusion is that every student knows the purpose. If we assume the opposite (that he does not know), then his pursuit would be vain (عبث), which is irrational. Since the consequent (vain pursuit) is false for a rational person, the antecedent (not knowing the purpose) must be false, thus establishing that he must know the purpose. Al-Dasuqi notes that the commentator omitted the explicit conditional form for brevity. Then al-‘Attar expands on the terminology: ghaya (end) is the benefit that results from an action, fa'ida (benefit) is the same considered from the perspective of result, while gharad (purpose) is the intention behind the action, which may differ from the actual benefit. The text distinguishes these concepts and notes that Allah's actions have benefits but no purpose in the sense of intention. The passage ends with a note from al-Sayyid that knowledge of the purpose can be certain or probable.

الغرض
الغاية
الفائدة

Key Takeaway
Gharad is the agent's intention, distinct from the objective end (ghaya) and benefit (fa'ida); this distinction is vital for precise theological and legal discourse.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditional Syllogism Against Universal Indication of Words
Author explains the conditional syllogism that refutes the claim that a word indicates every external thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate the conditional syllogism used to refute the claim that a word indicates every external thing.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism refutes universal indication of words by showing it implies infinite meanings, which is absurd.

This lesson examines a classical logical argument about how words indicate meanings. The claim that a word indicates every external thing leads to an absurd consequence: every word would then indicate infinite meanings. Using a conditional syllogism (modus tollens), the logicians prove this claim false, establishing that words require a condition for their indication. The commentaries of al-Dasuqi and al-Attar clarify the syllogistic structure and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
The conditional is: 'If the word indicates every external thing (antecedent), then every word placed for a meaning is indicative of infinite meanings (consequent).' Find these in the Arabic as 'لو دل اللفظ على كل خارج' and 'لكان كل لفظ موضوع لمعنى دالا على معان غير متناهية'.
The antecedent is the claim being refuted; the consequent is the necessary consequence.
2
Understand the necessary consequence (lazim)
The consequent follows from the antecedent because 'every external thing' includes an infinite set of existents and non-existents. If a word indicated every external, it would have to signify all these infinite meanings, which is impossible in reality.
The equality of all words in being 'placed' (wad') means the same consequence would apply to every word.
3
Recognize the falsity of the consequent
The consequent is false because when a word is uttered, the speaker and listener do not attend to infinite meanings. This is empirical and obvious. The commentators emphasize this point to establish the falsity of the consequent (Tali batil).
The falsity of the consequent is 'zahir' (apparent) as per al-Attar.
4
Apply modus tollens to infer the falsity of the antecedent
In a conditional syllogism, if the consequent is false, the antecedent must also be false. Here, 'If P then Q' and 'not Q', therefore 'not P'. This is a valid form of reasoning.
This step is the core of the proof: it disproves the universal indication claim without needing to prove the opposite directly.
5
Conclude the opposite: words need a condition
The falsity of the antecedent establishes its negation: the word does not indicate every external thing without a condition. Therefore, a condition (shart) is necessary for such indication. This refutation paves the way for the principle that linguistic indication is constrained.
The conclusion is not that words indicate nothing external, but that they do so only under certain conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا؛ لزم أن يكون كل لفظ موضوع لمعنى دالا على معان غير متناهية؛ وهو باطل.
الدسوقي
(قوله : وإلا ، لزم ... إلخ) أي : وإلا بأن دل اللفظ الموضوع لمعنى على كل أمر خارج، والحال : أن الألفاظ الموضوعة متساوية في كونها موضوعة؛ لزم أن يكون كل لفظ موضوع دالا على معان غير متناهية؛ لشمول المعاني للموجودات والمعدومات.
(قوله : وهو باطل) أي : أن هذا اللازم باطل، وهو دلالة اللفظ على معان غير متناهية؛ أي: وإذا بطل اللازم؛ بطل المقدم، وهو دلالة اللفظ على كل خارج، وإذا بطل هذا ثبت نقيضه، وهو أنه لا يدل على كل خارج، بل لا بد من شرط؛ فقوله: (فلا بد ... إلخ)؛ تفريع على بطلان اللازم؛ ليرتب عليه بطلان المقدم المترتب عليه ثبوت نقيض المقدم؛ أي: لأنه ليس عندنا لفظ يدل على معان غير متناهية؛ أي : لعدم الالتفات عند إطلاق لفظ منها إلى المعاني الغير المتناهية؛ لا إجمالا ولا تفصيلا.
العطار
(قوله : وإلا؛ لزم قياس استثنائي تقريره : هكذا : لو دل اللفظ على كل خارج؛ لزم أن كل لفظ موضوع لمعنى دال على معان غير متناهية، والتالي باطل، فبطل المقدم؛ فثبت نقيضه وهو المدعى، وكل من اللازم وبطلان التالي ظاهر، قال الفاضل عبد الحكيم: لو دل اللفظ الموضوع على كل أمر خارج؛ والحال أن الألفاظ الموضوعة متساوية في كونها موضوعة؛ لزم أن يكون كل لفظ معان غير متناهية؛ لشمولها الموجودات والمعدومات تفصيلا وإجمالا لخروجها عن الموضوع له، وهو ظاهر البطلان؛ لعدم الالتفات عند إطلاق لفظ منها إلى المعاني الغير المتناهية؛ لا إجمالا ولا تفصيلا. ا.هـ.، وبهذا؛ ظهر سر عدول الشارح عن الظاهر وهو قوله: وإلا؛ لزم أن يكون دالا إلى ما ذكره لما أن الألفاظ متساوية في ذلك؛ فنية بالعدول عليه.
Otherwise, it would necessarily follow that every word placed for a meaning is indicative of infinite meanings, and that is false. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: Otherwise, it would necessarily follow ... etc.) That is, if the word placed for a meaning indicates every external thing, while the situation is that words placed are equal in being placed, it would necessarily follow that every word placed is indicative of infinite meanings, because meanings include existents and non-existents. (His saying: And that is false) That is, this necessary consequence is false, namely the word's indication of infinite meanings. That is, when the consequent is false, the antecedent is false, namely the word's indication of every external. And when this is false, its opposite is established, namely that it does not indicate every external, but rather a condition is necessary. So his saying (Thus a condition is necessary ... etc.) is a branching from the falsity of the consequent, so as to establish the falsity of the antecedent, upon which the establishment of the opposite of the antecedent depends. That is, because we do not have a word that indicates infinite meanings; that is, due to the lack of attention when uttering a word to infinite meanings, neither in summary nor in detail. Al-Attar: (His saying: Otherwise, it would necessarily follow) A conditional syllogism whose formulation is thus: If the word indicated every external, then every word placed for a meaning would be indicative of infinite meanings. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is false; thus its opposite is established, which is the claim. Each of the necessary consequence and the falsity of the consequent are apparent. Al-Fadil Abd al-Hakim said: If a word placed for a meaning indicated every external thing, while the situation is that words placed are equal in being placed, then every word would indicate infinite meanings, because meanings include existents and non-existents in detail and in summary, due to their being outside of what it is placed for. This is clearly false, because of the lack of attention when uttering a word to infinite meanings, neither in summary nor in detail. End quote. By this, the reason for the commentator's deviation from the apparent meaning becomes clear, which is his saying: Otherwise, it would necessarily follow that it indicates... to what he mentioned, since words are equal in that; so the intention in deviation is upon it.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage explains a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) used to refute the claim that a word indicates every external thing. The argument is structured as: if a word indicated every external thing (the antecedent), then every word would indicate infinite meanings (the consequent) because external things are infinite. The consequent is false (we do not actually understand infinite meanings from any word), so the antecedent is false. Therefore, a word does not indicate every external thing; rather, a condition is needed for such indication. The commentators al-Dasuqi and al-Attar elaborate: the falsity of the consequent is obvious because when we hear a word, we do not consider infinite meanings. Abd al-Hakim adds that the equality of all words in being ‘placed’ means that if one word indicated every external, all would, leading to the absurdity of infinite indications. The commentary also notes why the commentator deviated from the apparent wording of the matn, turning 'dalla' (indicates) into 'dallan' (indicative) to fit the syllogistic structure.

Key Terms

اللازم

Key Takeaway
The conditional syllogism shows that if a word indicated every external, it would imply infinite meanings, which is false, so words require a condition for their indication.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Reconstructing the Syllogism for Tadamman and Iltizam
Commentator proves that derivative significations depend on primary signification and addresses al-Razi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first-figure categorical syllogism that proves tadamman and iltizam require mutabaqa, and explain why the qualification 'insofar as it is dependent' must attach to the predicate.

Core Idea
The dependent's existence as a dependent depends entirely on its object of dependence.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a dense syllogism that underpins the relationship between the three types of signification (dalala). He shows that tadamman and iltizam are dependent (tabi'an) on mutabaqa, and then uses a first-figure syllogism to prove they cannot exist without it. However, a subtle qualification is needed to ensure logical validity. We'll walk through the syllogism step by step and see why the qualifier 'insofar as it is dependent' is placed on the predicate, not the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is 'Tadamman and iltizam are dependent on mutabaqa.' In the syllogism, this serves as the minor premise because it predicates 'dependent on mutabaqa' of tadamman and iltizam.
The middle term is 'dependent on mutabaqa' (or just 'dependent' as a concept).
2
Identify the major premise
The major premise is 'Every dependent, insofar as it is dependent, does not exist without its object of dependence.' This is a universal affirmative statement about the property of being dependent.
The qualifier 'insofar as it is dependent' specifies the precise condition.
3
Determine the middle term
The middle term is 'dependent' (tabi') in the qualified sense. It appears in the minor premise as part of the predicate ('dependent on mutabaqa') and in the major premise as the subject ('the dependent'). For the syllogism to be valid, the middle term must be identical in both premises.
The qualifier ensures that the subject of the major premise refers specifically to things considered as dependent, not to any dependent thing in any state.
4
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Therefore, tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa.' Since they are subject to the minor premise, and the major premise says all dependents (as dependents) require their object, the conclusion follows logically.
This is a valid first-figure syllogism if the middles match.
5
Verify the placement of the qualifier
The commentator emphasizes that the qualifier 'insofar as it is dependent' is attached to the predicate of the major premise, not the subject. If it were attached to the subject, the middle term would not be identical because the subject of the minor premise (tadamman/iltizam) is not qualified as 'dependent' in the same way.
The correct logical form is: All dependents (as such) are such that they do not exist without their object. The 'as such' qualifies the predicate, not the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهُمَا تَابِعَانِ ... إلخ) قياس من الشَّكل الأوَّلِ، وقولُهُ: من حيثُ إِنَّهُ تابع ؛ تقييد للمحمول لا للموضوع؛ لئلا يردُّ عدم اتحادِ الوسط . وفيهِ قياس اقتراني هكذا التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام تابعان للمطابقة، والتَّابِعُ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ تَابِعٌ ؛ لا يوجد بدونِ متبوعه، ينتجُ : التَّضَمُّنُ والالتزام لا يُوجدانِ بِدونِها . وأمَّا الكُبرى؛ فَظاهرةٌ ، وإِنَّما قُيّدَ بِالحيثيَّةِ؛ لأنَّ التَّابِعَ قد يوجد بدونِ المتبوع، لكن لا يكون في تلك الحالةِ تَابِعَاً؛ كالحرارةِ التّابعةِ للنَّارِ؛ فَإِنَّها توجدُ مَعَ الشَّمسِ لكنْ لا تكون تابعةً للنَّارِ، فظهر أنَّ قيد الحيثيَّةِ مُعتبر في جانبِ المحمول؛ لا أَنَّهُ قيد في الموضوع، وهو التَّابِعُ؛ لأنَّهُ لَوْ جُعِلَ قَيْداً فيهِ؛ لَما تكرَّرَ الحدُّ الوسط، والمعنى حينئذٍ أنَّ كُلَّ تابع لا يوجد بدونِ مَتبوعِهِ مَوصوفاً بالتَّبعيَّةِ لَهُ، اللازمَ مِنَ الدَّليلِ حينئذٍ أنَّ التَّضمُّنَ والالتزامَ لا يُوجدانِ بدونِ المطابقة موصُوفَيْنِ بصفَةِ التَّبعيَّة للمطابقة، والمقصود أنَّهما لا يوجدانِ بدونِهَا مُطلقاً، وأجابَ بعضُ الفُضَلاءِ بأنَّ للتَّقييدِ بالحيثيَّةِ اعتبارين؛ أحدهما : أن يكون قيداً للحدثِ؛ فَحينئذٍ تفيدُ التَّبعيَّةُ مُقيَّدةٌ، والثَّاني : أنْ تكون قيداً لانتسابِ الحدثِ إلى الفاعل؛ فتؤولُ حينئذ إلى المشروطة أو العرفية العامتين، كأنَّهُ قِيلَ: وَكُلُّ تَابِعِ مَا دَامَ تابعاً؛ لا يوجد بدونِ المتبوع، فالصغرى دائمةٌ مع إحدى العامَّتَيْنِ، تنتج دائمةً كَمَا هو مذكورٌ في الموجّهات، فينتجُ : التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام لا يوجدانِ بدونِ المتبوع دائماً؛ وهو المطلوب، وقد نقضَ الرَّازِيُّ في شَرْحِ المطالعِ الدَّليلَ نَقْضاً إجماليا فقالَ: لَو صح البيانُ لاستلزمَتِ المطابقةُ التَّضمُّنَ والالتزام؛ لأنَّها متبوعةٌ، والمتبوع مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ متبوع؛ لا يوجد بدونِ التَّابع ا.هـ. وَنِعْمَ مَا قالَ السَّيِّدُ في حاشيةِ الشَّمسيَّةِ بعد أن أوردَ مُناقشاتٍ على الدَّليل المذكور : الأَوْلَى في بيانِ استلزامهما المطابقة أنْ يُقَالَ : هُمَا يَستلزمانِ الوضعَ المستلزم للمطابقة؛ فيستلزمَانِها قطعاً ا.هـ. لأنَّ المستلزم للمستلزم للشَّيء؛ مُستلزم لِذلكَ الشَّيء.
His statement: 'Because they are dependent... etc.' is a syllogism of the first figure. And his statement: 'insofar as it is dependent' is a qualification of the predicate, not of the subject, to avoid the objection of non-identity of the middle term. And it contains a conjunctive syllogism as follows: Accidental signification (tadamman) and implied signification (iltizam) are dependent on the primary signification (mutabaqa). And the dependent insofar as it is dependent does not exist without its object of dependence. This yields: Tadamman and iltizam do not exist without it. As for the major premise, it is evident. The qualification with 'insofar as' is because the dependent may exist without its object of dependence, but in that case it is not a dependent; like the heat that depends on fire: it is found with the sun but is not dependent on the fire. Thus it becomes clear that the qualification of 'insofar as' is considered on the side of the predicate, not that it is a qualification in the subject, i.e., 'the dependent', because if it were made a qualification in it, the middle term would not be repeated. The meaning then is that every dependent does not exist without its object of dependence while being described as dependence upon it. The necessary consequence of the proof is that tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa while being described as dependence upon mutabaqa, but the intended meaning is that they do not exist without it absolutely. Some virtuous scholars answered that the qualification by 'insofar as' has two considerations: one is that it is a qualification of the event, so then it conveys qualified dependence. The second is that it is a qualification of the attribution of the event to the agent, so it then amounts to the conditional or the universal customary, as if it were said: 'Every dependent as long as it is dependent does not exist without its object of dependence.' So the minor premise is perpetual with one of the two universals, and it yields a perpetual conclusion as is mentioned in modal logic. Thus it results: Tadamman and iltizam do not exist without their object of dependence perpetually; that is what is sought. Al-Razi in his commentary on al-Matali' criticized the proof with a general criticism saying: If the explanation were valid, then mutabaqa would necessitate tadamman and iltizam, because it is the object of dependence, and the object of dependence insofar as it is an object of dependence does not exist without the dependent. What al-Sayyid said in his gloss on al-Shamsiyya, after presenting some discussions on the aforementioned proof, is excellent: 'The best way to show that they necessitate mutabaqa is to say: They necessitate the subject of signification (wad') which necessitates mutabaqa, so they necessitate it necessarily.' Because what necessitates that which necessitates a thing necessitates that thing.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) analyzing a syllogism that proves the relationship between the three types of signification (al-dalalat al-thalath). The commentator is expanding on a statement in the matn that says: 'Because they (tadamman and iltizam) are dependent (tabi'an) on mutabaqa.' He first clarifies that this statement is a categorical syllogism of the first figure. The syllogism is: (1) Tadamman and iltizam are dependent on mutabaqa. (2) The dependent, insofar as it is dependent, does not exist without its object of dependence. Conclusion: Tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa. The commentator notes that the qualification 'insofar as it is dependent' is attached to the predicate (mahmul) of the major premise, not to the subject (mawdu'), to avoid the logical error of a non-identical middle term (middle term must be the same in both premises). If it were attached to the subject, the middle term would not be identical because the subject of the minor premise (tadamman/iltizam) is not qualified that way. The major premise is self-evident: a dependent qua dependent cannot exist without its object of dependence. The reason for this qualification is that a dependent can exist without its object of dependence in some other context, but then it is not considered a dependent in that context. For example, heat is dependent on fire (as its cause), but heat can be found from the sun without being dependent on fire. So the qualification ensures that we refer to the thing specifically as a dependent. Thus, the qualification belongs to the predicate ('does not exist without its object of dependence while being described as dependent'), not to the subject. If it were a qualification of the subject, it would change the middle term. The correct understanding is: 'Every dependent (while being described as dependent) does not exist without its object of dependence.' The conclusion then is that tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa while they are described as dependent. But the intended conclusion is absolute non-existence. To resolve this, some scholars propose that the qualification has two interpretations: (a) as a qualifier of the event (hadath), meaning 'dependent existence as such' – this yields a restricted conclusion; (b) as a qualifier of the attribution of the event to the agent, transforming it into a conditional or universal-customary modality: 'Every dependent, as long as it is dependent, does not exist without its object.' This, with a perpetual minor premise, yields a perpetual conclusion through modal logic. Thus, the desired absolute conclusion is obtained. Al-Razi then objects: If the explanation were sound, then mutabaqa (as the object of dependence) would also necessitate tadamman and iltizam, because the object of dependence, insofar as it is an object of dependence, does not exist without the dependent. This would imply mutual dependence, which is absurd. Al-Sayyid provides a better approach: Instead of focusing on the direct relationship, we should note that tadamman and iltizam necessitate the act of signification (wad'), which itself necessitates mutabaqa. Therefore, by transitivity, they necessitate mutabaqa. This avoids the reciprocity problem. The commentator endorses al-Sayyid's solution.

A) It restricts the subject of the minor premise to avoid a false premise.

C) It makes the syllogism a second-figure syllogism.

Key Takeaway
The first-figure syllogism is a precise tool to show that derivative signification depends on primary signification, but the logical formulation must carefully manage qualifications to avoid fallacies.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Resolving Al-Razi's Objection: Proving the Dependence via Transitivity
Commentator proves that derivative significations depend on primary signification and addresses al-Razi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in al-Razi's objection and explain how al-Sayyid's alternative proof using transitivity avoids the reciprocality problem.

Core Idea
Reciprocality is avoided by proving that tadamman and iltizam necessitate mutabaqa through a transitive chain.

After presenting the syllogism, the commentator recounts al-Razi's objection: If we prove that tadamman/iltizam depend on mutabaqa, we could also prove that mutabaqa depends on them, which is absurd. To address this, al-Sayyid offers a superior argument: tadamman and iltizam necessitate the very act of signification (wad'), which in turn necessitates mutabaqa. This transitive approach avoids the mutual dependence problem. We'll examine both the objection and the solution.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that if the commentator's syllogism were sound, then by symmetry, mutabaqa (as the object of dependence) would also necessitate tadamman and iltizam. This would imply that the three types of signification are mutually dependent, which cannot be the case because mutabaqa is primary.
The objection turns on the same logical structure: if 'dependent requires object' then 'object requires dependent'. But the commentary's syllogism uses a qualified major premise; al-Razi claims the same reasoning would apply to the converse.
2
Identify the fallacy in al-Razi's reasoning
The fallacy lies in assuming that the relationship is symmetrical. In the commentator's syllogism, the major premise is about the dependent (tabi') conditionally: 'insofar as it is dependent'. To apply the same reasoning to the object of dependence (matbu'), one would need a similar conditional premise: 'the object of dependence, insofar as it is an object of dependence, does not exist without the dependent.' But this premise may not be true: the object of dependence can exist without the dependent (e.g., fire can exist without heat). So al-Razi's objection is invalid because it uses an unqualified premise.
The commentator does not explicitly refute this, but al-Sayyid provides a different approach.
3
Examine al-Sayyid's alternative proof
Al-Sayyid argues: Tadamman and iltizam necessitate the existence of the conventional signification (wad') — the act of assigning meaning to a word. This wad' in turn necessitates mutabaqa (the primary signification) because the whole is signified by the same word. By transitivity, tadamman and iltizam necessitate mutabaqa.
This avoids any claim about mutabaqa depending on them; it only shows that if tadamman/iltizam exist, then mutabaqa must also exist (since they all depend on the same wad').
4
Validate the transitivity
The argument is valid if the premises are true: (1) Tadamman and iltizam necessitate wad' (because signification of parts or implications requires that the word was posited for the whole meaning). (2) Wad' necessitates mutabaqa (because the word's primary meaning is the whole, which is what wad' directly sets). Therefore, tadamman and iltizam necessitate mutabaqa. This is a valid hypothetical syllogism (if A then B, if B then C, therefore if A then C).
This proof is simpler and avoids the reciprocality objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهُمَا تَابِعَانِ ... إلخ) قياس من الشَّكل الأوَّلِ، وقولُهُ: من حيثُ إِنَّهُ تابع ؛ تقييد للمحمول لا للموضوع؛ لئلا يردُّ عدم اتحادِ الوسط . وفيهِ قياس اقتراني هكذا التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام تابعان للمطابقة، والتَّابِعُ مِنْ حيثُ هُوَ تَابِعٌ ؛ لا يوجد بدونِ متبوعه، ينتجُ : التَّضَمُّنُ والالتزام لا يُوجدانِ بِدونِها . وأمَّا الكُبرى؛ فَظاهرةٌ ، وإِنَّما قُيّدَ بِالحيثيَّةِ؛ لأنَّ التَّابِعَ قد يوجد بدونِ المتبوع، لكن لا يكون في تلك الحالةِ تَابِعَاً؛ كالحرارةِ التّابعةِ للنَّارِ؛ فَإِنَّها توجدُ مَعَ الشَّمسِ لكنْ لا تكون تابعةً للنَّارِ، فظهر أنَّ قيد الحيثيَّةِ مُعتبر في جانبِ المحمول؛ لا أَنَّهُ قيد في الموضوع، وهو التَّابِعُ؛ لأنَّهُ لَوْ جُعِلَ قَيْداً فيهِ؛ لَما تكرَّرَ الحدُّ الوسط، والمعنى حينئذٍ أنَّ كُلَّ تابع لا يوجد بدونِ مَتبوعِهِ مَوصوفاً بالتَّبعيَّةِ لَهُ، اللازمَ مِنَ الدَّليلِ حينئذٍ أنَّ التَّضمُّنَ والالتزامَ لا يُوجدانِ بدونِ المطابقة موصُوفَيْنِ بصفَةِ التَّبعيَّة للمطابقة، والمقصود أنَّهما لا يوجدانِ بدونِهَا مُطلقاً، وأجابَ بعضُ الفُضَلاءِ بأنَّ للتَّقييدِ بالحيثيَّةِ اعتبارين؛ أحدهما : أن يكون قيداً للحدثِ؛ فَحينئذٍ تفيدُ التَّبعيَّةُ مُقيَّدةٌ، والثَّاني : أنْ تكون قيداً لانتسابِ الحدثِ إلى الفاعل؛ فتؤولُ حينئذ إلى المشروطة أو العرفية العامتين، كأنَّهُ قِيلَ: وَكُلُّ تَابِعِ مَا دَامَ تابعاً؛ لا يوجد بدونِ المتبوع، فالصغرى دائمةٌ مع إحدى العامَّتَيْنِ، تنتج دائمةً كَمَا هو مذكورٌ في الموجّهات، فينتجُ : التَّضمُّنُ والالتزام لا يوجدانِ بدونِ المتبوع دائماً؛ وهو المطلوب، وقد نقضَ الرَّازِيُّ في شَرْحِ المطالعِ الدَّليلَ نَقْضاً إجماليا فقالَ: لَو صح البيانُ لاستلزمَتِ المطابقةُ التَّضمُّنَ والالتزام؛ لأنَّها متبوعةٌ، والمتبوع مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ متبوع؛ لا يوجد بدونِ التَّابع ا.هـ. وَنِعْمَ مَا قالَ السَّيِّدُ في حاشيةِ الشَّمسيَّةِ بعد أن أوردَ مُناقشاتٍ على الدَّليل المذكور : الأَوْلَى في بيانِ استلزامهما المطابقة أنْ يُقَالَ : هُمَا يَستلزمانِ الوضعَ المستلزم للمطابقة؛ فيستلزمَانِها قطعاً ا.هـ. لأنَّ المستلزم للمستلزم للشَّيء؛ مُستلزم لِذلكَ الشَّيء.
His statement: 'Because they are dependent... etc.' is a syllogism of the first figure. And his statement: 'insofar as it is dependent' is a qualification of the predicate, not of the subject, to avoid the objection of non-identity of the middle term. And it contains a conjunctive syllogism as follows: Accidental signification (tadamman) and implied signification (iltizam) are dependent on the primary signification (mutabaqa). And the dependent insofar as it is dependent does not exist without its object of dependence. This yields: Tadamman and iltizam do not exist without it. As for the major premise, it is evident. The qualification with 'insofar as' is because the dependent may exist without its object of dependence, but in that case it is not a dependent; like the heat that depends on fire: it is found with the sun but is not dependent on the fire. Thus it becomes clear that the qualification of 'insofar as' is considered on the side of the predicate, not that it is a qualification in the subject, i.e., 'the dependent', because if it were made a qualification in it, the middle term would not be repeated. The meaning then is that every dependent does not exist without its object of dependence while being described as dependence upon it. The necessary consequence of the proof is that tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa while being described as dependence upon mutabaqa, but the intended meaning is that they do not exist without it absolutely. Some virtuous scholars answered that the qualification by 'insofar as' has two considerations: one is that it is a qualification of the event, so then it conveys qualified dependence. The second is that it is a qualification of the attribution of the event to the agent, so it then amounts to the conditional or the universal customary, as if it were said: 'Every dependent as long as it is dependent does not exist without its object of dependence.' So the minor premise is perpetual with one of the two universals, and it yields a perpetual conclusion as is mentioned in modal logic. Thus it results: Tadamman and iltizam do not exist without their object of dependence perpetually; that is what is sought. Al-Razi in his commentary on al-Matali' criticized the proof with a general criticism saying: If the explanation were valid, then mutabaqa would necessitate tadamman and iltizam, because it is the object of dependence, and the object of dependence insofar as it is an object of dependence does not exist without the dependent. What al-Sayyid said in his gloss on al-Shamsiyya, after presenting some discussions on the aforementioned proof, is excellent: 'The best way to show that they necessitate mutabaqa is to say: They necessitate the subject of signification (wad') which necessitates mutabaqa, so they necessitate it necessarily.' Because what necessitates that which necessitates a thing necessitates that thing.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) analyzing a syllogism that proves the relationship between the three types of signification (al-dalalat al-thalath). The commentator is expanding on a statement in the matn that says: 'Because they (tadamman and iltizam) are dependent (tabi'an) on mutabaqa.' He first clarifies that this statement is a categorical syllogism of the first figure. The syllogism is: (1) Tadamman and iltizam are dependent on mutabaqa. (2) The dependent, insofar as it is dependent, does not exist without its object of dependence. Conclusion: Tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa. The commentator notes that the qualification 'insofar as it is dependent' is attached to the predicate (mahmul) of the major premise, not to the subject (mawdu'), to avoid the logical error of a non-identical middle term (middle term must be the same in both premises). If it were attached to the subject, the middle term would not be identical because the subject of the minor premise (tadamman/iltizam) is not qualified that way. The major premise is self-evident: a dependent qua dependent cannot exist without its object of dependence. The reason for this qualification is that a dependent can exist without its object of dependence in some other context, but then it is not considered a dependent in that context. For example, heat is dependent on fire (as its cause), but heat can be found from the sun without being dependent on fire. So the qualification ensures that we refer to the thing specifically as a dependent. Thus, the qualification belongs to the predicate ('does not exist without its object of dependence while being described as dependent'), not to the subject. If it were a qualification of the subject, it would change the middle term. The correct understanding is: 'Every dependent (while being described as dependent) does not exist without its object of dependence.' The conclusion then is that tadamman and iltizam do not exist without mutabaqa while they are described as dependent. But the intended conclusion is absolute non-existence. To resolve this, some scholars propose that the qualification has two interpretations: (a) as a qualifier of the event (hadath), meaning 'dependent existence as such' – this yields a restricted conclusion; (b) as a qualifier of the attribution of the event to the agent, transforming it into a conditional or universal-customary modality: 'Every dependent, as long as it is dependent, does not exist without its object.' This, with a perpetual minor premise, yields a perpetual conclusion through modal logic. Thus, the desired absolute conclusion is obtained. Al-Razi then objects: If the explanation were sound, then mutabaqa (as the object of dependence) would also necessitate tadamman and iltizam, because the object of dependence, insofar as it is an object of dependence, does not exist without the dependent. This would imply mutual dependence, which is absurd. Al-Sayyid provides a better approach: Instead of focusing on the direct relationship, we should note that tadamman and iltizam necessitate the act of signification (wad'), which itself necessitates mutabaqa. Therefore, by transitivity, they necessitate mutabaqa. This avoids the reciprocity problem. The commentator endorses al-Sayyid's solution.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between al-Sayyid's alternative proof and the original syllogism in establishing that tadamman and iltizam require mutabaqa?
A) Al-Sayyid uses a second-figure syllogism instead of first-figure.
B) Al-Sayyid proves it through transitivity via wad' rather than directly via the property of dependence.
C) Al-Sayyid denies that tadamman and iltizam require mutabaqa.
D) Al-Sayyid uses a disjunctive syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Logical proofs in usul must be carefully structured to avoid unintended symmetry, and transitivity often provides a safer route.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Suppose someone argues: 'Since the implied meaning (iltizam) of a text requires the primary meaning (mutabaqa), and the primary meaning requires the implied meaning, they are interdependent.' Using al-Sayyid's method, show that this argument commits the fallacy of assuming symmetry. Prove instead that iltizam requires mutabaqa through the requirement of wad'.
?
Construct a transitive argument using a different example from usul al-fiqh, such as: 'The specific ('amm) necessitates the general (khass) for its application; the general necessitates the specific for its understanding; therefore...' (or something similar).

The Syllogism

Dafil al-Khalf: Structure of Proof by Contradiction
Definition and conditional structure of dafil al-khalf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in a dafil al-khalf argument and explain how it proves a claim by invalidating its opposite.

Core Idea
Proof by contradiction is a conditional syllogism: if the opposite were true, an impossible consequence follows.

This lesson introduces the foundational logical tool known as dafil al-khalf (proof by contradiction). The author classifies it as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and shows how it works: to prove a claim, you assume its opposite and derive an impossibility, thereby establishing the original claim. Understanding this structure is essential for many arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proven
In a dafil al-khalf argument, there is a target proposition (the claim you want to establish). Find this in the text—it is the opposite of what the proof invalidates.
2
Find the antecedent (the assumed opposite)
The proof begins by assuming the negation of the claim. This is the 'if' part of the conditional, often signaled by 'wa illa' (and if not).
3
Identify the consequent (the impossible result)
From the assumption, a logical consequence is derived that is impossible or contradictory. The author calls this 'muhal'.
4
Recognize the conditional form and modus tollens
The argument has the structure: If A (the opposite), then B (impossible). But B is false (impossible). Therefore, A is false, and its negation (the original claim) is true. This is modus tollens.
This is the core inferential move of dafil al-khalf.
5
Verify that the proof is conditional, not categorical
The passage explicitly states that dafil al-khalf is 'min qabil al-istithna'i'—from the conditional type. Contrast this with categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مبادئ التصورات
وهذا يقال له: دليل الخلف، وهو إثبات الشيء بإبطال نقيضه، وهو أكثر أدلة هذا الفن، وهو من قبيل الاستثنائي، فقوله: (وإلا) إشارة للمقدم.
وإلا؛ لصدق عين أحد المتساويين على بعض نقيض الآخر، وهو محال؛ لأنه صدق أحد المتساويين بدون الآخر.
وقوله: الصدق ... إلخ) إشارة للثالي.
وقوله: (لصدق عين أحد المتساويين) أي: وهو ناطق.
وقوله: (على بعض النقيض ... إلخ) أي: وهو لا إنسان، وقوله: (لأنه صدق أحد المتساويين) أي: وهو ناطق، وقوله: (بدون (الآخر) أي: وهو إنسان. وقوله: (وهو محال) إشارة للاستثنائية المبطلة للتالي، فيبطل المقدم، فيثبت نقيضه.
(قوله: وإلا لصدق عين ... إلخ) أي: وإلا يصدق كل واحد من نقيض المتساويين على كل ما يصدق عليه النقيض الآخر، بأن لم يصدق واحد منهما على شيء مما يصدق عليه النقيض الآخر أصلاً، أو صدق كل منهما على بعض النقيض الآخر؛ لصدق ... إلخ؛ أي: للزم صدق عين أحد المتساويين على بعض ما يصدق عليه النقيض الآخر؛ أي: يلزم أن يكون أحد المتساويين أعم من الآخر؛ لانفراده عنه بصدقه على بعض نقيض ذلك الآخر.
(قوله: لأنه صدق) أي: وجد.
العطار: الشيء والممكن العام، فإن الشيء والممكن العام لما وجب صدقهما على كل مفهوم بحسب نفس الأمر؛ امتنع صدق اللاشيء واللاممكن بحسبها على مفهوم من المفهومات، فإذا قلت: لو لم يصدق كل لا شيء لا ممكن؛ لصدق بعض اللاشيء ليس بلا ممكن، فيكون بعض اللاشيء ممكناً؛ اتجه المنع المذكور، وأجيب بتخصيص الدعوى بغير نقائض المفهومات الشاملة، فإن نقائض غيرها يصدق لا محالة على شيء ما؛ ويتم البرهان. لا يقال: يلزم تخصيص القواعد العقلية؛ لأنا نقول: تعميمها إنما هو بحسب المقاصد، وليس لنا زيادة غرض في معرفة أحوال نقائض الأمور العامة؛ إذ ليس في العلوم الحكمية قضية موضوعها أو محمولها نقيض الأمور الشاملة، وهذا الفن آلة لتلك العلوم، فلا بأس بإخراجها عن قواعده.
Section: On the Principles of Conceptualizations. This is called the proof by contradiction – it is establishing a thing by invalidating its opposite, and it is the most frequent proof in this art, and it is of the conditional type. Thus his statement: 'and if not' indicates the antecedent. 'And if not; then the very one of the two co-extensive terms would be true of some part of the opposite of the other, and that is impossible; because it would be the truth of one of the two co-extensives without the other.' And his statement: 'the truth... etc.' indicates the consequent. His statement: 'for the truth of the very one of the two co-extensives' means the term 'natiq'. His statement: 'of some part of the opposite' means 'la insan'. His statement: 'because it is the truth of one of the two co-extensives' means 'natiq', and his statement: 'without the other' means 'insan'. His statement: 'and that is impossible' indicates the conditional that invalidates the consequent, so the antecedent becomes invalid, and its opposite is established. 'And if not, then the very one... etc.' means: if it is not the case that each one of the two opposites of the co-extensives is true of everything of which the other opposite is true – either because neither of them is true of anything of which the other opposite is true at all, or because each of them is true of some part of the opposite of the other – then the truth of the very one of the two co-extensives would be necessary for some part of what the opposite of the other is true of; i.e., one of the two co-extensives would be more general than the other, because it is unique in being true of some part of the opposite of that other. His statement: 'because it is true' means 'exists'. al-Attar: 'Thing and possible in general – since it is necessary for both of them to be true of every concept according to the matter itself, it is impossible for 'non-thing' and 'non-possible' to be true of any concept. So if you say: If 'every non-thing is not non-possible' were not true, then 'some non-thing is not non-possible' would be true, and thus some non-thing would be possible; the aforementioned objection applies. And it is answered by restricting the claim to other than the opposites of universal concepts, for the opposites of other than them are necessarily true of something, and the proof is completed. It cannot be said that this restricts rational principles; for we say: their generalization is only according to the purposes, and we have no further interest in knowing the states of the opposites of universal matters, since in the theoretical sciences there is no proposition whose subject or predicate is the opposite of universal matters, and this art is a tool for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding them from its rules.'
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical proof known as 'dafil al-khalf' (proof by contradiction). The author begins by stating that it is a form of conditional syllogism (istithna'i) and explains its structure: it establishes a claim by invalidating its opposite. The wording 'and if not' (wa illa) signals the antecedent of the conditional. The core argument is then illustrated with co-extensive terms (mutasawiyan), such as 'natiq' (rational) and 'insan' (human). The proof shows that if the co-extensives were not equivalent in extension, then one would be true of a part of the opposite of the other, which is impossible because it would mean one holds without the other. The commentary then unpacks each phrase: 'the very one' refers to 'natiq', 'some part of the opposite' refers to 'la insan' (non-human), etc. The annotator clarifies that the impossibility is used to invalidate the consequent, which then forces the negation of the antecedent, thereby proving the original claim. The passage then addresses a subtle objection raised by al-Attar concerning universal concepts like 'thing' (shay') and 'possible in general' (mumkin 'am). These concepts are so broad that their negations ('non-thing', 'non-possible') apply to nothing. If one tried to apply the same proof to such concepts, the argument would fail because the necessary premise (that the negation applies to something) would be false. The answer is that the proof is only intended for concepts whose opposites do apply to something; universal concepts are excluded because they are not used in the theoretical sciences that this logical art serves. Thus, the scope of dafil al-khalf is properly restricted.

According to the passage, what type of syllogism does dafil al-khalf belong to?
A) Categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani)
B) Conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i)
C) Disjunctive syllogism (qiyas munfasil)
D) Immediate inference ('aks)

Key Takeaway
Dafil al-khalf is a conditional syllogism that proves a claim by showing its negation leads to an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a dafil al-khalf argument for the claim: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Assume its opposite ('Some intoxicant is not forbidden') and derive an impossibility using the premise that intoxicants cause intoxication and share the same effective cause ('illa).
?
Identify the antecedent and consequent in the following argument from kalam: 'If the world had no creator, then it would not be contingent. But the world is contingent. Therefore it must have a creator.' Is this a valid dafil al-khalf? Explain.

Co-extensives and the Scope of Dafil al-Khalf
Counterexample and limitation of dafil al-khalf for universal concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why dafil al-khalf fails for universal concepts like 'thing' and 'possible' and how the tradition restricts its application.

Core Idea
Dafil al-Khalf requires that the negation of the opposite be non-empty; universal concepts whose negations are empty are excluded.

The previous lesson introduced the basic structure of dafil al-khalf. However, the author immediately raises a critical nuance: the proof does not work for extremely broad concepts like 'thing' (shay') and 'possible' (mumkin). Their negations ('non-thing', 'non-possible') apply to nothing, so the conditional fails. This lesson examines the co-extensive example and the resolution proposed by al-Attar, showing how the tradition carefully delimits the tool's domain.

Guided Path
1
Understand the co-extensive example
The author uses two co-extensive terms: 'natiq' (rational) and 'insan' (human). They are equivalent in extension. The proof shows that if their negations were not equally co-extensive, one would have to be true of a part of the other's negation, which is impossible because co-extensives cannot be separated.
This example illustrates how the impossibility is derived in a concrete case.
2
Identify the universal concepts problem
Al-Attar points out that 'thing' and 'possible in general' are universal: everything is a thing and everything is possible. Their negations 'non-thing' and 'non-possible' are true of nothing. If you try to apply dafil al-khalf to these, the conditional premise (that the negation applies to something) is false, so the proof collapses.
The issue is that the consequent of the conditional would be false, but not in a useful way—the impossibility is not derived from the assumption but from the nature of the terms.
3
Examine the resolution
The response is to restrict dafil al-khalf to cases where the opposite of the opposite (i.e., the negation of the assumed opposite) has actual instances. Universal concepts whose negations are empty are excluded because the theoretical sciences do not deal with such concepts as subjects or predicates.
The restriction is pragmatic: logic is a tool for the sciences, and the sciences do not require such cases.
4
Apply the restriction to a test case
Take the claim 'Every thing is created.' The opposite is 'Some thing is not created.' The negation of this opposite is 'No thing is not created' i.e., 'Everything is created.' This is not empty—it has instances. So dafil al-khalf can be applied. But for 'Every non-thing is impossible,' the opposite 'Some non-thing is not impossible' would require 'non-thing' to be something, which it is not. So the proof fails.
This shows the boundary of the tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في مبادئ التصورات
