Section: On the Principles of Conceptualizations. This is called the proof by contradiction – it is establishing a thing by invalidating its opposite, and it is the most frequent proof in this art, and it is of the conditional type. Thus his statement: 'and if not' indicates the antecedent. 'And if not; then the very one of the two co-extensive terms would be true of some part of the opposite of the other, and that is impossible; because it would be the truth of one of the two co-extensives without the other.' And his statement: 'the truth... etc.' indicates the consequent. His statement: 'for the truth of the very one of the two co-extensives' means the term 'natiq'. His statement: 'of some part of the opposite' means 'la insan'. His statement: 'because it is the truth of one of the two co-extensives' means 'natiq', and his statement: 'without the other' means 'insan'. His statement: 'and that is impossible' indicates the conditional that invalidates the consequent, so the antecedent becomes invalid, and its opposite is established. 'And if not, then the very one... etc.' means: if it is not the case that each one of the two opposites of the co-extensives is true of everything of which the other opposite is true – either because neither of them is true of anything of which the other opposite is true at all, or because each of them is true of some part of the opposite of the other – then the truth of the very one of the two co-extensives would be necessary for some part of what the opposite of the other is true of; i.e., one of the two co-extensives would be more general than the other, because it is unique in being true of some part of the opposite of that other. His statement: 'because it is true' means 'exists'. al-Attar: 'Thing and possible in general – since it is necessary for both of them to be true of every concept according to the matter itself, it is impossible for 'non-thing' and 'non-possible' to be true of any concept. So if you say: If 'every non-thing is not non-possible' were not true, then 'some non-thing is not non-possible' would be true, and thus some non-thing would be possible; the aforementioned objection applies. And it is answered by restricting the claim to other than the opposites of universal concepts, for the opposites of other than them are necessarily true of something, and the proof is completed. It cannot be said that this restricts rational principles; for we say: their generalization is only according to the purposes, and we have no further interest in knowing the states of the opposites of universal matters, since in the theoretical sciences there is no proposition whose subject or predicate is the opposite of universal matters, and this art is a tool for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding them from its rules.'
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical proof known as 'dafil al-khalf' (proof by contradiction). The author begins by stating that it is a form of conditional syllogism (istithna'i) and explains its structure: it establishes a claim by invalidating its opposite. The wording 'and if not' (wa illa) signals the antecedent of the conditional. The core argument is then illustrated with co-extensive terms (mutasawiyan), such as 'natiq' (rational) and 'insan' (human). The proof shows that if the co-extensives were not equivalent in extension, then one would be true of a part of the opposite of the other, which is impossible because it would mean one holds without the other. The commentary then unpacks each phrase: 'the very one' refers to 'natiq', 'some part of the opposite' refers to 'la insan' (non-human), etc. The annotator clarifies that the impossibility is used to invalidate the consequent, which then forces the negation of the antecedent, thereby proving the original claim. The passage then addresses a subtle objection raised by al-Attar concerning universal concepts like 'thing' (shay') and 'possible in general' (mumkin 'am). These concepts are so broad that their negations ('non-thing', 'non-possible') apply to nothing. If one tried to apply the same proof to such concepts, the argument would fail because the necessary premise (that the negation applies to something) would be false. The answer is that the proof is only intended for concepts whose opposites do apply to something; universal concepts are excluded because they are not used in the theoretical sciences that this logical art serves. Thus, the scope of dafil al-khalf is properly restricted.

الشيء
الممكن العام
اللاشيء
اللاممكن

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Syllogism on Possibility and Its Refutation
Author presents a syllogism on possibility leading to contradiction with multiple responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of equivocation in a categorical syllogism and analyze the dialectical responses used by Islamic logicians to resolve it.

Core Idea
Ambiguity in the middle term can turn a valid syllogism into a contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents a syllogism that seems to produce a contradiction. The logicians then dissect it, showing how the middle term shifts in meaning between premises. This lesson will walk you through the syllogism and the various refutations, teaching you to spot equivocation and appreciate the rigor of Islamic logical debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Read the first part: the syllogism uses two premises about 'possible with specific possibility' and 'possible with general possibility'. Extract them: (1) Everything not possible with specific possibility is either necessary or impossible, and each of those is possible with general possibility. (2) Everything not possible with general possibility is not possible with specific possibility, and everything not possible with specific possibility is possible with general possibility. The conclusion: Everything not possible with general possibility is possible with general possibility.
This conclusion is a contradiction because it says something is both not possible and possible in the same sense.
2
Spot the contradiction
The conclusion 'Everything not possible with general possibility is possible with general possibility' directly asserts A and not-A of the same thing, which is logically impossible (contradiction). This indicates the syllogism is invalid despite the premises seeming plausible.
This contradiction is called ijtima' al-naqidayn (coincidence of contradictories).
3
Examine the first response: Sahib al-Qistas
Sahib al-Qistas (author of al-Qistas) argues that 'not possible with specific possibility' includes a third category: 'necessary of both extremes' (daruri al-tarafayn), which is neither necessary nor impossible nor possible. He claims it is not included in the tripartite division, so the syllogism's major premise (that it is either necessary or impossible) is false.
This response introduces a controversial fourth modality to avoid the contradiction.
4
Analyze al-Sayyid's objection
Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani objects that the 'necessary of both extremes' is not a real division; it vanishes with reflection. He argues that general possibility actually includes all concepts, so the supposed fourth category is an illusion. This refutes the defense by showing the major premise is not false.
Al-Sayyid's objection targets the ontological status of the fourth modality.
5
Understand Mir Zahid's point on the middle term
Mir Zahid argues that the syllogism is invalid because the middle term (what is not possible with specific possibility) is used in the minor premise in a 'prima facie' sense and in the major premise in an 'investigative' sense. Thus, the middle term is not identical, causing equivocation (istirak al-hadd al-awsat).
This is a classic fallacy of equivocation. The middle term must be univocal for the syllogism to be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالإمكان الخاص، وعندنا قضيَّةٌ صادقة وهي قولنا : كُلُّ مَا ليسَ بِممكن بِالإمكانِ الخاص ؛ فهو إما واجب أو مُمتنع ، وكُلُّ واحدٍ منهما ممكن بالإمكان العام؛ فنقول : كُلُّ مَا ليسَ بِممُكن بالإمكان العام ؛ فهو ليس بممكن بالإمكان الخاص، وكُلُّ ما ليس يممكن بالإمكان الخاص؛ فهو مُمكن بالإمكان العام، ينتجُ : كُلّ مَا ليسَ بِممكن بِالإمكان العام؛ فهو مُمكن بالإمكان العام، وهو اجتماع النقيضين، وأجاب صاحب القِسطاس بأنَّ مَا ليسَ بِممكن خاص يتناولُ ضروري الطَّرفين، وهو ليس مندرجاً في الواجب والممتنع ، ولا في الممكن العام؛ إذ لا يتحقَّقُ بدونِ سَلْبِ الضَّرورةِ، قال : فإِنْ قُلْتَ : مَا طَرفَاهُ ضروريَّانِ يكون مُمتنعاً ، وكُلُّ مُمتنع ممكن بِالإمكان العام؛ قُلْتُ : ليسَ كُلَّ مُمتنع ممكناً بالإمكان العام، بل الممتنع الذي هو ضَروري العدم فقط، واعترضَهُ السَّيِّدُ في حاشية شرح المطالع بأنَّ هذا القسم؛ أعني : ضروري الطَّرفَيْنِ، وإنْ كانَ محتملاً في بادئ الرأي ؛ لكنَّهُ في التَّحقيقِ مِمَّا لَا يعدُّهُ العقلُ قِسماً رابعاً للأقسامِ الثَّلاثةِ المشهورةِ ، وتَخَيَّلُ القسم الرابع يَضمحِلُّ بأدنى التفاتٍ، فَالممكِنُ العام شامل لجميع المفهومات . .ا.هـ. وأجاب مير زاهد بأنَّ مقصود صاحب القِسطاس أنَّ الحدَّ الأوسط لم يتكرر في القياس ؛ فإنَّه في الصُّغرى أعم بحسبِ المفهوم العُنوانيّ مِمَّا هو في الكُبرى، فَفِي الصُّغرى ما هو في بادئ الرأي، وفي الكُبرى ما هو عند التَّحقيق، وأجابَ شارح المطالع بأنَّهُ إِنْ أرادَ بقولِهِ : كُلُّ مَا ليسَ يممكن بالإمكان الخاص ؛ فهو إما واجب أو مُمتنع؛ موجبة سالبة الموضوع؛ فلا نَسلَّمُ صدقها ، وإن أراد به موجبةٌ مَعدولة الموضوع ; فمسلَّم، لكنَّ الإنتاج ممنوع، فإِنَّ القضيَّةَ اللازمة سالبة الطَّرفين، فلا يتَّحدُ الوسط ا.هـ. وأجيب بغير ذلك .
With the specific possibility, and we have a true proposition which is our statement: Everything that is not possible with specific possibility, then it is either necessary or impossible, and each of the two is possible with general possibility; so we say: Everything that is not possible with general possibility, then it is not possible with specific possibility, and everything that is not possible with specific possibility, then it is possible with general possibility. The conclusion is: Everything that is not possible with general possibility is possible with general possibility, which is a contradiction. And the author of al-Qistas answered that 'what is not possible with specific possibility' includes the necessary of both extremes, and it is not included in the necessary and the impossible, nor in the general possible, because it cannot be realized without the negation of necessity. He said: If you say: 'That whose two extremes are necessary is impossible, and every impossible is possible with general possibility', I say: Not every impossible is possible with general possibility, but only the impossible that is necessary non-existence only. And al-Sayyid objected to him in his gloss on Sharh al-Mutala' that this division—I mean the necessary of both extremes—although it may be plausible at first glance, in reality the intellect does not consider it a fourth division alongside the three well-known divisions, and the imagined fourth division vanishes with the least reflection, so the general possible includes all concepts. ... And Mir Zahid answered that the intention of the author of al-Qistas is that the middle term is not repeated in the syllogism; for in the minor premise it is more general in terms of the conceptual sense than what is in the major premise. So in the minor premise it is what appears at first glance, and in the major premise what is upon investigation. And the commentator on al-Mutala' answered that if he intended by his statement 'Everything that is not possible with specific possibility, then it is either necessary or impossible' as a universal negative with a negative subject, then we do not concede its truth; and if he intended it as a universal affirmative with a privative subject, then it is conceded, but the production is prevented, for the necessary proposition is negative in both terms, so the middle does not coincide. ... And it was answered otherwise.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of a syllogism concerning the concepts of specific possibility (الإمكان الخاص) and general possibility (الإمكان العام). The syllogism attempts to show that something not possible in the general sense becomes possible in the general sense, leading to a contradiction (اجتماع النقيضين). The author of al-Qistas (صاحب القسطاس) defends the syllogism by distinguishing a third category: that which is necessary in both extremes (ضروري الطرفين), which falls outside the standard tripartite division of necessity, impossibility, and possibility. He argues that not everything impossible is possible in the general sense; only that which is necessarily non-existent (ضروري العدم فقط). However, al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani in his gloss on Sharh al-Mutala' rejects this addition, claiming the intellect does not recognize a fourth division and that general possibility encompasses all concepts. Mir Zahid then offers another defense: the middle term in the minor premise is conceptually broader than in the major premise, so the syllogism is invalid due to equivocation. The commentator on Sharh al-Mutala' (possibly al-Taftazani) further analyzes the logical form, arguing that the major premise either has a negative subject (making it false) or a privative subject (making it true but the syllogism invalid due to the negative terms). The multiple responses illustrate the rigorous dialectical method in Islamic logic, where even a seemingly problematic syllogism is dissected with precision. Throughout, the Islamic example of possibility and necessity is central, tying to kalam discussions of the contingency of the world.

ضروري الطرفين

The Existence of the Universal Natural: Argument, Objection, and Response
Author presents a consensus-based argument for existence of universal natural and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument for the existence of the universal natural, identify the objection regarding unity of existence, and explain the response based on the distinction between real accidents and intentional abstract existence.

Core Idea
The universal natural exists through an intentional abstract existence, not subject to the laws of real accidents.

This passage presents a classic dialectical exchange in Islamic logic. The author first argues from consensus that the universal natural must exist externally. Then an objection is raised: if it exists, it would require one existence for two things or invalidate predication. The response, drawing on the distinction between real accidents and abstract considerations, defends the possibility of one abstract existence. Let's walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial consensus argument
Locate the premise: if individuation is not the essence itself, it requires a cause. Then examine the two alternatives: cause is itself (leads to species in one individual – false) or not itself (then cause is materials/accidents – leads to external existence of essence).
This is a disjunctive syllogism eliminating the impossible horn.
2
Understand the objection as a dilemma
Find the objection: if universal natural exists, it either shares the individual's existence (one existence in two things – impossible for accidents) or has a different existence (predication invalid). Both horns seem impossible, challenging the existence claim.
The objection is a destructive dilemma.
3
Analyze the response's key distinction
The response chooses the first horn (shared existence) and argues that the impossibility of one thing subsisting in two applies only to real accidents, not to intentional abstract considerations. Existence is abstract, so the objection fails.
This is the critical refinement: distinguishing real accidents from intentional abstractions.
4
Apply the distinction to clarify the conclusion
Confirm that the universal natural does exist externally in individuals through an abstract existence. The supporting quote from Hikma al-Ayn illustrates that absolute animal only enters existence when restricted.
This shows how the universal is always individuated in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ قال مؤيداً لِمَا استدلَّ بِهِ على وجودِ الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي : ( ويشهد له) أي : لوجودِ الكُلِّي الطَّبيعي (مَا اتَّفقوا عليهِ مِنْ أَنَّ الماهيَّةَ إذا لم يكنْ تَشخُصُهَا نفسها لَا بُدَّ لهُ) أي : لِلتَشخُص ، وهوَ التَّعيُّنُ الخارجي (مِنْ عِلَّةٍ) وتلكَ العِلَّةُ (إِمَّا نَفْسُهَا فَينحصرُ) على هذا التَّقديرِ (نَوْعُهَا في فردٍ) ضرورة وحدة المعلول وحدة العلَّة، لكن انحصار نوع الماهية في فردٍ واحدٍ باطل بالمشاهدة (أوْ) لا تكونُ به،

فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات
الدسوقي
التى تتحقَّقُ تتحقق في الأفراد على القولِ الأَوَّلِ هي الماهيَّةُ لا بشرط شيء، أما الماهية بشرط لا شيء، فهو الكُلِّي مِن حيثُ كُلِّيَّته، وهذا لا يحتوي عليه الفرد والماهية بشرط شيء جنس الأفراد
العطار
العلَّةُ نَفْسُهَا (فَ) يُعَلَّلُ التَّشخُصُ (بموادّها ) أي : ذاتيّاتِها (أو أعراض تكشفُ لها) وهو الواقع، وحينئذ ؛ يلزمُ وجود الماهية خارجاً فإنَّ الاحتياج في الاتّصافِ بالتَّشخُصِ إلى العلَّةِ يقتضي أن يكون الانصافُ بِهِ خارجيا هو أي: الاتصافُ الخارجي (يقتضي وجود الموصوفِ في الخارج مَّ أَشارَ لِدفع ما أورده أيضاً على القول بوجودِ الكُلّيّ الطَّبيعيّ خَارجاً بِقَوْلِهِ : وَلَا غبار على هذا المطلب إلا ما قالُوا مِنْ أَنَّهُ لَوْ كانَ مَوجوداً ; فإمَّا بوجودِ الفردِ فَيلزمُ قيام وجودٍ واحدٍ بأمرَيْنِ) إذ الفرضُ أنَّ الوجودَ لِلفردِ والماهية في ضمنِهِ، فَهُمَا مَوجودانِ بوجودِ واحد وفي حاشية مير زاجان على شرح حكمة العينِ ; أنَّ ذلك الوجود الواحدَ إِنْ قامَ بِكُلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الماهيَّتَيْنِ ; كانَ في قوَّةِ قيام العرض الواحدِ بِمَحلَّيْنِ، وإنْ قامَ بِالمجموع; لزمَ وجودُ الكُلِّ بِدونِ أجزائِهِ، وإنْ قامَ بأحدِهِمَا ; لم يكن الموجود إلا ذلك الواحد .. هـ. (أو بوجودِ مُغاير لهُ فَلَا يصح الحملُ ; لأنَّ الحمل يقتضي الاتحاد في الوجودِ (و) يرد أيضاً أنَّ كُلَّ مَوجودٍ في الخارج فَهُوَ مُتَشخّصُ بِالبديهة، وهذا) أيْ: مَا ذكرَ مِنَ الأمور الموردة هو الذي قادَهُم وجَرَّهُم (إلى الحُكْمِ بِامتناع وُجودِهِ) أي : الكُلِّيِّ الطَّبيعي، وقدْ أُجيب عَنِ الأوَّلِ) وَهُوَ قَولُهُ: لَوْ كَانَ مَوجوداً ; فإما بوجود الفرد أو بوجودِ مُغَايرٍ لهُ بِمَا لا يحتملُ المقام إيراده) وحاصلُ ما أُجيب به اختيارُ الشَّقِّ الأوَّلِ، وتسليم لزومِ قيام الوجودِ الواحد بأمرين، فإنَّ قيامَ الشَّيء الواحدِ بِأمرين; إنَّما ثبتَتْ مُحاليَّتُهُ في العرض الموجود; لا الأمور الاعتبارية الانتزاعيَّةِ والوجود منها، والأدلة التي أوردها على الامتناع إنَّما تَمَّتْ في بطلانِ قيام الأعراض الموجودة، ويؤيِّدُ مَا قُلْنَا قول صاحبِ حكمة العين: الحيوان المطلق لا يدخل في الوجودِ إلَّا بعدَ تَقييدِهِ بقيدٍ ; فإنَّهُ مَا لم يصر ناطقاً أو صَهَّالاً أو غيرهما
Then he said, supporting what he argued for the existence of the universal natural: (and witnesses for it) i.e., for the existence of the universal natural (what they have agreed upon: that the essence, if its individuation is not itself, must have for it) i.e., for individuation, which is external determination (a cause). And that cause (either is itself, then on this assumption its type is confined to a single individual) due to the necessity of the unity of the effect with the unity of the cause, but the confinement of the type of essence to one individual is false by observation. (Or) it is not itself. Section: on the principles of concepts. al-Dasuqi: That which is realized in individuals on the first opinion is the essence without any condition. As for the essence with the condition of nothing, it is the universal in terms of its universality, and this is not contained in the individual. And the essence with the condition of something is the genus of individuals. al-Atar: The cause itself (then) individuation is explained (by its materials) i.e., its essentials (or accidents that accrue to it), and that is the reality. And then, the existence of the essence externally is necessary because the need for the description of individuation to a cause necessitates that the description be external, i.e., the external description (necessitates the existence of the described in the external). Then he indicated to repel what was also objected against the statement of the existence of the universal natural externally by his statement: (and there is no dust on this demand except what they said: if it existed, then either with the existence of the individual, then the subsistence of one existence in two things would follow) since the assumption is that existence is for the individual and the essence within it, so they are two existents with one existence. And in the hashia of Mir Zajan on the commentary of Hikma al-Ayn: that that one existence if it subsists in each of the two essences, it would be in the power of the subsistence of one accident in two loci; and if it subsists in the composite, then the existence of the whole without its parts would follow; and if it subsists in one of them, then the existent would be nothing but that one. (Or with a different existence, then predication would not be valid because predication requires unity in existence). (And) it is also objected that every existent in the external is necessarily individuated, and this) i.e., what was mentioned of the objections, it is what drove them (to the judgment of the impossibility of its existence) i.e., the universal natural. (And it has been answered concerning the first) which is his statement: if it existed, then either with the existence of the individual or with a different existence, with something that the context cannot accommodate. And the summary of what was answered is the choice of the first alternative, and the admission of the necessity of one existence subsisting in two things, for the subsistence of one thing in two things has been proven impossible only in the existent accident, not in intentional and abstract matters, and existence is among them. And the proofs he brought for impossibility only hold for the falsity of the subsistence of existent accidents. And what we have said is supported by the statement of the author of Hikma al-Ayn: the absolute animal does not enter existence except after being restricted by a condition; for it does not become a speaker or a whinnyer or other than that.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange presenting an argument for the existence of the universal natural, an objection, and a response. The argument begins with a consensus: if the essence's individuation is not identical to itself, then individuation requires a cause. If that cause were the essence itself, then the species would be limited to one individual (since cause and effect are one), which is false by observation. Therefore, the cause must be something else, implying that the essence exists externally distinct from its individuating factors. The text then inserts a section heading 'Section: on the principles of concepts' with a brief remark by al-Dasuqi about the three conditions of the essence (la bi sharṭ shay', bi sharṭ la shay', bi sharṭ shay'). The commentary by al-Atar continues: the cause of individuation is either the essence's essentials or accidents, which leads to the necessity of the existence of the essence externally. Then an objection is raised: if the universal natural existed, it would either exist with the same existence as the individual (leading to one existence subsisting in two things, which is impossible for accidents) or with a different existence (making predication invalid). The response, briefly summarized, chooses the first alternative and admits one existence for two things, but argues that impossibility of one thing subsisting in two applies only to real accidents, not to intellectual considerations like existence. The lesson concludes with a supporting quote from the author of Hikma al-Ayn: the absolute animal only enters existence when restricted by a condition.

Key Terms
التشخُّص
الماهية لا بشرط شيء
الماهية بشرط لا شيء
الماهية بشرط شيء
الوجود الاعتباري الانتزاعي

A) Because the universal natural is a real entity existing separately from individuals.
B) Because existence is an intentional abstract consideration, not a real accident.
C) Because the objection only applies to essential attributes.
D) Because the universal natural has no existence at all.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the universal natural in individuals is an intentional abstract consideration, not a real accident, so it is not subject to the impossibility of one accident in two loci.

The Symbolic Convention: Using Letters for Subject and Predicate
Author explains why logicians use ج and ب for subject and predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the use of the letters ج and ب in logical propositions and explain why logicians use them instead of specific terms.

Core Idea
Letters in logic signify form, not content, making arguments universally applicable.

In this short but foundational lesson, we explore why Islamic logicians adopted the convention of using the letters ج (jim) for the subject and ب (ba) for the predicate. This simple notational device serves two crucial purposes: it abbreviates expressions and, more importantly, it emphasizes that the logical form is independent of any particular subject matter. Understanding this convention is essential for reading and constructing abstract logical arguments in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the convention
Note that the author states it is their custom to express the subject by ج and the predicate by ب.
2
Reason 1: Brevity
The first reason is for brevity in expression. Using single letters shortens the proposition and makes arguments easier to handle.
3
Reason 2: Universal applicability
The second reason is to prevent the misconception that the logical judgments are tied to a specific matter. By using abstract letters, the logician shows that the rules apply to any content.
4
Apply the convention
When you see a proposition like 'كل ج ب' (All J is B), recognize that ج can stand for any subject and ب for any predicate, and the logical form remains valid regardless of what content you plug in.
This is the essence of formal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ جَرَتْ عَادَتُهُمْ أَنْ يُعَبِّرُوا عَنِ الْمَوْضُوعِ بِـ (ج)، وَعَنِ الْمَحْمُولِ بِـ (ب)، إِمَّا لِلاخْتِصَارِ فِي الْعِبَارَةِ، وَإِمَّا لِدَفْعِ تَوَهُمِ أَنَّ الْأَحْكَامَ الْمُقَدَّرَةَ قَاصِرَةٌ عَلَى مَادَّةٍ.
And know that their custom is to express the subject by (J) and the predicate by (B), either for brevity in expression, or to repel the illusion that the hypothetical judgments are restricted to a particular matter.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage explains the convention in Islamic logic of using the letters ج (jim) and ب (ba) to represent the subject and predicate respectively in logical propositions. The author gives two reasons: first, for brevity in writing and speaking; second, to avoid the misunderstanding that the logical rules only apply to specific subject matters. By using abstract letters, the logician shows that the form of the argument applies universally, not just to examples like 'every human is animal'.

C) To follow the tradition of the Greeks.
D) To make the proposition easier to memorize.

Key Takeaway
The letters ج and ب in Islamic logic are not arbitrary; they serve to abstract the logical form from specific content, enabling universal application of syllogistic rules.

The Syllogism

Al-Sharnubi's Conditional Syllogism for Possibility Conditions
Al-Sharnubi presents a conditional syllogism proving that non-necessity of negation is required for possibility of affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) that proves the conditions for general possibility and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Possibility requires neither necessity of affirmation nor necessity of negation.

In this passage, al-Sharnubi explains how a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) is used to establish the two key conditions for general possibility. By showing that the opposite leads to contradiction, he demonstrates that for a proposition to be possible, its negation cannot be necessary, and its affirmation cannot be necessary. This proof is foundational for understanding modal logic in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Al-Sharnubi aims to prove two conditions: (1) non-necessity of negation is required for the possibility of affirmation, and (2) non-necessity of affirmation is required for the possibility of negation.
These are the two halves of the proof.
2
Extract the conditional premise
For the first part, the conditional is: 'If the non-necessity of negation were not true, then its opposite (necessity of negation) would be true.' This is an if-then statement linking the negation of the desired condition to its contradictory.
The conditional is implicit; al-Sharnubi writes: 'لو لم يصدق عدم ضرورة السلب لصدق نقيضه'.
3
Identify the exception (istithna')
The syllogism proceeds by denying the consequent: 'But the consequent is false' (لكن التالي باطل). This is the istithna' step.
In conditional syllogisms, the second premise either affirms the antecedent (modus ponens) or denies the consequent (modus tollens). Here it denies the consequent.
4
Verify the implication (mulazama)
Al-Sharnubi states that the implication (ملازمة) is obvious (ظاهرة). The implication is valid because if non-necessity of negation is false, then negation must be necessary, by logical necessity.
This step ensures the conditional premise is sound.
5
Confirm the falsity of the consequent
Why is the consequent false? Because necessity of negation would entail the impossibility of affirmation, contradicting the assumption that affirmation is possible (الفرض أنه ممكن).
This relies on the definition of possibility: a proposition is possible if its negation is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) (قَوْلُ الشَّارح : لو لم يكن ... إلخ) قياس استثنائي حذف منه الاستثنائية، والمقصود به إثبات المطلوب، وهو عدم ضرورة السلب في إمكان الإيجاب وعدم ضرورة الإيجاب في مكان السلب بإبطال نقيضه وتركيبه في (الأول) هكذا لو لم يصدق عدم ضرورة السلب لصدق نقيضه وهو ضرورة السلب لكن التالي باطل، فبطل المقدّم وهو نفي عدم ضرورة السلب فيثبت نقيضه وهو عدم ضرورة السلب وهو المطلوب، أما الملازمة فظاهرة، وأما بطلان التالي فلأن ضرورة السلب تقتضي استحالة الإيجاب والفرض أنه ممكن. (وفي الثَّاني) هكذا لو لم يصدق عدم ضرورة الإيجاب لصدق نقيضه وهو ضرورة الإيجاب، لكن التالي باطل فبطل المقدم وهو نفي عدم ضرورة الإيجاب فثبت نقيضه وهو عدم ضرورة الإيجاب وهو المطلوب، أما الملازمة فظاهرة، وأما بطلان التالي فلأن ضرورة الإيجاب تقتضي استحالة السلب والفرض أنَّه ممكن. واعلم أن النسبة الممكنة العامة بین والموجهات السابقة العموم المطلق وهي أعمها فتجتمع في كل إنسان حيوان إما بالضرورة بأقسامها الأربعة أو بالدوام بقسميه أو بالفعل أو بالإمكان، وتنفرد الممكنة العامة في كل إنسان يمشي على أربع بالإمكان العام فقط . ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
(1) (The commentator's saying: if it were not...) is a conditional syllogism from which the hypothetical [premise] is omitted. Its purpose is to prove the required, which is the non-necessity of negation in the possibility of affirmation, and the non-necessity of affirmation in the place of negation, by refuting its opposite. Its composition (in the first) is: if the non-necessity of negation were not true, then its opposite, which is the necessity of negation, would be true. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent, which is the negation of the non-necessity of negation, is false, so its opposite, which is the non-necessity of negation, is established, and that is the required. As for the implication, it is obvious. As for the falsity of the consequent, because the necessity of negation entails the impossibility of affirmation, and the presumption is that it is possible. (And in the second) similarly: if the non-necessity of affirmation were not true, then its opposite, which is the necessity of affirmation, would be true. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent, which is the negation of the non-necessity of affirmation, is false, so its opposite, which is the non-necessity of affirmation, is established, and that is the required. As for the implication, it is obvious. As for the falsity of the consequent, because the necessity of affirmation entails the impossibility of negation, and the presumption is that it is possible. And know that the general possible relation among the preceding modal propositions has absolute generality, and it is the most general. So it is found in every man [who is] an animal, either with necessity in its four divisions, or with perpetuity in its two divisions, or with actuality, or with possibility. And the general possible is unique to every man who walks on four [legs] with only general possibility. End. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This footnote explains the logical structure of a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) that was presented in the main commentary. The syllogism proves two required results: first, that non-necessity of negation is required for the possibility of affirmation; second, that non-necessity of affirmation is required for the possibility of negation. The proof proceeds by reductio ad absurdum: assume the opposite, i.e., that the negation is necessary, then affirmation would be impossible, contradicting the assumption that it is possible. Therefore, the negation cannot be necessary, so non-necessity of negation is true. The same reasoning applies to the other side. The footnote then adds a meta-commentary by al-Sharnubi: the general possible proposition is the most general among modal propositions, encompassing all others. For example, the proposition 'Every man is an animal' can be true in various modalities (necessary, perpetual, actual, possible). However, the general possible proposition stands alone in cases like 'Every man walks on four legs', which is only possibly true in the general sense (i.e., not necessarily false).

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
الملازمة
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A categorical syllogism in the first figure
B) A conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i)
C) An inductive argument
D) An analogical argument

Key Takeaway
The possibility of a proposition requires that its negation is not necessary and its affirmation is not necessary, proven by conditional syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Types of Exclusive Disjunction and Intrinsic Opposition
Author defines three types of exclusive disjunction and classifies them based on intrinsic opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of exclusive disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya, māniʿat al-jamʿ, māniʿat al-khuluw) and determine whether a given disjunction is oppositional (ʿinādiyya) based on the source of incompatibility.

Core Idea
An exclusive disjunction's truth conditions depend on whether it prevents conjunction, emptiness, or both.

In this passage, the author introduces the three primary types of exclusive disjunction (munfaṣila) and then further classifies them based on whether the incompatibility is intrinsic (ʿinādiyya) or accidental. This classification is essential for constructing sound conditional syllogisms in Islamic logic. We will walk through the definitions and apply them to familiar examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
The passage lists three types: al-ḥaqīqiyya (true exclusive), māniʿat al-jamʿ (preventer of conjunction), and māniʿat al-khuluw (preventer of emptiness). Write down their names and note that they are all types of exclusive disjunction (munfaṣila).
Remember that ḥaqīqiyya is the strongest form; the others are weaker.
2
Understand the truth conditions
For each type, determine when it is false. Ḥaqīqiyya: false when both parts are true or both false. Māniʿat al-jamʿ: false only when both parts are true. Māniʿat al-khuluw: false only when both parts are false. This is the key differentiating factor.
You can think of these as 'not both', 'not neither', and 'exactly one'.
3
Grasp the oppositional condition
The passage states that each type is oppositional (ʿinādiyya) if the incompatibility is due to the essences of the parts (li-dhāt al-juzʾayn). This means the two parts are mutually exclusive by their very nature, not by external factors. Example: even and odd are intrinsically opposed.
Al-Dasuqi's commentary explains that if both parts were true, a thing would equal its negation; if both false, both would be removed.
4
Apply to Islamic science examples
Consider the kalam statement: 'Either the world is temporally originated or it is eternal.' This is a ḥaqīqiyya and oppositional because originated and eternal are intrinsic opposites. For māniʿat al-jamʿ: 'Either Zaid is in the house or in the mosque' (cannot be both, but could be in neither). For māniʿat al-khuluw: 'Either the action is permissible or obligatory' (cannot be neither, but could be both? Actually in fiqh an action cannot be both, but logically it allows both true). Use these to test your understanding.
The distinction between oppositional and accidental affects how the disjunction behaves in conditional syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي أَقْسَامِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ
فالمنفصلة ثلاثة أقسام : حقيقية، ومانعة الجمع، ومانعة الخلو
(وَكُلَّ مِنْهُمَا ) ؛ أي: من أقسام المنفصلة (عِنَادِيَّةٌ ، إِنْ كَانَ التَّنَا فِي) بينَ الجزأين (لِذَاتِ الجُزْأَيْنِ)، كالتَّنافي بين الزوج والفردِ، والشَّجرِ والحَجَرِ،
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : لِذَاتِ الْجُرْأَيْن) أي : لكونهما بحيث إذا أُوحِظَا؛ كان بينَهُمَا مَا يقتضي التَّنافي وعدم الاجتماع في الصدق أو الكذب أو فيهما .
(قَوْلُهُ : كَالتَّنَا فِي بَيْنَ الزَّوْجِ وَالْفَرْدِ أي : في مثالِ المنفصلة الحقيقية، فإنَّهما إذا لُوحِظَا ؛ وُجِدَ بينَهُمَا مَا يَقتضي التَّنافي وعدم الاجتماع في الصدق والكذب؛ لأنَّ كُلا منهما مساءٍ لِنقيض الآخَرِ، فَلَوْ صدقا ؛ لزم اجتماعُ الشَّيء والمساوي لنقيضه، وَلَو كذبا ؛ لزم ارتفاعُهُمَا، وهو مُحالٌ.
Chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. The exclusive disjunction has three divisions: the true exclusive, the preventer of conjunction, and the preventer of emptiness. And each of them (i.e., the divisions of the exclusive) is an oppositional exclusive if the incompatibility between the two parts is due to the essence of the two parts, like the incompatibility between even and odd, and tree and stone. Al-Dasuqi: His statement 'due to the essence of the two parts' means that they are such that when considered, there is between them what necessitates incompatibility and non-coexistence in truth or falsehood or both. His statement 'like the incompatibility between even and odd' is in the example of the true exclusive, for when they are considered, there is found between them what necessitates incompatibility and non-coexistence in truth and falsehood because each of them is equal to the negation of the other. So if both were true, then the coexistence of a thing and that which is equal to its negation would be necessary; and if both were false, then their removal would be necessary, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of exclusive disjunction (al-munfasila) and then classifies them as either 'oppositional' (ʿinādiyya) or 'accidental' (ittifāqiyya) based on the source of incompatibility. The author begins by stating that the exclusive conditional (sharṭiyya munfaṣila) has three divisions: the true exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya), which is false when both parts are true or both false; the preventer of conjunction (māniʿat al-jamʿ), which is false only when both are true; and the preventer of emptiness (māniʿat al-khuluw), which is false only when both are false. Then the author notes that each of these three is further divided into 'oppositional' (ʿinādiyya) and 'accidental' (ittifāqiyya). The oppositional type occurs when the incompatibility between the two parts is due to their essences (li-dhāt al-juzʾayn), meaning the two parts are mutually exclusive by their very natures. Al-Dasuqi's commentary explains this condition: when the two parts are considered, there is an inherent incompatibility that prevents them from being both true, both false, or both in one of these ways, depending on the type. The author gives examples: even and odd (zawj wa fard) and tree and stone (shajar wa ḥajar). Al-Dasuqi elaborates on the example of even and odd as an instance of the true exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya) that is also oppositional (ʿinādiyya). He explains that each of these is equal to the negation of the other (i.e., even = not odd, odd = not even). Therefore, if both were true, a thing would be equal to its negation, which is impossible; if both were false, both would be removed, which is also impossible. Thus the incompatibility is intrinsic and covers both truth and falsehood.

Key Takeaway
An exclusive disjunction can be true exclusive (ḥaqīqiyya), preventer of conjunction (māniʿat al-jamʿ), or preventer of emptiness (māniʿat al-khuluw), and each may be oppositional if the incompatibility is inherent.

Classification of Compound Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates six types of muttasila and three of munfasila with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six mixed types of connected conditionals and three mixed types of disjunctive conditionals, and explain the reason for the difference in count.

Core Idea
In conditionals, order matters for muttasila but not for munfasila, yielding six vs. three mixed types.

Building on the three homogeneous conditional types, the author now turns to mixed types where the two parts differ. He explains that the natural order of antecedent (malzum) and consequent (lazim) in connected conditionals creates distinct types when reversed, resulting in six types. For disjunctive conditionals, no such order exists, so only three are listed. However, a later commentator argues that reversal also applies there.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three homogeneous types
Before tackling mixed types, remember that there are three homogeneous conditionals: both parts muttasila, both munfasila, and both hamliyya. The mixed types combine different natures.
2
Understand natural order in muttasila
In a connected conditional (if A then B), the antecedent (A) is the malzum (necessary condition) and the consequent (B) is the lazim (necessary consequence). This order is fixed by nature: reversing A and B generally gives a different meaning.
The author says 'the necessary condition is determined to be antecedent, and the consequence consequent.'
3
List the three basic mixed combinations
The three mixed types for muttasila are: (1) categorical antecedent + connected consequent, (2) categorical antecedent + disjunctive consequent, (3) connected antecedent + disjunctive consequent. Their converses yield three more, giving six total.
Examples are provided: 'If the rising of the sun is a necessary condition for day, then whenever the sun rises, day exists.' (type 1)
4
Recognize why munfasila has only three
In a disjunctive conditional (either A or B), there is no natural order between the disjuncts. Both are equally possible, so reversing the parts yields the same proposition. Therefore, only three mixed types are enumerated, not six.
The author says the disjunctive 'does not divide into those two [sections] due to lack of distinction.'
5
Note the commentator's correction
The super-commentator (likely al-Dasuqi) objects: 'Rather six, as we explained.' He argues that reversal does apply to munfasila when the parts are of different types, doubling the count to six. Reflect on this refinement.
This shows how later scholars revisited and expanded the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة تنقسم في المتصلة إلى قسمين؛ لما ثبت أن امتياز قسمين؛ المقدم فيها عن التالي بحسب الطبع، فالملزوم فيها متعين بأن يكون مقدما، واللازم تاليا، والاستلزام من الجانبين غير ضروري، والفرق بين مقدمها حملية، وتاليها متصلة ومنفصلة، ومقدمها وتاليها بالعكس، ظاهر، بخلاف المنفصلة، فلا تنقسم فيها إليهما؛ لعدم الامتياز على الوجه المذكور، فالمتصلة من المختلفتين ستة أقسام.
فصل في أقسام الشرطية: ثم طرفا القضية الشرطية، وإن كانا قبل التركيب قضيتين تامتين إلا أنهما خرجنا بزيادة أداة الاتصال.
الأول: من حملية ومتصلة نحو: إن كان طلوع الشمس ملزوما لوجود النهار؛ فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود.
الثاني: عكسه.
الثالث: من حملية ومنفصلة نحو: إن كان هذا عددا؛ فهو إما زوج وإما فرد.
الرابع: عكسه.
الخامس: من متصلة ومنفصلة نحو: إن كان كلما كانت الشمس غاربة فالليل موجود، فإما أن تكون الشمس غاربة وإما ألا يكون الليل موجودا.
السادس: عكسه.
والمنفصلة منهما ثلاثة: الأول: من حملية ومتصلة نحو: إما ألا يكون طلوع الشمس ملازما لوجود النهار، وإما أن يكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا.
الثاني: من حملية ومنفصلة نحو: إما أن يكون العدد واحدا، وإما أن يكون إما زوجا أو فردا.
الثالث: من متصلة ومنفصلة نحو: إما أن يكون إذا كان العدد فردا فهو لا زوج، وإما أن يكون العدد إما زوجا وإما فردا.
فأقسام المتصلات تسعة، وأقسام المنفصلات ثلاثة.
(قوله: ثلاثة) بل ستة كما بيناه.
Then these three types divide in the connected [conditional] into two [sections]; because it is established that the distinction of two aspects: the antecedent from the consequent according to nature, so the necessary condition is determined to be antecedent, and the consequence consequent, and implication from both sides is not necessary, and the difference between its antecedent being categorical, and its consequent being connected and disjunctive, and its antecedent and consequent being the opposite, is clear, contrary to the disjunctive [conditional], so it does not divide into those two [sections] due to lack of distinction in the aforementioned manner. So the connected [conditional] from the differing [types] has six divisions.
Section on the divisions of the conditional: Then the two extremes of the conditional proposition, even though before composition they were complete propositions, they are only removed [from their original form] by the addition of the particle of connection.
The first: composed of a categorical and a connected, e.g., 'If the rising of the sun is a necessary condition for the existence of day; then whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.'
The second: its converse.
The third: composed of a categorical and a disjunctive, e.g., 'If this is a number; then it is either even or odd.'
The fourth: its converse.
The fifth: composed of a connected and a disjunctive, e.g., 'If whenever the sun is setting, the night exists; then either the sun is setting or the night does not exist.'
The sixth: its converse.
And the disjunctive from them are three: the first: composed of a categorical and a connected, e.g., 'Either the rising of the sun is not a necessary condition for the existence of day, or that whenever the sun is rising, the day exists.'
The second: composed of a categorical and a disjunctive, e.g., 'Either the number is one, or it is either even or odd.'
The third: composed of a connected and a disjunctive, e.g., 'Either if the number is odd then it is not even, or the number is either even or odd.'
So the divisions of connected [conditionals] are nine, and the divisions of disjunctive [conditionals] are three.
(His statement: 'three') Rather six, as we explained.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage classifies compound conditional propositions (shartiyya murakkaba) after the earlier classification into three homogeneous types (both parts muttasila, both munfasila, both hamliyya). The author now turns to mixed types where the two parts are of different natures. He first explains that in muttasila (connected conditional), the natural order of antecedent and consequent is fixed—the necessary condition (malzum) is logically prior—so reversing the order produces distinct types. Therefore, for muttasila there are six mixed types: three original (hamliyya+muttasila, hamliyya+munfasila, muttasila+munfasila) and their converses. For munfasila (disjunctive), however, there is no such natural order, so only three mixed types are listed (the same combinations without reversal). The author then provides examples for each: first type: compound with a categorical antecedent and a connected consequent (e.g., 'If sun's rising necessitates day, then whenever sun rises day exists'). Second type is the converse (connected antecedent, categorical consequent). Similarly for the other pairs. The disjunctive mixed types use 'either...or' structures; the first example: 'Either sun's rising does not necessitate day, or it does (i.e., whenever sun rises day exists).' The commentary notes that the total number of muttasila types is nine (three homogeneous + six mixed) and munfasila three (three homogeneous + three mixed). However, a marginal note from the super-commentator (perhaps al-Dasuqi) corrects that the disjunctive mixed should be six, not three, because the reversal also applies if the parts are of different types, contrary to the author's claim. This passage is crucial for understanding the full taxonomy of conditionals in Aristotelian logic as adapted in the Islamic tradition, specifically in the context of arguments like qiyas istithna'i.

Key Takeaway

Objection to the Disjunctive Contradictory and Its Resolution
Objection to the disjunctive contradictory rule and its resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which the contradictory of a compound disjunction is a simple proposition, and explain why logicians maintain the disjunctive form as the standard contradictory.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound may be simple in specific cases, but the disjunctive form ensures uniform logical operation.

In the study of compound propositions, a key rule states that the contradictory of a disjunctive compound is itself a disjunction composed of the contradictories of its parts. However, some scholars objected that this rule fails when the truth values of the parts are not both true or both false. This lesson examines that objection and the resolution provided by the commentator, clarifying when the contradictory may be simple and why the disjunctive form is still preferred.

Guided Path
1
Restate the standard rule
Recall that for a disjunctive compound (e.g., 'Either A or B' with the condition that at least one is true), its contradictory is a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts ('Either not-A or not-B') that excludes emptiness (they cannot both be false).
2
Identify the objection
Some scholars noted that if one part of the original compound is true and the other false, the contradictory according to the rule would be true (since one of the contradictories is true), but the original is also true, violating the definition that a contradictory must have opposite truth values.
This suggests the contradictory might be a simple proposition, not a compound.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentator responds that the contradictory is 'either this or that' – meaning it can be either of the two simple contradictories, depending on which part is false. When one part is true and the other false, the contradictory is the simple contradictory of the false part.
When both parts are true or both false, the contradictory can be a compound (the disjunction of both contradictories).
4
Evaluate the implication
While the contradictory may be simple in specific truth-value cases, logicians prefer the disjunctive form because it works uniformly for all truth-value combinations, ensuring a single contradictory regardless of the truth values of the parts.
The disjunction excluding emptiness is the standard choice for its generality.

Source Text

التقادير
Some virtuous people said: 'There is an investigation in this; because...' (Footnote: 'His statement 'some virtuous people' refers to Mīr Abū al-Fatḥ in his commentary on the matn, transmitted from him by Rajab Efendi in his gloss on this book. End.) So it is said: 'Either this contradictory, or that one.' [Dassūqī explains: This means that the contradictory is one of them consistently, and this does not negate that the contradictory may be both of them. This occurs when both of the two propositions contained in the compound disjunction are true or both false. But if one is true and the other false, then the contradictory is one of them. So reflect on it. (Footnote: 'Reflect on it' means he commands reflection because he transmitted their expression and there is something in the soul about it. For the compound is either true by the truth of both its parts—then the disjunctive concept is false in both its sides; or false by the falsity of both its parts—then the disjunctive concept is true in both its sides. As for the truth of one side of the compound and the falsehood of the other, that which corresponds to the true part of the concept is false and vice versa, so it is far from the truth, as is not hidden from one who practices compounds. And the apparent is that it is merely a possibility and supposition, evidenced by the fact that they chose for the contradictory of the compound the disjunction excluding emptiness over its two siblings, so that it would be a contradictory on all suppositions.) And his statement 'either this contradictory or that one' is like your saying in the contradictory of the specific conditioned compound: if it is universal, its two parts are two simple universal propositions, and their contradictories are two simple particular propositions. So their contradictory is the disjunctive concept between these two particular propositions. And what is understood from the disjunctive concept between them is either a disjunction excluding emptiness composed of them, or a categorical proposition, or a proposition with a distributed predicate between them. So the contradictory of the positive non-permanent universal affirmative, for example our saying 'Every writer is actually a human not permanently' is our saying 'Either some human is not permanently (a writer) or some human is permanently a writer.' And compare the rest. So specifying the disjunctive concept as a disjunction is not good; reflect. (His statement 'either this contradictory' has 'this' as a predicate fronted, and 'the contradictory' as a subject delayed, and 'or that' is coordinated with the predicate, meaning 'or that contradictory.' Fronting the predicate over the subject is for emphasis. (Footnote: 'Reflect on it' – he commands reflection because he transmitted their expression and there is unease about it; for the compound is either true by the truth of both parts, then the disjunctive concept is false in both its sides; or false by the falsity of both parts, then the disjunctive concept is true in both its sides. As for the truth of one side and falsehood of the other, what corresponds to the true part of the concept is false and vice versa, so it is far away, as is clear to one who practices with compounds. The apparent is that this is merely a possibility and assumption, evidenced by their choice of the disjunction excluding emptiness for the contradictory of the compound over its two siblings, so that it is a contradictory in all scenarios.)
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from some virtuous scholars (identified in the footnote as Mīr Abū al-Fatḥ) against the previous definition that the contradictory of a compound is always a disjunction excluding emptiness. The objection suggests that there is room for investigation because in some cases the contradictory might be a simple proposition rather than a disjunction. The commentator responds by stating 'Either this contradictory, or that one,' meaning that the contradictory can be either of the two contradictory parts depending on the truth values. Dassūqī explains that the contradictory is consistently one of them, but it is not contradictory for it to be both when both parts of the original compound are true or both false. However, if one part is true and the other false, then the contradictory is specifically one of them—the one that corresponds to the false part. The passage then provides an example: for a universal affirmative irregular ('positive non-permanent universal'), like 'Every writer is actually a human not permanently,' the contradictory is 'Either some human is not permanently (a writer) or some human is permanently a writer.' The commentator notes that the disjunctive concept derived from the contradictories of the parts can be understood as either a disjunction excluding emptiness, a categorical proposition, or a proposition with a distributed predicate, and it is not necessarily restricted to a disjunction. The final gloss adds a reflection: the rule of always using the disjunction excluding emptiness is still preferred because it works uniformly for all truth-value scenarios, while the alternative (using a simple proposition when one part is true and the other false) relies on a specific case that may not always apply, so the disjunction is the safe choice.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

10 min
Modal Syllogisms in the First Figure: Proof by Contradiction and Critical Refinements
Dasuqi and 'Attar comment on a modal syllogism leading to a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of a modal particular affirmative proposition using a first-figure categorical syllogism and identify the necessary modal refinements as noted by Dasuqi and 'Attar.

Core Idea
Modal precision is essential for valid syllogistic proofs; omission of modality can invalidate a logical argument.

This lesson examines a subtle modal syllogism in the first figure, where the truth of a temporal particular affirmative is proved by contradiction. The commentators Dasuqi and 'Attar refine the original formulation by explicitly adding the necessary modalities—such as 'always'—to ensure logical rigor. You will learn to follow the reductio argument and appreciate how even a single omitted modal qualifier can undermine a proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall proof structure
The proof aims to show that the statement 'some B is C when it is B' must be true. It assumes the contrary (that 'nothing of B is C as long as it is B' is true) and combines it with 'all C is B' to derive a contradiction ('nothing of C is C'), which is impossible. Therefore, the original statement is true.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assuming the negation leads to an absurd conclusion.
2
Identify the first-figure categorical syllogism
The syllogism used in the proof is: Major premise: 'Nothing of B is C as long as it is B' (universal negative), Minor premise: 'All C is B' (universal affirmative), Conclusion: 'Nothing of C is C' (universal negative). This is a first-figure syllogism (middle term B, major term C, minor term C). The mood is Celarent (EIO) but with added modalities.
First-figure validity requires an affirmative minor and a universal major. Here both conditions are met.
3
Analyze Dasuqi's critical refinements
Dasuqi points out that the original text omitted the modality 'always' from the contradictory statement and the conclusion. He insists that the contradictory should be 'nothing of B is C always as long as it is B', and the conclusion should be 'nothing of C is C always' (or with 'as long as C'). Without these qualifiers, the proof may not be fully rigorous.
Dasuqi also clarifies that the syllogism's minor must be affirmative and major universal, which the proof respects.
4
Examine 'Attar's contribution
'Attar explains that the necessity or perpetuity of the predicate for the subject's essence ensures that the absolute temporal proposition (hiniyya mutlaqa) holds when the subject is described. This grounds the modal inference in essential attributes.
This connects the logical form to metaphysical foundations, common in Islamic logical tradition.
5
Synthesize: Why modal precision matters
The omitted modalities, if left unspecified, could allow for different interpretations that might invalidate the syllogism. By explicitly adding 'always' and 'as long as', the proof becomes airtight. The lesson highlights that in modal logic, every qualifier carries weight.
This attention to detail is characteristic of the Islamic commentary tradition.

(His saying: 'Otherwise; nothing... etc.') That is: Otherwise, the truth of some (B C)... etc. is necessary because its contradictory is true, which is a universal negative conventional general saying: nothing of (B)... etc., and the commentator omitted its modality, which is always, so he should have said: nothing of (B C) always as long as (B). (His saying: 'to the original which is all (C) (B).' That is: according to (His saying: 'it must be true that some B (C) because the necessary or perpetual predicate of the essence of the subject is initially according to the description of the subject; inevitably it is established at the time of the subject's description being for the essence, so the absolute temporal is true.
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentators Dasuqi and 'Attar on a previous logician's formulation of a modal syllogism in the first figure. The argument proceeds as follows: If the statement 'some B is C when it is B' is false, then its contradictory 'nothing of B is C as long as it is B' is true. Combining this with the premise 'All C is B' (with some modality) yields the conclusion 'Nothing of C is C' (with necessity, perpetuity, or as long as C). This conclusion is impossible because it denies a thing of itself. Therefore, the original statement must be true. Dasuqi points out that the modality ('always') was omitted by the commentator and should be specified. He also clarifies that the syllogism is in the first figure, requiring an affirmative minor and universal major. 'Attar explains that the predicate's necessity or perpetuity for the subject's essence ensures the temporal truth when the subject is described, thus the absolute temporal (hiniyya mutlaqa) follows. This is a classic proof of a modal conversion using reductio ad absurdum.

Key Takeaway
Modal omissions in syllogisms can lead to invalid reasoning; careful specification of temporal and modal qualifiers is essential for rigorous logical proofs.

Why Universal Affirmative Necessity Cannot Convert
Demonstrates via syllogism that converting a universal affirmative necessity leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two-syllogism proof that a universal affirmative necessity proposition cannot convert to a universal affirmative of the same modality, and explain why the contradiction is valid via subalternation.

Core Idea
Converting a universal affirmative necessity leads to contradiction, proving conversion impossible.

This passage presents a rigorous syllogistic proof that a universal affirmative proposition with the modality of necessity cannot be simply converted into a universal affirmative of the same modality. The proof constructs two syllogisms that yield contradictory conclusions, forcing us to reject the possibility of such conversion. Understanding this argument sharpens your ability to test the validity of immediate inferences in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three propositions
Locate the original proposition (every J is B as long as it is J), its assumed converse (every B is J as long as it is J), and the two parts of the original: the universal affirmative necessity (part 1) and the universal negative absolute (part 2).
The minor premise used in both syllogisms is the assumed converse.
2
Construct the first syllogism
Take the first part of the original (every J is B as long as it is J) as the major premise, and the assumed converse (every B is J as long as it is J) as the minor premise. Work out the conclusion: every B is B always.
This conclusion is a tautology but is necessary for the proof.
3
Construct the second syllogism
Take the second part of the original (nothing of J is B in the absolute general sense) as the major premise, and the same assumed converse as the minor premise. The conclusion is: nothing of B is B in the absolute sense.
The term 'in the absolute sense' means without any temporal restriction.
4
Analyze the contradiction
The first conclusion (every B is B always) and the second conclusion (no B is B absolutely) are contradictory. However, the direct contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, not a universal negative. The commentator resolves this by noting that the universal negative entails the particular negative via subalternation, so a genuine contradiction still arises.
This refinement shows the proof's logical rigor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And you combine it as a minor premise with the first part of the original, which is our statement: 'necessarily or always every J is B as long as it is J', it produces: 'every B is B always'. Then you combine it as a minor premise with the second part of the original, which is our statement: 'nothing of J is B in the absolute general sense', it produces: 'nothing of B is B in the absolute sense', so the two contradictories converge. (His statement: 'minor premise') meaning: in the state of being a minor premise. And his statement: 'from the original', and it is necessarily: 'every B is J as long as it is J' not always. (His statement: 'and it is') meaning: the first part is our statement, and his statement: 'produces: every B is B'; that is, necessarily 'every B is B'. And his statement: 'then you combine it'; meaning: the universal affirmative absolute permanent which is the contradictory of the second part of the conversion. (His statement: 'then you combine it') meaning: the mentioned proposition which is the contradictory of the second part of the conversion. (His statement: 'so the convergence of the two contradictories follows') meaning: because 'nothing of B is B' entails 'not some B is B' in the absolute sense, and it contradicts 'every B is B' always. (His statement: 'the two contradictories') intended: the two mutual opposites, so the result of the first syllogism is a universal affirmative, and the second is a universal negative. So 'the two contradictories' mean: the first result which obtained from combining the contradictory of the second part of the conversion with the first part of the original, and the second result which obtained from combining that contradictory with the second part of the original. But if it is said: the two results are contradictory because the negative of a universal affirmative is the particular negative, not the universal negative, but here its contradictory is made a universal negative; then it may be said: the universal negative entails the particular negative because it is more specific. (Note: Al-Sharnubi's marginal comment: 'His statement: that is, necessarily...' etc., sees a problem with combining necessity and permanence.)
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that a universal affirmative proposition with the modality of necessity cannot be converted into a universal affirmative of the same modality. The argument proceeds through two syllogisms. First, the original proposition 'every J is B as long as it is J' is taken as a major premise; then a minor premise 'every B is J as long as it is J' (which is the converse of the original?) is combined to yield 'every B is B'. But the text says 'you combine it as a minor premise with the first part of the original, which is our statement: necessarily or always every J is B...'. Actually, it seems they are constructing a syllogism to test conversion. The first syllogism yields 'every B is B always' which is a tautology and not a genuine conversion. Then, they combine the same minor premise with the second part of the original (a universal negative absolute) to get 'nothing of B is B in the absolute sense', contradicting the first result. This shows that the assumed converse leads to contradiction, proving that the original universal affirmative with necessity cannot convert to a universal affirmative. The commentator then explains that the two results are not direct contradictories because a universal affirmative is contradicted by a particular negative, but the universal negative (the second result) entails the particular negative due to subalternation, so a contradiction still follows. This refines the proof.

Proving Simple Conversion via Assumption and Syllogism
Dussuqi elaborates on the proof of simple conversion by assumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a third-figure categorical syllogism using the method of assumption to prove the simple conversion of a particular negative proposition with temporal qualification.

Core Idea
Simple conversion is proved by assuming a concrete instance and building a third-figure syllogism.

Simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) is a fundamental immediate inference, but its validity is not always obvious, especially for particular negatives with temporal conditions. In this passage, Dussuqi shows how to prove such a conversion by the method of assumption (al-iftirad): we pick a concrete individual (Zayd) and construct two third-figure syllogisms. This lesson will walk you through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Start with the given proposition: 'Some writer is not finger-still as long as he is writing, not always.' Note the temporal qualification: the negative holds only during writing, not absolutely. This is a particular negative (O-type) with a conditional scope.
The proposition is 'Some A is not B as long as it is A, not always.'
2
Assume a concrete individual for 'some'
Pick a specific person, here Zayd, to represent the 'some writer' who is not finger-still while writing. This is the method of assumption (iftirad). We treat Zayd as an instance satisfying both descriptions: he is a writer, and at that time he is not finger-still (the original negates finger-stillness).
Zayd is not arbitrary; he must satisfy the subject description and the negative condition.
3
Form the first two premises
From the assumption we derive: (1) Zayd is a writer (subject description). (2) Zayd is finger-still? Wait—the original says 'not finger-still', so how do we get 'Zayd is finger-still'? Actually, Dussuqi uses a different route: he first gets 'Zayd is a writer' and then 'Zayd is (something else)'. Read carefully: he says 'we also attribute to it the description of the predicate, so we obtain a second premise: Zayd is finger-still.' But that seems to contradict the original. The trick is that the predicate description is not the negation; the original negation is part of the copula. So the predicate 'finger-still' is attributed as a universal description? Actually, Dussuqi is constructing a syllogism that uses a third premise to get the conversion. The key point: you form two premises about Zayd: one from the subject, one from the predicate, then add a third premise relating them under the temporal condition.
The second premise is 'Zayd is finger-still' (affirmative). This seems to assume that Zayd is finger-still at some other time? The text is ambiguous; for our pedagogical purpose, follow the logical structure.
4
Introduce the third premise and form the first syllogism
The third premise is a conditional: 'Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still' (salba muqayyada). Combine with 'Zayd is finger-still' to form a third-figure syllogism: minor: Zayd is finger-still; major: Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still. Middle term Zayd (subject in both). This yields the conclusion: 'Some finger-still is not a writer as long as he is finger-still.' That is the first part of the converse (O-type with temporal qualification).
The third figure is used because the middle term is the subject in both premises. The conclusion is a particular negative (O).
5
Form the second syllogism by swapping premise order
Now reverse the two premises: 'Zayd is finger-still' as major, and 'Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still' as minor. This again yields a third-figure syllogism with the same middle term, producing the same conclusion. This shows the converse is symmetrical.
The repetition may be for completeness or to emphasize the logical necessity.

Source Text

فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي وحَمْلُ وصفي الموضوع والمحمول عليه؛ ليحصل مفهوم العكس. وَلْتَذْكُرْ لهذا البحث زيادةَ تحقيق في عكس النَّقيض. الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : وَصْفَي الْمَوْضُوْعِ وَالْمَحْمُوْلِ) أي: مفهومهما . Chapter: On simple conversion. And the attribution of the two descriptions of the subject and predicate to it, so that the concept of conversion obtains. And you should mention for this research an additional verification on conversion of the contradictory. al-Dussuqi: His saying: 'the two descriptions of the subject and predicate' means their concepts. His saying: 'and you should mention for this research' means: at the author's saying: 'and he explained the conversion of the two particulars', and its clarification: our statement in the prior original, which is: Some writer is not finger-still as long as he is writing, not always; we posit that 'some' as a specific thing like Zayd, and we attribute to it the description of the subject which is 'writer', so we say: Zayd is a writer. The proof of this proposition is the truth of the description of the subject about its individuals, and we also attribute to it the description of the predicate, so we obtain a second premise: Zayd is finger-still. Then we bring a third premise whose truth is established by invalidating the consequent of its contradictory; then we combine it with the second premise from the two premises of assumption saying: Zayd is finger-still, making this the minor premise and that the major, we obtain a syllogism in the third figure, and it reverts to the first by conversion of its minor premise as follows: Some finger-still is Zayd, and Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still, yields: Some finger-still is not a writer as long as he is finger-still. This result is exactly the first part of the converse. Then you take the two premises of assumption, and you put the second first and make it the minor, you obtain a syllogism in the third figure also as follows: Zayd is finger-still, Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still, yields: Some finger-still is not a writer as long as he is finger-still.
Source pages p. 731

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by Dussuqi on the chapter of simple conversion. The main text (matn) states the chapter heading and then says that by attributing the two descriptions (subject and predicate) to the subject, the concept of conversion is obtained. Dussuqi first clarifies that 'two descriptions' means the concepts of subject and predicate. He then explains the author's instruction to add verification on conversion of the contradictory. He provides a detailed example. The original proposition is: 'Some writer is not finger-still as long as he is a writer, not always.' We want to prove its converse. The method assumes a specific individual, Zayd, who is that 'some writer'. We first form the premise 'Zayd is a writer' (true by assumption). Then we form 'Zayd is finger-still' (true because the original proposition says some writer is not finger-still, implying others are? Actually, careful: the original states 'some writer is not finger-still', so the assumed individual Zayd must be one of those who are not finger-still? Wait, the example says 'we posit that some as a specific thing like Zayd, and attribute to it the description of the subject which is writer, so we say Zayd is a writer. The proof of this proposition is the truth of the description of the subject about its individuals, and we also attribute to it the description of the predicate, so we obtain a second premise: Zayd is finger-still.' This seems contradictory: if Zayd is a writer and not finger-still (as per original), then how is Zayd finger-still? Actually, the original is 'some writer is not finger-still as long as he is a writer, not always'. That means there are some writers who are not finger-still while they are writing, but that may not be always. The assumption picks one such writer (Zayd) who is a writer and not finger-still at that time. But then the second premise says 'Zayd is finger-still'? That would be a different time? The text is complex. Dussuqi then introduces a third premise 'Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still' (salba muqayyada). The two premises 'Zayd is finger-still' and 'Zayd is not a writer as long as he is finger-still' form a syllogism in the third figure: middle term Zayd is subject in both. The conclusion is 'Some finger-still is not a writer as long as he is finger-still'. That is the first part of the converse. Then he repeats with swapped premises to get a similar conclusion. The example seems to be demonstrating conversion of a particular negative with temporal qualification. The commentary walks through the construction of the syllogism.
