The Linguistic Definition and Centrality of Qiyas
Author defines qiyas linguistically and argues for its centrality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qiyas is considered the most important method for achieving certain knowledge in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Qiyas is the only path to certainty in Islamic logic.

This passage marks a significant turn in the logic text: from the principles of propositions to their ultimate purpose—the syllogism (qiyas). The author gives a simple linguistic definition of qiyas as 'estimating one example by another' and then explains why qiyas takes center stage. The commentators expand on this, showing that while there are three paths to affirmative knowledge—qiyas, induction, and analogy—only qiyas yields the certainty that the sciences require. Understanding this hierarchy is key to appreciating the role of logic in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition of qiyas
Find the Arabic phrase where the author defines qiyas linguistically. Notice it is very simple: 'the estimation of an example upon another example'. This is a broad definition that later will be refined.
The technical definition is deferred, so we only have the linguistic sense so far.
2
Distinguish the three paths to affirmation
The passage states that the path to affirmation (al-tasdiq) is divided into three: qiyas, istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). List them and note what each involves based on your general knowledge.
Induction involves examining particulars to derive a general rule; analogy moves from one example to another based on similarity.
3
Recognize why qiyas is prioritized
The commentator explicitly says that qiyas is 'the mainstay' (al-umda) and 'beneficial for certain knowledge' (al-ilm al-yaqini). This is why discussion of qiyas becomes the 'utmost purpose' in logic.
Induction and analogy provide only probable knowledge, not certainty.
4
Trace the commentator's reasoning for calling qiyas the 'utmost purpose'
The commentator explains that the ultimate goal of the sciences is affirmative knowledge (tasdiq). Since qiyas is the most reliable means to that goal, it is the most important topic in logic.
The logic is instrumental to the sciences; qiyas is the instrument's core.

(قَوْلُهُ : شَرَعَ) أي : حانَ أنْ يشرع فيما هو العُمدة في تحصيل المطالبِ التصديقية. لمفهوم (قَوْلُهُ: وَهِيَ الْقِيَاسِ) أَنَّتَ الصَّمِيرَ لِعودِهِ على المقاصدِ، وجعل القياس مقاصد ؛ باعتبارِ تنوُّعِهِ إلى اقتراني واستثنائي، وكُلُّ منهما تحتَهُ أنواع، فكانَ كُلُّ واحدٍ في نفسِهِ مَقصداً ،مُبالغة، وعبَّرَ غيرُهُ بِلَفْظِ : مقصد ؛ نظراً إلى أنَّ القياس اسمٌ يعم جميع أنواعِهِ، وَوجهُ كَونِ القياس مقصداً ؛ بأن مقاصد العلوم المدوّنةِ الَّتي اعتُبر المنطقُ آلةٌ لها ؛ هي مسائلها الَّتي إدراكهاتُها تصديقات، فالمقصود في تلك العلوم هو الإدراكاتُ التَّصديقيَّةُ، وأمَّا الإدراكاتُ التَّصوريَّةُ ؛ فإِنَّما تُطلب في تركِ العلوم؛ لِكَونها وسائل إلى تلك التصديقات، وإذا كانَ المقصودُ الأصلي هو العِلْمَ التَّصديقي ؛ كانَ البحثُ في هذا الفنّ عن الطَّريقِ الموصل إليهِ أدخلَ في القصدِ بِالقياس إلى البحثِ عن الموصل إلى التَّصورِ، ثمَّ الموصل إلى التصديقِ ينقسم إلى قياس واستقراء وتمثيل، لكن العمدة منها والمفيد للعلم اليقيني هو القياسُ، فَصارَ الكلامُ فيهِ مَقصداً أقصى ومطلباً أعلى في هذا
Section: On Qiyas [Definition of Qiyas] Section: On Qiyas. And when he finished from the principles of affirmations, he began upon their purposes, and it is the chapter of qiyas. So he said: Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'From the principles of affirmations') they are the propositions. Al-'Attar: Section: On Qiyas. It is linguistically: the estimation of an example upon another example, and its technical meaning will come. (His saying: 'From the principles of affirmations') means: the propositions and their rulings. (His saying: 'He began') means: it is time to begin upon what is the mainstay in obtaining affirmative demands. For the concept (His saying: 'and they are qiyas') you make the pronoun refer to the purposes, and he made qiyas purposes, considering its division into conjunctive and exceptional, and each of them has types under it, so each one in itself is a purpose, hyperbolically. And another expressed it with the word 'purpose', considering that qiyas is a name that includes all its types. And the reason for qiyas being a purpose: because the purposes of the codified sciences for which logic is considered an instrument are their issues whose perceptions are affirmations. So the intended thing in those sciences is the affirmative perceptions. As for conceptual perceptions, they are sought only in the composition of the sciences because they are means to those affirmations. And if the primary intention is affirmative knowledge, then the investigation in this art about the path leading to it is more within the intention, by qiyas, than the investigation about the path to conception. Then the path to affirmation divides into qiyas, induction, and analogy. But the mainstay among them and the one beneficial for certain knowledge is qiyas. So discussion of it became the utmost purpose and the highest aim in this.
Source pages p. 759

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on qiyas (syllogism). It begins with a linguistic definition: qiyas is 'the estimation of an example upon another example', meaning analogy or syllogism. The technical definition is deferred. The commentator then explains why the author transitions from the principles of affirmations (tasdiqat) to qiyas. The reason is that the ultimate goal of the sciences is affirmative knowledge (tasdiq), and the means to obtain it are qiyas, induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil). Among these, qiyas is the most important because it yields certain knowledge (yaqini). Therefore, qiyas is the 'utmost purpose' (maqsad aqsa) and 'highest aim' (matlab a'la) in logic. The commentary also notes that qiyas is divided into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptional (istithna'i), each with subtypes. Conceptual perceptions (tasawwurat) are merely means to affirmations, so the focus is on the path to affirmation, which qiyas dominates.

The Syllogism

The Definition of Qiyas and Its Essential Components
Matn defines qiyas; Dasuqi explains its three components: qadaya, lizum, qawl akhar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential components of a syllogism as defined in the matn: propositions, necessary consequence, and distinct conclusion.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a composite saying that intrinsically entails another saying.

This lesson unpacks the classic definition of qiyas (syllogism) from the matn, breaking down each part: what kind of 'saying' it is, the role of propositions (including that they may be true or false), what 'necessary consequence' means, and why the conclusion must be a distinct statement sought after the premises are granted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and genus
The definition begins with 'al-qiyas: qawl mu'allaf' – a syllogism is a composite saying. Note that qawl is the genus, encompassing any meaningful expression. The composition must be from propositions.
2
Understand the material: propositions (qadaya)
Dasuqi explicitly notes that the propositions can be true or false. This is important because syllogisms are used not only in demonstration (burhan) with true premises, but also in rhetoric and sophistry with false ones.
3
Grasp the formal condition: necessary consequence (luzum)
The phrase 'yalzamu li dhatihi' indicates that the conclusion follows from the very composition of the premises, not from an external addition. The necessity is intrinsic.
4
Recognize the distinctness of the conclusion (qawl akhar)
The conclusion is 'another saying' (qawl akhar) meaning it is different from the premises. It is the sought result (matlub), not assumed beforehand like the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس : قول مؤلف من قضايا يلزمه لذاته قول آخر. الدسوقي: (قولُهُ: من قضايا) أي : صادقة أو كاذبة. (قولُهُ : يلزمه) المراد باللزوم أعم من أن يكون بيناً يكفي في العلم باللازمِ العِلمُ بالملزومِ كَالشَّكلِ الأَوَّل، فإنَّ إنتاجَهُ بيِّن لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء، أو يكون غيرَ بَيِّن بأن يكونَ العِلمُ باللازم يحتاج إلى تأمل ونظر بعد العِلم بالملزوم كالأشكال الباقية، فإنَّ إنتاجها غير بين؛ لتوقفه على ردّها لِلشَّكل الأوَّل، إمَّا بعكس الكُبرى كما في الشكل الثَّاني، أو بعكس الصُّغرى كَمَا في الثَّالث، أو يعكسهما معاً كما في الرابع. (قولُهُ : قول آخَرُ) إشارة إلى مغايرة النتيجة لكلِّ من المقدمتين؛ لأنّ النتيجة مطلوبة غير مفروضة التسليم؛ بخلاف المقدّمة.
Qiyas (syllogism) is a composite statement consisting of propositions from which another statement necessarily follows by itself. Al-Dasuqi: 'His saying: of propositions — meaning true or false. His saying: necessarily follows — the meaning of necessity is general, including that which is self-evident, sufficient for knowledge of the necessary from knowledge of the necessitating, like the first figure, for its production is self-evident, not dependent on anything; or that which is not self-evident, such that knowledge of the necessary requires reflection and consideration after knowledge of the necessitating, like the remaining figures, for their production is not self-evident, because it depends on reducing them to the first figure, either by conversion of the major premise as in the second figure, or by conversion of the minor premise as in the third, or by converting both as in the fourth. His saying: another statement — an indication of the conclusion being different from each of the two premises, because the conclusion is sought, not assumed to be accepted, unlike the premise.'
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of qiyas (syllogism) from the matn and its expansion by Dasuqi. The definition states that a syllogism is a composite saying (qawl mu'allaf) composed of propositions (qadaya) that, by virtue of their own composition, necessitate another saying (qawl akhar). Dasuqi explains three key terms: First, 'min qadaya' includes both true and false propositions, because syllogisms can be used not only in demonstration (burhan) with true premises, but also in rhetoric and sophistry with false ones. Second, 'yalzamu' (necessity) covers two types: self-evident necessity, as in the first figure where the conclusion follows immediately without any additional mental step, and non-self-evident necessity, as in the other figures where the conclusion requires converting premises to fit the first figure. For example, in the second figure one converts the major premise, in the third the minor, and in the fourth both. Third, 'qawl akhar' indicates that the conclusion is distinct from the premises; it is what is sought (matlub) and not already assumed, unlike the premises which are granted. This definition thus marks the essential features of a syllogism: it is composed of at least two propositions, they entail a new proposition intrinsically, and the entailment may be immediate or require transformation.

Key Terms
قياس
قول
قضايا
لزوم
نتيجة

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas precisely specifies its logical structure: composite propositions that by themselves necessitate a new proposition.

Self-Evident vs. Non-Self-Evident Necessity in Syllogisms
Dasuqi expands on two types of luzum in the definition of qiyas: self-evident (first figure) and non-self-evident (other figures requiring conversion).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between self-evident necessity (as in the first figure) and non-self-evident necessity (other figures) and describe how conversion operations reduce non-first figures to the first.

Core Idea
The first figure's necessity is immediate; other figures require reduction through conversion.

Dasuqi's commentary explains that the 'necessity' in the definition includes two levels: self-evident (bayyin) as in the first figure where the conclusion is obvious with no extra step, and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin) as in the second, third, and fourth figures where the necessity is only seen after converting premises to fit the first figure. This lesson explores how these reductions work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of luzum
Dasuqi distinguishes between light self-evident (bayyin) necessity, where knowing the premises suffices to know the conclusion, and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin) necessity, where reflection or conversion is needed.
2
Understand the first figure's immediacy
The first figure (shakl awwal) is the standard: the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor. Its production is self-evident, requiring no additional operation.
3
Recognize the need for reduction in other figures
For the second, third, and fourth figures, the conclusion does not follow immediately; one must convert one or both premises to transform the syllogism into a first-figure format.
4
Learn the specific conversion operations
Second figure: convert the major premise (e.g., from 'All A are B' to 'Some B are A'). Third figure: convert the minor premise. Fourth figure: convert both premises. After conversion, the syllogism becomes first-figure and the necessity becomes evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس : قول مؤلف من قضايا يلزمه لذاته قول آخر. الدسوقي: (قولُهُ: من قضايا) أي : صادقة أو كاذبة. (قولُهُ : يلزمه) المراد باللزوم أعم من أن يكون بيناً يكفي في العلم باللازمِ العِلمُ بالملزومِ كَالشَّكلِ الأَوَّل، فإنَّ إنتاجَهُ بيِّن لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء، أو يكون غيرَ بَيِّن بأن يكونَ العِلمُ باللازم يحتاج إلى تأمل ونظر بعد العِلم بالملزوم كالأشكال الباقية، فإنَّ إنتاجها غير بين؛ لتوقفه على ردّها لِلشَّكل الأوَّل، إمَّا بعكس الكُبرى كما في الشكل الثَّاني، أو بعكس الصُّغرى كَمَا في الثَّالث، أو يعكسهما معاً كما في الرابع. (قولُهُ : قول آخَرُ) إشارة إلى مغايرة النتيجة لكلِّ من المقدمتين؛ لأنّ النتيجة مطلوبة غير مفروضة التسليم؛ بخلاف المقدّمة.
Qiyas (syllogism) is a composite statement consisting of propositions from which another statement necessarily follows by itself. Al-Dasuqi: 'His saying: of propositions — meaning true or false. His saying: necessarily follows — the meaning of necessity is general, including that which is self-evident, sufficient for knowledge of the necessary from knowledge of the necessitating, like the first figure, for its production is self-evident, not dependent on anything; or that which is not self-evident, such that knowledge of the necessary requires reflection and consideration after knowledge of the necessitating, like the remaining figures, for their production is not self-evident, because it depends on reducing them to the first figure, either by conversion of the major premise as in the second figure, or by conversion of the minor premise as in the third, or by converting both as in the fourth. His saying: another statement — an indication of the conclusion being different from each of the two premises, because the conclusion is sought, not assumed to be accepted, unlike the premise.'
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of qiyas (syllogism) from the matn and its expansion by Dasuqi. The definition states that a syllogism is a composite saying (qawl mu'allaf) composed of propositions (qadaya) that, by virtue of their own composition, necessitate another saying (qawl akhar). Dasuqi explains three key terms: First, 'min qadaya' includes both true and false propositions, because syllogisms can be used not only in demonstration (burhan) with true premises, but also in rhetoric and sophistry with false ones. Second, 'yalzamu' (necessity) covers two types: self-evident necessity, as in the first figure where the conclusion follows immediately without any additional mental step, and non-self-evident necessity, as in the other figures where the conclusion requires converting premises to fit the first figure. For example, in the second figure one converts the major premise, in the third the minor, and in the fourth both. Third, 'qawl akhar' indicates that the conclusion is distinct from the premises; it is what is sought (matlub) and not already assumed, unlike the premises which are granted. This definition thus marks the essential features of a syllogism: it is composed of at least two propositions, they entail a new proposition intrinsically, and the entailment may be immediate or require transformation.

Key Terms
لزوم بيّن
لزوم غير بيّن
شكل أول
شكل ثان
شكل ثالث
شكل رابع
عكس

Checkpoint
According to Dasuqi, what must be done to make the necessity evident in a second-figure syllogism?
A) Convert the minor premise.
B) Convert the major premise.
C) Convert both premises.
D) Convert the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The necessity in a syllogism may be immediate (first figure) or require transformation (other figures), but all figures ultimately rely on the first figure's clarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the second-figure example: 'The world is changing (minor); everything temporal is changing (major); therefore the world is temporal.' Explain why this is invalid as stated, then convert the major premise to form a valid first-figure syllogism.

The Syllogism

Al-Attar's Refinement of the Definition of Qiyas
Attar raises three objections to the classical definition and provides refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three objections al-Attar raises against the classical definition of qiyas and explain how his responses refine the definition to account for form, exclude extraneous premises, and allow multiple proofs.

Core Idea
A definition must account for both form and matter; Attar shows how the classical definition of qiyas can be refined to avoid ambiguity.

The classical definition of qiyas (syllogism) as 'a composite statement from statements that intrinsically entail another statement' seems straightforward. However, al-Attar identifies three critical ambiguities concerning the role of form, extraneous premises, and the possibility of multiple proofs. His responses sharpen the definition without discarding it, showing the depth of logical analysis in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Classical Definition
Locate the definition being critiqued in the Arabic: 'قَوْلٌ مُؤلَّفٌ مِنْ أَقْوَالٍ مَتَى سَلِمَتْ لَزِمَ عَنْهَا لِذَاتِهَا قَوْلٌ آخَرُ'. Note that the condition 'when they are sound' and the preposition 'an' are omitted to avoid implying that premises are a cause.
The omission is justified by well-known usage.
2
Understand the First Objection
The objection hinges on the role of form (surah). If form is not a part of the syllogism, why mention composition? If it is a part, then the conclusion does not follow from the matter alone, contradicting 'li dhatiha' (by themselves). Grasp this dilemma.
3
Analyze Attar's Answer to the First Objection
Attar clarifies that 'li dhatiha' means 'insofar as they are composed' (min haythu kauniha mu'allafatan). The word 'ta'lif' indicates consideration of form, resolving the dilemma without discarding the definition.
Thus form is an integral part of the entailment.
4
Process the Second Objection and Response
The second objection is that the definition could apply to a composite with extraneous premises. Attar replies that 'yalzamu' (they entail) implies each single premise contributes to the entailment, so extraneous premises are excluded because they have no input.
This refines the definition to require necessity of each premise.
5
Examine the Third Objection and Response
The third objection concerns multiple proofs for one conclusion: if a second proof gives the same knowledge, it is 'tahsil al-hasil'; if different, contradictories occur. Attar answers that the second proof is considered hypothetically: it would give knowledge if presented first, but the actual purpose is knowledge of the proof's indication (wajh al-dalala), not the conclusion itself. This allows multiple proofs and benefits by increasing tranquility (itmi'nan) in the details.
Thus the definition is not undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العطار: الفنّ؛ بالقياس إلى الكلام في الموصل إلى التّصورِ وبالقياس إلى سائر ما يوصل إلى التصديقِ، وَلِهذا جُعِلَ الاستقراءُ والتَّمثيلُ مِن لَواحِقِ القياس وتوابعه . (قَوْلُهُ : القِيَاسُ قَوْلٌ ... إلخ) التّعريف المشهور هنا : قولٌ مُؤلَّفٌ مِن أقوال متى سَلِمَتْ ؛ لزمَ عنها لذاتها قولُ آخَرُ ، فَحُذِفَ قيد : متى سَلِمَتْ، المفيد تعميمُ التَّعريف بِشمولِ الصَّادِقِ مِنَ المقدّماتِ والكاذب منها ، كأنَّهُ لِلاستغناء عنه بالشُّهرة، وحذفُ كلمة عن لإيهامها ؛ كونَ الملزوم وهوَ المقدّماتُ ؛ عِلَّةٌ لِلازم، وهوَ النَّتيجةُ بحسبِ نفس الأمر، فيلزم عدم صدقِ التَّعريف على غيرِ الدَّليلِ اللَّمِّي هذا، وأورد على التّعريف أنَّ الصُّورة إن لم تُعتبر في القياس على طريق الجزئية ؛ يكون القياسُ عبارةً عن مُجرَّدِ القضايا المستلزمةِ لِذاتِهَا قَولاً آخرَ فَلا حاجة في تعريفه إلى إيرادِ قولِهِ : قول مُؤلَّفٌ مِن قضايا ، فإِنَّ لَفْظَ التَّأليفِ مُشعرُ باعتبارِ جزئيَّةِ الصُّورة، بل يكفي أنْ يُقالَ : هوَ قضايا يلزمُهَا لِذَاتِها قولُ آخَرُ ، وإن اعتُبِرَت فيه بالجزئية؛ كانَ لها مدخل في لزومِ النَّتيجةِ، فَلَمْ تلزم مِنْ نفسِ القضايا لِذَاتِهَا الَّتِي هِيَ المادَّةُ فقط، بل تلزمها معَ مُقارنةِ الصُّورةِ، فَلَا يستقيم قوله : يلزمها لذاتها قول آخر، وأيضاً : إن هذا التعريف يصدقُ على القول المؤلّفِ مِنَ القياسِ وَمِنْ مُقدِّماتٍ أُخَرَ لَا دخل لها في الاستلزام، فيلزم أن تكون قياساً بالنسبة إلى النتيجة، وهو خلافُ الظاهر، وأيضاً يقتضي هذا التّعريفُ أن لا يكونَ لشيء واحدٍ دلائل؛ لأنَّهُ إِذا أُقيم دليلٌ أَوَّلاً وحصل منهُ العِلْمُ بِالنَّتيجة، ثمَّ أُقيم عليه دليلٌ آخَرُ ؛ فإن لم يلزم منه العلمُ بِالنَّتيجة؛ لم يكن دليلاً؛ لِعَدَم صِدْقِ تعريف القياس عليه وإن لزم عنه ، فإنْ كانَ ذلكَ العِلْمُ عينَ العِلْم الأَوَّلِ ؛ لَزِمَ تَحصيل الحاصل، وإنْ كانَ غيره؛ لزم اجتماع المتنافين، واللوازمُ كُلُّهَا باطلة، والجوابُ عَنِ الأَوَّلِ : أَنَّا نختارُ أنَّ لِلصُّورة مدخلاً في لزوم النتيجة، ولا : يلزم منه عدمُ صِحَّةِ قَوْلِهِ : (يلزمُ ... إلخ) ، وإنَّما يلزمُ ذلك لو كان معناه : يلزمها وحدَها بدونِ مُلاحظة الغيرِ ، وليس كذلك، بل المرادُ : يلزمُهَا مِن حيثُ كونها مؤلَّفةً، ولفظُ التَّأليف يُشعِرُ باعتبارِ الصُّورةِ، وعن الثَّاني : بأنَّ المتبادرَ مِن لزومِهِ عنها أنَّ لِكُلِّ واحدٍ منها مدخلاً في اللُّزوم ، فتخرج مادَّةُ النَّقض لعدم مدخليَّتِهَا في اللُّزومِ، وعن الثَّالث : بأنَّ كونَ الثَّاني دليلاً عقليًّا على طريق الفرضِ، بمعنى أنَّه لو أُقيمَ قَبلَ إقامةِ الدَّليل الأوَّلِ ؛ يلزمُهُ العِلْمُ بالقول الآخَرِ، فَلَا يكونُ المقصود من النَّظرِ في الدَّليلِ الثَّاني هو العلم المنظور فيهِ الَّذي هو النتيجةُ ، بل العلم بوجه دلالةِ الدَّليلِ الثَّاني عليه، وهذا الوجه غيرُ مَعلوم ههنا ، ولا يلزم طلب الحاصل، بخلافِ ما إذا قصد به العلم بالمنظورِ فيه، فإنَّهُ يَستلزم طلبه مع كونه حاصلاً، والفائدة في طلب العلم الدَّلالة في الدليلِ الثَّاني : زيادة الاطمئنان بتفاصيل الأدلَّةِ.
Al-Attar: [This is] an art; in relation to discourse about that which leads to conception (tasawwur) and in relation to all that leads to assent (tasdiq). For this reason, induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil) have been made appendages and followers of syllogism. (His saying: 'Qiyas is a statement... etc.') The well-known definition here is: a composite statement from statements which, when they are sound, necessitates by itself another statement. So the condition 'when they are sound' was omitted, which is useful for generalizing the definition to include true and false premises, as if it was omitted due to its being well-known. The word 'an' (from) was omitted because it might imply that the necessitating, i.e. the premises, is a cause for the necessary, i.e. the conclusion, in re, leading to the definition not being true of non-apodictic (lami) proofs. And objections were raised against the definition: (1) if form is not considered in the syllogism as a part, then the syllogism would be merely the propositions that intrinsically entail another statement, so there would be no need in its definition to include 'a composite statement from propositions', for the word 'composition' suggests that form is considered as a part; rather it would suffice to say 'it is propositions that intrinsically entail another statement'. But if form is considered as a part, then it has an input in the entailment of the conclusion, so the conclusion would not follow from the same propositions by themselves, which are only the matter, but rather it follows with the accompaniment of form, so his saying 'they intrinsically entail another statement' is not correct. (2) Also, this definition applies to a composite consisting of a syllogism and other premises that have no role in the entailment, so it would follow that such a composite is a syllogism with respect to the conclusion, which is contrary to what is apparent. (3) Also, this definition entails that there cannot be multiple proofs for one thing; because if one proof is first given and knowledge of the conclusion is obtained, then another proof is given for it, if that other does not produce knowledge of the conclusion, it is not a proof for lack of fitting the definition; but if it does produce knowledge, then if that knowledge is the same as the first knowledge, it is a case of obtaining the already obtained (tahsil al-hasil); if it is different, then two contradictories would occur. All these consequences are false. The answer to the first: we choose that form has an input in the entailment of the conclusion, but this does not make his saying 'it entails...' incorrect; that would only happen if its meaning were 'they entail alone without consideration of anything else', but it is not so; rather it means 'they entail insofar as they are composed', and the word 'composition' suggests consideration of form. The answer to the second: what is understood from 'they entail' is that each single one has an input in the entailment, so the extraneous matter (maddat al-naqd) is excluded because it has no input in the entailment. The answer to the third: that the second proof is a rational proof in a hypothetical way, meaning that if it had been given before the first proof, it would have produced knowledge of the other statement; so the purpose of considering the second proof is not the knowledge of the object of consideration which is the conclusion, but rather knowledge of the manner of indication (wajh al-dalala) of the second proof upon it, and this manner is not known here; thus obtaining the already obtained is not required, unlike if one intended by it knowledge of the object of consideration, for then it would require seeking it when it is already obtained. The benefit of seeking knowledge of the indication in the second proof is an increase in tranquility (itmi'nan) regarding the details of proofs.
Source pages p. 760

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by al-Attar. He first presents the standard definition of qiyas and notes that the condition 'idha salimat' (when sound) was omitted because it is well-known that premises can be false, and the preposition 'an' was omitted to avoid implying that premises are efficient causes of the conclusion. Then Attar raises three objections: (1) The role of form (surah) is ambiguous—if form is not a part, why mention 'mu'allaf'? If it is a part, then the conclusion does not follow from the matter alone, contradicting 'li dhatiha'. (2) The definition could include extraneous premises that do not contribute to entailment. (3) It seems to preclude multiple proofs for one conclusion. Attar answers each: (1) Form is considered, but 'li dhatiha' means 'insofar as they are composed', and 'ta'lif' indicates form. (2) The phrase 'yalzamu' implies each premise contributes, so extra premises are excluded. (3) The second proof is hypothetical—it would have given knowledge if presented first; the actual aim is knowledge of the proof's indication, not the conclusion itself, thus avoiding tahsil al-hasil. The benefit is increased confidence (itmi'nan) in the details of proofs. An example: proving that nabidh is haram via two different arguments; the second argument does not seek to reobtain the same knowledge but to understand the proof's indicatory power.

مادة
لزوم
دليل
نتاج

Checkpoint
How does Attar answer the first objection that if form is considered a part, the conclusion does not follow from the premises 'by themselves'?
A) He denies that form has any role in entailment.
B) He says 'by themselves' means 'insofar as they are composed', which includes form.
C) He argues that the premises alone are sufficient without form.
D) He redefines the syllogism to exclude form.

Key Takeaway
Attar's three objections refine the definition of qiyas by clarifying the roles of form, premise contribution, and the nature of multiple proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'Qiyas is an argument that transfers the ruling of a case (asl) to another case (far') based on a shared 'illah.' Using Attar's first objection, check whether this definition explicitly includes the form (i.e., the structure of analogy) or only the matter (premises). Does it need to mention 'composition' like the logical definition?
?
In a fiqhi example, suppose a jurist argues that nabidh is haram based on the 'illah of intoxication, and then offers another argument based on the 'illah of being a mind-altering substance. Apply Attar's third objection and response: does the second argument produce redundant knowledge, or does it provide additional knowledge of the indication (wajh al-dalala)? Explain.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Definition of Qiyas: Conditions and Objections
Author defines qiyas and responds to three objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions in the definition of qiyas (mu'allaf min qadaya and li-dhatihi) and explain how they exclude induction, analogy, compound propositions, and the syllogism of equality.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a composite that necessitates by itself; not every inference counts.

This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) as the heart of demonstrative reasoning in Islamic logic. The author lays down a precise definition with two conditions and then defends it against three objections that test its exclusivity and comprehensiveness. By working through the objections, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate definitions and distinguish genuine syllogisms from mere inferences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Find the Arabic sentence that defines qiyas. Note its genus (qawl) and differentia (mu'allaf min qadaya...lazima anha li-dhatiha qawl akhar).
2
Understand the two conditions
The definition has two key conditions: composed of propositions (mu'allaf min qadaya) and necessitating by itself (li-dhatihi). Discuss why each is necessary.
3
Analyze the first objection
The first objection claims the definition is not exclusive because it includes compound propositions that imply their own converse. Follow the response: such propositions lack the specific composition (ta'lif) that yields implication through intellectual movement.
4
Analyze the second and third objections
The second objection concerns non-demonstrative arguments; the third concerns multiple proofs. See how the responses refine the definition by emphasizing form (sura) and the distinction between seeking the conclusion and seeking the aspect of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ. فَالقولُ - وهو المفهوم المركَّبُ المؤلَّفُ من قضايا إذا سُلِّمَت لزم عنها لذاتها قول آخر. قال الشارح: يعني أن القياس يُطلق على المعقول والملفوظ على قياس القول والقضية. فإن كان المعرَّف هو القياس المعقول؛ لأنه هو القياس حقيقة، وهو اللائق بنظر الفنّ، ولذلك قال الشيخ في الشفاء: القياس المسموع ليس بقياس من حيث اللفظ، فإن اللفظ من حيث هو لفظ لا يستلزم لفظاً آخر، بل من حيث إنه دال على معنى معقول، لكن القياس المعقول كافٍ في تحصيل المطالب البرهانية، كان المراد بالقول الأول والقضايا الأمور المعقولة. وإن كان المعرَّف هو الملفوظ؛ كان المراد بها الأمور الملفوظة. وأورد على هذا التعريف أولاً أنه غير مانع لدخول القضية المركبة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها. وأجاب الشارح بأنها خرجت بالقيد الأول إذ هي قضية واحدة مستقلة بالإيجاب أو السلب واللادوام تابع لها. وأجاب العطار بأنها على فرض أنها قضيتان خرجت بقيد التأليف إذ هو ارتباط خاص يحدث عنه جزء صوري ينشأ عنه هذا الاستلزام والقضية المركبة ليست كذلك فإن كل واحدة على حيالها مستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها، وأيضاً لزوم المقدمات للنتيجة إنما هو بالحركة الفكرية الواقعة في الترتيب، وهذا المعنى مفقود في لزوم عكس المركبة فإنه بحسب الواقع ونفس الأمر بدليل أننا نعلم قضايا ولا يخطر ببالنا عكسها ولا عكس نقيضها. وثانياً: أنه غير جامع لخروج غير البرهاني كالخطابي والجدلي مما يفيد الظن إذ لا يلزمه قول آخر كالاستقراء والتمثيل. وأجيب بأن ظنيتهما من جهة ظن المقدمات، وأما النتيجة فلازمة للمقدمات من جهة اندراج الحد الأصغر في الأكبر ولا كذلك الاستقراء والتمثيل لفقد الصورة فيهما. وثالثاً: أن هذا التعريف يقتضي أن لا يكون لشيء واحد دلائل؛ لأنه إذا أقيم دليل أولاً وحصل منه العلم بالنتيجة، ثم أقيم عليه دليل آخر؛ فإن لم يلزم منه العلم بالنتيجة؛ لم يكن دليلاً؛ لعدم صدق تعريف القياس عليه، وإن لزم عنه، فإن كان ذلك العلم عين العلم الأول؛ لزم تحصيل الحاصل، وإن كان غيره؛ لزم اجتماع المتنافين، واللوازم كلها باطلة. والجواب عن الأول: أنا نختار أن للصورة مدخلاً في لزوم النتيجة، ولا يلزم منه عدم صحة قوله: (يلزم ... إلخ)، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان معناه: يلزمها وحدها بدون ملاحظة الغير، وليس كذلك، بل المراد: يلزمها من حيث كونها مؤلفة، ولفظ التأليف يشعر باعتبار الصورة. وعن الثاني: بأن المتبادر من لزومه عنها أن لكل واحد منها مدخلاً في اللزوم، فتخرج مادة النقض لعدم مدخليتها في اللزوم. وعن الثالث: بأن كون الثاني دليلاً عقلياً على طريق الفرض، بمعنى أنه لو أقيم قبل إقامة الدليل الأول؛ يلزمه العلم بالقول الآخر، فلا يكون المقصود من النظر في الدليل الثاني هو العلم المنظور فيه الذي هو النتيجة، بل العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل الثاني عليه، وهذا الوجه غير معلوم ههنا، ولا يلزم طلب الحاصل، بخلاف ما إذا قصد به العلم بالمنظور فيه، فإنه يستلزم طلبه مع كونه حاصلاً، والفائدة في طلب العلم الدلالة في الدليل الثاني: زيادة الاطمئنان بتفاصيل الأدلة. وبما ذكرنا تعلم أن الاستقراء والتمثيل والقضية المركبة والبسيطة خرجت بالقيد الأول وهو قوله: مؤلّف من قضايا، والشارح لم يخرج به إلا البسيطة، وأن قياس المساواة يخرج بالقيد الثاني وهو قوله: يلزمه لذاته. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Chapter: On the Syllogism. The statement – which is the composite concept composed of propositions such that if they are granted, another statement follows from them by virtue of itself. The commentator said: He means that the syllogism is applied to the intelligible and the uttered, analogous to the statement and proposition. If what is defined is the intelligible syllogism – because it is the syllogism in reality, and it is suitable for the view of the art – therefore the Shaykh said in al-Shifa: The heard syllogism is not a syllogism in terms of utterance, for the utterance in terms of being an utterance does not necessitate another utterance, but rather in terms of it indicating an intelligible meaning. However, the intelligible syllogism suffices for obtaining demonstrative demands. Then the first statement and propositions are intended as intelligible things. And if what is defined is the uttered syllogism, then they are intended as uttered things. An objection is raised against this definition, firstly: that it is not exclusive because it includes the compound proposition that necessitates its converse or the contrapositive of its converse. The commentator answered that it is excluded by the first condition, for it is a single proposition independent in affirmation or negation, and its implication is subsidiary to it. Al-Attar answered that on the assumption that they are two propositions, they are excluded by the condition of composition, for it is a specific connection that produces a formal part from which this implication arises, but the compound proposition is not like that, for each one by itself necessitates its converse or the contrapositive of its converse. Also, the implication of the premises for the conclusion is only through the intellectual movement occurring in arrangement, and this meaning is absent in the implication of the converse of a compound, for it is according to reality and the matter itself, as evidenced by the fact that we know propositions and do not think of their converse or the contrapositive of their converse. Secondly: that it is not comprehensive because it excludes non-demonstrative [syllogisms] such as rhetorical and dialectical [syllogisms] that yield probability, for they do not necessitate another statement like induction and analogy. It was answered that their probability is due to the probability of the premises, but as for the conclusion, it is necessitated by the premises due to the inclusion of the minor term in the major, unlike induction and analogy because of the absence of form in them. Thirdly: that this definition implies that there are no multiple proofs for one thing; because if a proof is first established and knowledge of the conclusion is obtained from it, then another proof is established upon it; if the latter does not entail knowledge of the conclusion, then it is not a proof, due to the definition not applying to it; and if it does entail it, then if that knowledge is the same as the first knowledge, it entails obtaining what is already obtained; and if it is different, it entails the coexistence of contradictories; and all consequences are false. The answer to the first: we choose that the form has an input in the necessitation of the conclusion, and it does not entail the incorrectness of his statement (it necessitates...), for that would only be the case if its meaning were: it necessitates it alone without noticing the other, but it is not so; rather, the meaning is: it necessitates it in terms of being composed, and the word 'composition' indicates considering the form. And regarding the second: because what is understood from its being necessitated by them is that each one of them has an input in the necessitation, thus the material of refutation is excluded due to its lack of input in the necessitation. And regarding the third: because the second proof being an intellectual proof hypothetically, meaning that if it were established before the first proof, it would necessitate knowledge of the other statement; so the purpose of considering the second proof is not the knowledge being considered which is the conclusion, but rather knowledge of the aspect of indication of the second proof upon it, and this aspect is not known here; and it does not entail seeking what is obtained, unlike if by it one intends knowledge of what is being considered, for then it entails seeking it while it is already obtained; and the benefit in seeking indicative knowledge in the second proof is increased reassurance in the details of the proofs. And from what we have mentioned you know that induction, analogy, compound proposition, and simple proposition are excluded by the first condition, which is his statement: composed of propositions; and the commentator did not exclude by it except the simple [proposition]; and that the syllogism of equality is excluded by the second condition, which is his statement: it necessitates by itself. End of al-Sharnubi's words.
Source pages p. 761

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by stating the definition: 'The statement – which is the composite concept composed of propositions such that if they are granted, another statement follows from them by virtue of itself.' The commentator then explains that qiyas applies to both intelligible (ma'qul) and uttered (malfuz) forms, quoting al-Shifa to argue that the heard syllogism is not a syllogism by virtue of utterance but by virtue of its intelligible meaning. The passage then presents three objections to the definition and their answers.

First objection: The definition is not exclusive (mani') because it includes compound propositions that imply their own converse or contrapositive. The answer from al-Attar explains that such compound propositions lack the specific composition (ta'lif) that generates the implication; the implication of premises for the conclusion arises from a formal structure (sura) and intellectual movement, unlike the automatic implication of a compound proposition. Second objection: The definition is not comprehensive (jami') because it excludes non-demonstrative arguments like rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms that yield only probability, as well as induction and analogy. The answer clarifies that the probability stems from the premises, not the form; in proper qiyas, the conclusion necessarily follows due to the inclusion of the minor term in the major, which is absent in induction and analogy. Third objection: The definition implies that one thing cannot have multiple proofs, leading to either redundancy or contradiction. The answer distinguishes between seeking the conclusion itself and seeking the aspect (wajh) of its indication; the second proof provides increased reassurance (itmi'nan) by clarifying the aspect, not the conclusion.

The passage concludes by summarizing that induction, analogy, compound, and simple propositions are excluded by the first condition (composed of propositions), and the 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat) is excluded by the second condition (necessitates by itself). This discussion guards against misunderstandings about the scope and conditions of qiyas, ensuring that only valid demonstrative syllogisms are included.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): A composite concept composed of propositions that, when granted, necessitate another statement by virtue of themselves; includes both intelligible and uttered forms. [Note: In earlier texts like al-Abhari's Isaghuji, qiyas is defined narrowly as categorical syllogism; here it is expanded to include any valid inference form, but still excludes induction and analogy.]
قول (qawl): A statement or composite concept that is the genus in the definition of qiyas. [Note: Stable term across the tradition; sometimes used for proposition (qadiyya) but here it is the broader composite.]
مؤلّف من قضايا (mu'allaf min qadaya): Composed of propositions; the first condition that excludes simple and compound propositions that are not composed in the specific syllogistic way. [Note: No significant shift.]
لذاته (li-dhatihi): By virtue of itself; the second condition that excludes qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) where the implication is not intrinsic. [Note: No significant shift; a standard condition in definitions of qiyas.]
استقراء (istiqra'): Induction; excluded from qiyas because it lacks the formal structure that necessitates the conclusion with certainty. [Note: In later texts, istiqra' is sometimes considered a form of qiyas, but here it is strictly excluded.]
تمثيل (tamthil): Analogy; a non-demonstrative argument that yields probability, excluded from strict qiyas due to lack of formal necessity. [Note: In some traditions, tamthil is considered a type of qiyas, but here it is distinguished.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is induction (istiqra') not considered a valid qiyas?
A) Because its premises are always false.
B) Because it lacks the formal structure that necessitates the conclusion.
C) Because it is used only in rhetoric.
D) Because it does not involve propositions.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas requires both composition from propositions and intrinsic necessity, which excludes induction, analogy, and other non-demonstrative forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following argument meets the definition of qiyas: 'All intoxicants are forbidden (khamr). Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore, nabidh is forbidden.' Explain which conditions it satisfies.

Given the definition, explain why the argument 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause' is a valid qiyas, while the argument 'We have observed many crows and they are black, so all crows are black' is not.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Defining Qiyas: Mental vs Uttered and the Three Restrictions
Author defines qiyas, distinguishes mental from uttered, and lists three restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three restrictions in the definition of qiyas and explain the distinction between mental and uttered syllogism.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a mental or uttered composition that entails another proposition by itself.

Before we can evaluate arguments, we must know what counts as a syllogism (qiyas). The author gives a precise definition with three restrictions that exclude induction, analogy, and simple propositions. He also clarifies that the definition can apply to either mental or uttered forms, following Avicenna's insight that the uttered syllogism is syllogistic only because of its mental meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The definiendum is 'al-qiyas' (syllogism). Notice that the definition is given in the commentary, not in the matn itself.
2
Distinguish mental (ma'qul) vs uttered (malafuz) qiyas
The author explains that the definition can apply to either, but true qiyas is the mental one. Avicenna argued that uttered words do not entail other words unless they indicate mental meanings.
This avoids confusion: a grammatical sentence does not make a syllogism.
3
Extract the three restrictions
The definition restricts qiyas to: (1) composed of propositions (ta'lif min qadaya), (2) from which another saying is entailed (yalzimuhu qawl akhar), and (3) that entailment is 'by itself' (li-dhatihi).
These restrictions are the tools to exclude non-syllogistic arguments.
4
See what each restriction excludes
The first excludes simple propositions; the second excludes incomplete induction and analogy because they yield only opinion (zann), not necessary entailment; the third excludes 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat).
Induction and analogy are not false, but they lack the formal necessity of syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعقول والملفوظ على قياس القول والقضيَّة، فإن كان المعرَّفُ هو القياس المعقول؛ لأنه هو القياس حقيقةٌ، وهو اللائق بنظرِ الفنّ، ولذلك قال الشيخ في الشّفاء: القياسُ المسموع ليس بقياس من حيث اللفظ ، فإِنَّ اللفظ من حيث هو لفظ لا يستلزم لفظاً آخَرَ، بلْ مِن حيثُ إِنَّه دال على معنى معقول، لكنَّ القياس المعقول كافٍ في تحصيل المطالب البرهانيَّةِ، كانَ المراد بالقولِ الأَوَّلِ والقضايا الأمورُ المعقولة، وإنْ كانَ المعرَّفُ هو الملفوظ ؛ كانَ المراد بها الأمور الملفوظة، وعلى يلزمه قول آخر، وخرج به الاستقراء الناقص والتمثيل لإفادتهما الظَّنِّ . والثَّالث : قوله : لذاته وخرج به قياس المساواة. وأورد على هذا التعريف أولاً أنه غير مانع لدخول القضية المركبة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها . وأجاب الشارح بأنها خرجت بالقيد الأوّل إذ هي قضية واحدة مستقلة بالإيجاب أو السلب واللادوام تابع لها وأجاب العطار بأنها على فرض أنها قضيتان خرجت بقيد التأليف إذ هو ارتباط خاص يحدث عنه جزء صوري ينشأ عنه هذا الاستلزام والقضية المركبة ليست كذلك فإن كل واحدة على حيالها مستلزمة لعكسها أو عکس نقيضها، وأيضاً لزوم المقدّمات للنتيجة إنما هو بالحركة الفكرية الواقعة في الترتيب، وهذا المعنى مفقود في لزوم عكس المركبة فإنه بحسب الواقع ونفس الأمر بدليل أننا نعلم قضايا ولا يخطر ببالنا عكسها ولا عكس نقيضها . ا . هـ. باختصار وهو كلام حق يجب الحرص عليه. وثانياً : أنَّه غير جامع لخروج غير البرهاني كالخطابي والجدلي مما يفيد الظَّنِّ إذ لا يلزمه قول آخر كالاستقراء والتمثيل وأجيب بأن ظنيتهما من جهة ظنّ المقدّمات، وأما النتيجة فلازمة للمقدّمات من جهة اندراج الحد الأصغر في الأكبر ولا كذلك الاستقراء والتمثيل لفقد الصورة فيهما ولذا لو ردا إلى القياس المنطقي لكانا منه بأن يقال : النبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر حرام ينتج : النبيذ ،حرام، ويقال في الاستقراء الحيوان أنواعه كثيرة وكل أنواعه تحرك فكها الأسفل عند المضغ ينتج : الحيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ إذ المدار على تسليم المقدمات وبما ذكرنا تعلم أن الاستقراء والتمثيل والقضية المركبة والبسيطة خرجت بالقيد الأول وهو قوله : مؤلّف من قضايا والشَّارح لم يخرج به إلا البسيطة، وأن قياس المساواة يخرج بالقيد الثاني وهو قوله : يلزمه لذاته ... إلخ. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
The mental and the uttered are parallel to the saying and the proposition. If the defined is the mental syllogism—because it is the true syllogism and befitting the discipline's purview, hence the Shaykh said in al-Shifa: 'The audible syllogism is not a syllogism in terms of the utterance, for the utterance as utterance does not entail another utterance, but only insofar as it indicates a mental meaning'—then the first saying and the propositions are understood as mental matters. If the defined is the uttered, then they are understood as uttered matters. And upon that, another saying is entailed, and by this incomplete induction and analogy are excluded because they yield opinion. The third [restriction] is 'by itself,' and by it syllogism of equality is excluded. An objection was raised against this definition: first, that it is not exclusive because it includes the composite proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. The commentator answered that it is excluded by the first restriction, for it is a single proposition independent in affirmation or negation, and the non-eternity is subordinate to it. Al-'Attar answered that even if it were two propositions, it is excluded by the restriction of composition, for composition is a specific connection that produces a formal part from which this entailment arises, which is not the case for the composite proposition, for each part individually entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. Moreover, the entailment of premises for the conclusion is only through intellectual movement occurring in the arrangement, and this meaning is absent in the entailment of the converse of a composite, for it is according to reality and the matter itself, as evidenced by the fact that we know propositions and their converse does not occur to our minds. [...] And secondly, that it is not comprehensive because it excludes the non-demonstrative [syllogisms] such as rhetorical and dialectical that yield opinion, since they do not entail another saying like induction and analogy. It was answered that their probability is due to the probability of the premises, but the conclusion is necessitated by the premises in terms of the subsumption of the minor term under the major, unlike induction and analogy which lack the form. Thus if they were reduced to logical syllogism, they would be of that type: e.g., 'Nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden' yields 'Nabidh is forbidden,' and in induction: 'The animal's species are many, and all its species move their lower jaw when chewing' yields 'The animal moves its lower jaw when chewing,' for the criterion is the acceptance of premises. From this you learn that induction, analogy, composite and simple propositions are excluded by the first restriction, which is 'composed of propositions,' and the commentator excluded only the simple by it, and that the syllogism of equality is excluded by the second restriction, which is 'entails by itself' etc. End of al-Sharnubi's commentary.
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by clarifying that the definition can apply to either the mental (ma'qul) or uttered (malafuz) syllogism. The author cites Avicenna's al-Shifa to argue that the uttered syllogism is not a syllogism in terms of the utterance alone, because an utterance as such does not necessitate another utterance; only insofar as it indicates a mental meaning does it entail. Therefore, if the defined is the mental syllogism, the 'first saying' and 'propositions' in the definition refer to mental matters; if uttered, then to uttered matters. The definition includes three restrictions: (1) composed of propositions, (2) from which another saying is entailed, and (3) by itself. The first excludes simple propositions; the second excludes incomplete induction and analogy (since they yield only opinion, not certain entailment); the third excludes qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality, where the middle term is equal to both extremes but does not yield a new conclusion?). The text then presents two objections. The first objection is that the definition is not exclusive (ghayr mani') because it would include a composite proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. Two answers are given: the commentator (al-Sharh) says such a composite is a single proposition, thus excluded by the first restriction; al-'Attar adds that even if considered as two propositions, the entailment is not due to the intellectual movement of syllogistic arrangement but is a direct logical consequence. The second objection is that the definition is not comprehensive (ghayr jami') because non-demonstrative arguments like rhetorical or dialectical ones, though they yield only opinion, also entail conclusions but are excluded. The answer distinguishes: in syllogism, the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises via the form (subsumption of minor under major), whereas in induction and analogy, the form is missing. However, if they are recast in syllogistic form (e.g., 'Nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden, therefore nabidh is forbidden'), they become valid syllogisms. The passage concludes by noting that all excluded types are removed by the first two restrictions.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

Responding to Objections: Exclusivity and Comprehensiveness of the Definition
Author rebuts two objections on exclusivity and comprehensiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the two objections against the definition of qiyas and evaluate the replies, including the reduction of non-syllogistic arguments to syllogistic form.

Core Idea
The definition of qiyas is defended against objections by distinguishing syllogistic entailment from mere logical equivalence and by showing that non-demonstrative arguments lack syllogistic form.

After defining qiyas, the author addresses two critical objections: (1) the definition is not exclusive because a composite proposition can entail its converse, and (2) it is not comprehensive because it excludes rhetorical and dialectical arguments that also yield conclusions. The replies refine our understanding of what makes syllogistic entailment unique: it requires intellectual movement and proper form, not just any logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
State the first objection
The objector says: a composite proposition (e.g., 'If A then B, and if B then C') may entail its converse or the converse of its contradictory, so it would wrongly be included in the definition.
This challenges the 'entailment' restriction.
2
Analyze the two replies to the first objection
Al-Sharh replies that such a composite is a single proposition, so it is excluded by the 'composition' restriction. Al-'Attar adds that even if it were two propositions, the entailment is not syllogistic because it does not arise from intellectual movement in arrangement.
The key is that syllogistic entailment is active, not merely logical equivalence.
3
State the second objection
The objector says: non-demonstrative arguments (rhetorical, dialectical) yield conclusions but are excluded, so the definition is too narrow.
This challenges the 'comprehensiveness' of the definition.
4
Analyze the reply to the second objection
The answer distinguishes: in syllogisms, the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises via form (subsumption of minor under major). In induction and analogy, the conclusion is only probable, and the form is missing; but if they are recast as syllogisms, they become valid.
The passage gives the example: 'Nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden, therefore nabidh is forbidden'.
5
Observe the reduction of induction/analogy
The commentator shows that induction and analogy can be turned into categorical syllogisms by supplying the missing premises or recasting the argument. For induction: 'The animal's species are many, and all its species move their lower jaw when chewing' can be turned into a syllogism.
This shows that the definition of qiyas is about form, not content.

المعقول والملفوظ على قياس القول والقضيَّة، فإن كان المعرَّفُ هو القياس المعقول؛ لأنه هو القياس حقيقةٌ، وهو اللائق بنظرِ الفنّ، ولذلك قال الشيخ في الشّفاء: القياسُ المسموع ليس بقياس من حيث اللفظ ، فإِنَّ اللفظ من حيث هو لفظ لا يستلزم لفظاً آخَرَ، بلْ مِن حيثُ إِنَّه دال على معنى معقول، لكنَّ القياس المعقول كافٍ في تحصيل المطالب البرهانيَّةِ، كانَ المراد بالقولِ الأَوَّلِ والقضايا الأمورُ المعقولة، وإنْ كانَ المعرَّفُ هو الملفوظ ؛ كانَ المراد بها الأمور الملفوظة، وعلى يلزمه قول آخر، وخرج به الاستقراء الناقص والتمثيل لإفادتهما الظَّنِّ . والثَّالث : قوله : لذاته وخرج به قياس المساواة. وأورد على هذا التعريف أولاً أنه غير مانع لدخول القضية المركبة المستلزمة لعكسها أو عكس نقيضها . وأجاب الشارح بأنها خرجت بالقيد الأوّل إذ هي قضية واحدة مستقلة بالإيجاب أو السلب واللادوام تابع لها وأجاب العطار بأنها على فرض أنها قضيتان خرجت بقيد التأليف إذ هو ارتباط خاص يحدث عنه جزء صوري ينشأ عنه هذا الاستلزام والقضية المركبة ليست كذلك فإن كل واحدة على حيالها مستلزمة لعكسها أو عکس نقيضها، وأيضاً لزوم المقدّمات للنتيجة إنما هو بالحركة الفكرية الواقعة في الترتيب، وهذا المعنى مفقود في لزوم عكس المركبة فإنه بحسب الواقع ونفس الأمر بدليل أننا نعلم قضايا ولا يخطر ببالنا عكسها ولا عكس نقيضها . ا . هـ. باختصار وهو كلام حق يجب الحرص عليه. وثانياً : أنَّه غير جامع لخروج غير البرهاني كالخطابي والجدلي مما يفيد الظَّنِّ إذ لا يلزمه قول آخر كالاستقراء والتمثيل وأجيب بأن ظنيتهما من جهة ظنّ المقدّمات، وأما النتيجة فلازمة للمقدّمات من جهة اندراج الحد الأصغر في الأكبر ولا كذلك الاستقراء والتمثيل لفقد الصورة فيهما ولذا لو ردا إلى القياس المنطقي لكانا منه بأن يقال : النبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر حرام ينتج : النبيذ ،حرام، ويقال في الاستقراء الحيوان أنواعه كثيرة وكل أنواعه تحرك فكها الأسفل عند المضغ ينتج : الحيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ إذ المدار على تسليم المقدمات وبما ذكرنا تعلم أن الاستقراء والتمثيل والقضية المركبة والبسيطة خرجت بالقيد الأول وهو قوله : مؤلّف من قضايا والشَّارح لم يخرج به إلا البسيطة، وأن قياس المساواة يخرج بالقيد الثاني وهو قوله : يلزمه لذاته ... إلخ. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
The mental and the uttered are parallel to the saying and the proposition. If the defined is the mental syllogism—because it is the true syllogism and befitting the discipline's purview, hence the Shaykh said in al-Shifa: 'The audible syllogism is not a syllogism in terms of the utterance, for the utterance as utterance does not entail another utterance, but only insofar as it indicates a mental meaning'—then the first saying and the propositions are understood as mental matters. If the defined is the uttered, then they are understood as uttered matters. And upon that, another saying is entailed, and by this incomplete induction and analogy are excluded because they yield opinion. The third [restriction] is 'by itself,' and by it syllogism of equality is excluded. An objection was raised against this definition: first, that it is not exclusive because it includes the composite proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. The commentator answered that it is excluded by the first restriction, for it is a single proposition independent in affirmation or negation, and the non-eternity is subordinate to it. Al-'Attar answered that even if it were two propositions, it is excluded by the restriction of composition, for composition is a specific connection that produces a formal part from which this entailment arises, which is not the case for the composite proposition, for each part individually entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. Moreover, the entailment of premises for the conclusion is only through intellectual movement occurring in the arrangement, and this meaning is absent in the entailment of the converse of a composite, for it is according to reality and the matter itself, as evidenced by the fact that we know propositions and their converse does not occur to our minds. [...] And secondly, that it is not comprehensive because it excludes the non-demonstrative [syllogisms] such as rhetorical and dialectical that yield opinion, since they do not entail another saying like induction and analogy. It was answered that their probability is due to the probability of the premises, but the conclusion is necessitated by the premises in terms of the subsumption of the minor term under the major, unlike induction and analogy which lack the form. Thus if they were reduced to logical syllogism, they would be of that type: e.g., 'Nabidh is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden' yields 'Nabidh is forbidden,' and in induction: 'The animal's species are many, and all its species move their lower jaw when chewing' yields 'The animal moves its lower jaw when chewing,' for the criterion is the acceptance of premises. From this you learn that induction, analogy, composite and simple propositions are excluded by the first restriction, which is 'composed of propositions,' and the commentator excluded only the simple by it, and that the syllogism of equality is excluded by the second restriction, which is 'entails by itself' etc. End of al-Sharnubi's commentary.
Source pages p. 762

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by clarifying that the definition can apply to either the mental (ma'qul) or uttered (malafuz) syllogism. The author cites Avicenna's al-Shifa to argue that the uttered syllogism is not a syllogism in terms of the utterance alone, because an utterance as such does not necessitate another utterance; only insofar as it indicates a mental meaning does it entail. Therefore, if the defined is the mental syllogism, the 'first saying' and 'propositions' in the definition refer to mental matters; if uttered, then to uttered matters. The definition includes three restrictions: (1) composed of propositions, (2) from which another saying is entailed, and (3) by itself. The first excludes simple propositions; the second excludes incomplete induction and analogy (since they yield only opinion, not certain entailment); the third excludes qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality, where the middle term is equal to both extremes but does not yield a new conclusion?). The text then presents two objections. The first objection is that the definition is not exclusive (ghayr mani') because it would include a composite proposition that entails its converse or the converse of its contradictory. Two answers are given: the commentator (al-Sharh) says such a composite is a single proposition, thus excluded by the first restriction; al-'Attar adds that even if considered as two propositions, the entailment is not due to the intellectual movement of syllogistic arrangement but is a direct logical consequence. The second objection is that the definition is not comprehensive (ghayr jami') because non-demonstrative arguments like rhetorical or dialectical ones, though they yield only opinion, also entail conclusions but are excluded. The answer distinguishes: in syllogism, the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises via the form (subsumption of minor under major), whereas in induction and analogy, the form is missing. However, if they are recast in syllogistic form (e.g., 'Nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicant is forbidden, therefore nabidh is forbidden'), they become valid syllogisms. The passage concludes by noting that all excluded types are removed by the first two restrictions.

عكس
عكس النقيض
الحركة الفكرية
الحد الأصغر
الحد الأكبر

Checkpoint
In the reply to the second objection, why do rhetorical and dialectical arguments fail to be syllogisms even though they yield a conclusion?
A) Because they lack a middle term.
B) Because their conclusions are not entailed by the premises.
C) Because their probability comes from the premises, but the conclusion would follow necessarily if the premises were certain—however, the form (subsumption) is present, so they are syllogisms, but the objector was wrong.
D) Because they lack the proper form (subsumption of the minor under the major) and thus the conclusions are not necessary in themselves.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas is defended by showing that non-syllogistic arguments either lack proper composition or lack the formal arrangement that produces necessary entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the argument: 'Every khamr is forbidden; this beverage is khamr; therefore this beverage is forbidden.' Identify whether this is a valid categorical syllogism according to the definition. Then consider an objection that it is not exclusive because a simple proposition like 'All khamr is forbidden' entails its converse by subalternation? Explain why that does not violate the definition.
?
Construct a reduction of an inductive argument from hadith science into a categorical syllogism. For example, 'All narrated hadiths from reliable chains are accepted; this hadith is narrated from a reliable chain; therefore it is accepted.' Check if the form holds.

The Syllogism

Mental vs Uttered Syllogism and the Notion of Entailment
Author critically examines whether the definition of qiyas should be mental or uttered, and refines the concept of logical entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the mental and uttered syllogism and evaluate the role of real vs epistemic entailment in defining qiyas.

Core Idea
The definition of qiyas must be for mental syllogism because entailment is real, not merely epistemic.

This passage from a commentary on the Tadhheeb critically examines the definition of qiyas. The authors debate whether it applies to the mental syllogism conducted in the mind or the uttered syllogism expressed in words. At stake is the nature of logical entailment: does the uttered syllogism necessarily produce a conclusion in reality, or only in knowledge? The critique sharpens our understanding of what a syllogism truly is in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of qiyas
Find where the author mentions 'al-qiyas al-'aqli' (mental) and 'al-qiyas al-malfuz' (uttered). Note that the discussion revolves around which one should be the definiendum in the definition of syllogism.
The passage opens with 'العقلي أو الملفوظ'.
2
Understand al-Dasuqi's initial point
Al-Dasuqi states that the uttered syllogism as a mere utterance does not entail another utterance; it only indicates a mental meaning. Therefore, if the definition is for the uttered, the conclusion must still be mental.
Key phrase: 'لا يستلزم لفظاً آخَر'.
3
Grasp al-'Attar's objection about entailment
Al-'Attar argues that the entailment in the definition must be real (bi hasab nafs al-amr), not epistemic. The uttered syllogism entails its conclusion only epistemically (through knowledge), not ontologically. Hence the definition cannot apply to the uttered syllogism.
Look for 'فيه نظر' and the distinction between اللزوم بحسب نفس الأمر and اللزوم العلمي.
4
Recognize the resolution
The author concludes that the definition should be for the mental syllogism, even though many commentators treat it as interchangeable. The composite view (uttered+mental) is dismissed as invalid.
Final sentence: 'والأولى أن يجعل التعريف للقياس العقلي'.

فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
العقلي أو الملفوظ - جنس يشمل القياس وغيره
الدسوقي
لا يستلزم لفظاً آخَر، بل من حيثُ إنَّه دال على معنى معقول، لكن القياس المعقول كافٍ في تحصيل المطالب البرهانيَّةِ ؛ كانَ المراد بالقول الأوَّل، والقضايا؛ الأمور المعقولة، وإنْ كَانَ المعرَّفُ هو الملفوظ ؛ كانَ المراد بها الأمور الملفوظة، وعلى كلا التقديرين يُراد بالقول الأول الآخر المعقول؛ لعدم لزوم التلفظ بالقول الملفوظ لشيء لا من القولِ الملفوظ وَلَا مِنَ القول.
(قَوْلُهُ : الْعَقْلِيُّ) أي : كما إذا أجريت على قلبك العالم متغيّر، وكلُّ متغيّر حادث . (وقَوْلُهُ : أو الملفوظ) أي : كما إذا تلفظت بما ذكر.
المطار
كِلَا التَّقديريْنِ يُراد بالقولِ الأَوَّلِ : الآخَرُ المعقولُ ؛ لعدم لزوم التلفظ بالقول الملفوظ لشيءٍ؛ لَا مِنَ القولِ الملفوظ وَلَا مِنَ القولِ المعقول، بل إنَّما يلزمُ القول المعقول من القولِ المعقول بلا واسطةٍ وَمِنَ القولِ الملفوظ بواسطةٍ ؛ دلالته على المعقول بالنَّظر إلى العالم بالوضع، لأنَّ التَّلفظَ يَستلزم تعقُل المعنى بالنسبة للعالم بالوضع، وتَعقُلُ المعنى على تقدير تسليمه يستلزم النتيجةَ ، وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّ المرادَ بِاللُّزوم هَهُنا هو اللُّزوم بحسب نفس الأمر لا اللُّزوم العلمي، والقولُ الملفوظ المؤلَّفَ مِنَ القضايا الملفوظة؛ لَا يَستلزم مدلولَهُ بحسب نفس الأمرِ حتّى يستلزم القولَ الآخَرَ بحسب نفس الأمرِ بواسطة، بل إنَّما يَستلزمه بحسب العلم لدلالته عليه، وأيضاً القولُ الملفوظ يستلزم مدلوله بحسب العلم التّصوُّري، ومَدلُولُهُ إِنَّما يستلزم القول الآخَرَ باعتبار العلم التّصديقي، وأيضاً قد يؤدى المعقولُ بألفاظ مجازيَّةٍ، والمجاز ليس موضوعاً، إلا أنْ يُرادَ بِالوضع : مَا يشملُ النَّوعيَّ والتَّخصُّصي، والأَوْلَى أن يجعل التعريف للقياس العقليّ ؛ وإنْ كانَ المتبادرُ مِن عبارةِ الشَّارحِ كَالمصنفِ في شرح الرسالة والقطبِ الرَّازي أنَّ المراد العقلي أو اللفظي على البدل في المعرَّفِ والمعرفِ وفي بعض الحواشي، وأمَّا احتمالُ المركَبِ مِنَ الملفوظ والمعقول؛ فَلَمْ يقع في كلامِهِم التّصريح باعتبارِهِ ، فإن صح اعتبارُهُ فنقولُ : إِنَّ عبارةَ المصنِّف تتنزَّلُ عليه ا . هـ. وهذا كلام ليسَ لهُ اعتبار عندَ ذَوي الأنظار .
Chapter on Syllogism: Mental or Uttered - A genus that includes syllogism and other things. Al-Dasuqi: It does not entail another utterance, but only insofar as it indicates a mental meaning. Yet the mental syllogism is sufficient for acquiring demonstrative claims. If the defined is the mental, then the first saying and the propositions are understood as mental matters; if the defined is the uttered, then they are understood as uttered matters. And in both estimations, the first saying is intended to be the other mental [saying], because the uttered saying does not entail anything by itself—neither from the uttered saying nor from the mental saying. (His statement: 'the mental' i.e., as when you run in your heart: 'The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated.' And his statement: 'or the uttered' i.e., as when you utter what was mentioned.) Al-'Attar: In both estimations, the first saying is intended to be the other mental [saying] because the uttered saying does not entail anything by itself—neither from the uttered saying nor from the mental saying. Rather, the mental saying is entailed from the mental saying directly, and from the uttered saying indirectly: by its indication of the mental meaning, considering the knower of the convention (al-wad'), because the utterance entails the comprehension of the meaning for the knower of the convention, and comprehension of the meaning, assuming its acceptance, entails the conclusion. But this is questionable because the entailment intended here is according to reality (bi hasab nafs al-amr), not epistemic entailment. The uttered saying composed of uttered propositions does not entail its referent according to reality such that it would entail another saying according to reality indirectly; rather, it entails it epistemically because it indicates it. Also, the uttered saying entails its referent by conceptual knowledge, and its referent entails the other saying by verificatory knowledge. Also, the mental meaning may be expressed by metaphorical expressions, and metaphor is not conventional, unless by 'convention' is meant including both generic and particular types. The best course is to make the definition for the mental syllogism, even if the apparent meaning from the commentator's and the author's words in Sharh al-Risala and al-Qutb al-Razi is that the mental or uttered is meant interchangeably in the defined and the definer. As for the possibility of a composite of uttered and mental, it is not explicitly considered in their words. If it is valid, we say that the author's expression accommodates it. End. And this speech carries no weight among those with insight.
Source pages p. 763

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the definition of qiyas, specifically whether it applies to mental or uttered syllogism. It begins with a chapter heading. Al-Dasuqi notes that the uttered syllogism as utterance does not entail another utterance; only as indicator of a mental meaning does it entail. Thus if the defined is mental, the terms refer to mental matters; if uttered, to uttered matters. But in both cases, the 'first saying' (the conclusion) is mental because the uttered saying does not entail anything directly. Al-'Attar elaborates: the uttered saying entails the mental conclusion only indirectly, through comprehension of meaning. However, he criticizes this view: the entailment (luzum) in the definition is real (bi hasab nafs al-amr), not epistemic. An uttered proposition does not naturally cause another proposition to exist; rather, it indicates a meaning, and that meaning is then known. The entailment is epistemic, not ontological. Moreover, mental meanings can be expressed metaphorically, which are not conventional, so the definition better suits mental syllogism. The passage concludes that the common understanding of the commentators is that the definition applies to both mental and uttered interchangeably, but the more precise view is to restrict it to the mental. The final remark dismisses the idea of a composite definition as invalid.

Key Terms
اللزوم
الوضع
القياس العقلي

A) Because the entailment is epistemic, not real (bi hasab nafs al-amr).
B) Because the uttered syllogism is not composed of propositions.
C) Because the mental syllogism cannot be expressed in words.
D) Because the conclusion must be uttered as well.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas must be for the mental syllogism because only it provides real (ontological) entailment, while uttered syllogism gives only epistemic entailment through knowledge of meaning.

Understanding the Definition of Qiyas and Its Exclusions
Definition of qiyas with exclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which statements qualify as qiyas according to the definition and which are excluded, by applying the conditions of being composed of premises and entailing a conclusion.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a multi-premise argument, not any statement that entails something.

In this lesson, we dive into the precise definition of qiyas (syllogism) as given by the logicians. You will learn that not every statement that implies another is a syllogism; it must be composed of multiple premises. We will also see what kinds of statements are excluded, such as simple propositions and analogical reasoning, and why they fall outside the scope of proper logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the definition: qiyas is a composed statement (qawl) from premises (qadaya) that entails another statement (the conclusion) by itself.
The definition includes both mental and uttered forms.
2
Distinguish mental and uttered
Understand that the conclusion is always mental; uttered qiyas entails the conclusion only by indicating the mental qiyas.
3
Examine the condition 'composed of premises'
This excludes simple propositions that entail their converse or the converse of their contradictory, because they are not composed of multiple premises.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' entails 'Some animal is human', but it is one statement, not a syllogism.
4
Recognize other exclusions
The definition also excludes compound propositions (e.g., conditionals), induction (istiqra'), analogy (tamthil), and equality syllogism.
Induction follows particulars of a universal; analogy attaches a branch to a root due to a shared attribute.
5
Apply the definition to examples
Test a given argument: does it consist of at least two premises leading to a conclusion? If not, it is not a qiyas.

(قَوْلُهُ : الْعَقْلِيُّ) أي : كما إذا أجريت على قلبك العالم متغيّر، وكلُّ متغيّر حادث . (وقَوْلُهُ : أو الملفوظ) أي : كما إذا تلفظت بما ذكر.
