فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : يُخْرِجُ الاِسْتِقْراءَ الْغَيْرَ (التَّامّ أي : يخرجُ القياس المستند للاستقراء الغير النَّام الَّذي هو إجراء حكم أكثر الجزئيات على الكلّيّ، وأمَّا الثام: فهو إجراء حكم جميع الجزئيات مضبوطة على الكلي. انتهى ياسين.
العطار
القياس؛ لِكُونِ جزئياتِهِ مَضبوطةٌ ، فيكونُ مُفيداً لليقين كانحصار جزئيات العنصر الماء والنَّارِ والهواء والتُّرابِ، فإذا قِيلَ : كُلُّ عنصر متحيز؛ لا يوجد جزئي مِنَ الأفراد إلا وهذا الحكمُ ثابت له، وإنَّما خرجَ الاستقراءُ غيرُ التَّامّ والتَّمثيلُ؛ لأنَّ المراد باللُّزوم هو اللُّزوم بحسب نفس الأمر بالنَّظر إلى صورة القول المؤلف، ونتيجة الاستقراء والتَّمثيل ليست لازمةٌ لهما بهذا المعنى وإن كانت لازمة لهما بحسب العلمِ الظَّنّي مُطلقاً، وبحسب نفس الأمر في بعض المواد، وذلكَ لِتَخَلُّفِ نتيجتهما بحسب نفس الأمر عن صورتيهما في بعض المواد كَمَا في قولك : أكثر الحيواناتِ يُحرّكُ فَكَهُ الأسفل عند المضغ ، فكُلُّ حيوان يحرّك فَكَّهُ الأسفل عندَ المضغ ؛ لأنَّهُ وَإِنْ تحقَّقَ هَهُنا اللُّزومُ الظَّنّي ؛ لكن قد تخلَّفَ اللُّزوم بحسب نفسِ الأمر ؛ لعدم جريان هذا الحُكْمِ في التّمساح ، قال العصام: فإنْ قُلْتَ: الاستقراء والتَّمثيلُ كَقِياس المساواة يستلزمانِ النَّتيجةَ بواسطة مُقدِّمةٍ غريبةٍ، أَمَّا الْأَوَّلُ : فَلِأَنَّ كون الإنسان والفرس والحمار إلى غير ذلكَ مُحرّكاً فَكَهُ الأسفل عند المضغ؛ يستلزم كُلَّ حيوان يُحرّكُ فَكَهُ الأسفل عند المضغ بواسطةٍ أَنَّ مَا لَمْ يُستقرأْ مِنْ أنواع الحيوانِ مثلُ مَا اسْتُقْرِئَ منه، وأمَّا الثَّاني : فلأنَّ قولَنَا : العالم كَالبيت في التأليف، فهو حادث يستلزمُ النَّتيجةَ بواسطة قولِنَا : كُلُّ مؤلَّف حادث، فيخرجان بقوله: لزمَ عنهُمَا ، بلْ بِقولِهِ : لِذَاتِهِ؛ قُلْتُ : ليس الاستقراء والتمثيل بناء على هاتين المقدِّمتين بخلافِ قياس المساواة، إذْ مِن مُجرَّدِ مُلاحظةِ حالِ الأكثر يحصلُ الظَّنُّ بحالِ الكُلِّيِّ في الاستقراءِ، وَمِنْ مُجرَّدِ مُلاحظةِ مُشاركة العالم معَ البيتِ في التَّأليفِ يحصلُ الظَّنِّ بحال العالم، لا يُقالُ متى انتفى اللُّزوم فيهما : كيف اندرجا في الدليل المعرَّفِ بما يلزمُ مِنَ العلم به العلم بشيءٍ آخر؟ لأنَّا نقولُ : بجوزُ أنْ يتخلَّفَ الشَّيْءُ الآخَرُ معَ لزوم علمه للعلم بشيءٍ آخَرَ ؛ لأنَّ المعلوم قد يتخلَّفُ عَنِ العلم، وأجابَ عنه السَّيِّدُ بأنَّ المرادَ مِنَ اللَّزوم في تعريفِ الدَّليل ؛ المناسبة المصححة للانتقال. 
وقوله : «لذاته» يُخرجُ الدسوقي وقولُهُ: الَّذي هو إجراء... إلخ، فيه تسامح؛ لأنَّ هذا الحكم مطلوبٌ مِنَ الاستقراء لا نفْسه ، فكأنَّهم أرادوا أنَّ إثبات المطلوب بالاستقراء هو حكم على كلّي . والصَّحيحُ : أنَّه تصفُحُ أمور جزئية ليحكم بحكمها على أمرٍ يشتمل على تلك الجزئيات، كذا ذكر بعض الفضلاء، ويؤيِّدُهُ ما سيأتي في المتن. مثال قياس الاستقراء كما إذا قُلْتَ: الفرسُ حيوان يحرّكُ فَكَهُ الأسفل عند المضغ، والحمار حيوان يحرّك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ ... وهَلُمَّ جرًا، فَإِنَّ هذا القياس مؤلَّفٌ من قولين، ولكن لا يلزم منه قول آخرُ ، وهو أنَّ كلَّ حيوان يحرّك فكَّهُ الأسفل عندَ المضغ ؛ لإمكانِ التَّخلُفِ عقلاً، وقد حُكي لنا أنَّ التمساح إنَّما يحرّلُ فكَّهُ الأعلى. ومثال قياس التمثيل ؛ أي : التشبيه : النَّبيذُ كَالخمر بجامع الإسكار، فهذا قول مؤلّفٌ مِن قولين؛ لأنَّ قولَه : بجامع الإسكار، خبر مبتدأ محذوف؛ أي: وهو متلبس بجامع الإسكار، ولكن لا يلزمُ منه أنَّ النَّبياً حرام عقلاً ؛ لإمكان أنْ تكون الحرمة لعلَّةٍ أخرى غيرِ هذه، أمَّا إذا لم نَقُلْ : (إِنَّ) بجامع الإسكار) خبراً لمحذوف ؛ فإنَّهُ يكون خارجاً بقولِهِ : مؤلَّفٌ مِن قَولَيْن، وحينئذ ؛ فَلَا يُسمى الاستقراء والتمثيل قياساً ؛ 
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : وَقَوْلُهُ : لِذَاتِهِ يُخْرِجُ ... إِلَخ) إذ المعنى أنْ يكونَ اللَّازِمُ لِذاتِ القولِ المؤلَّفِ ؛ أي: لا يكونُ بواسطة مقدّمةٍ غريبةٍ؛ إمَّا غيرِ لازمة لإحدى المقدمتين؛ وَهِيَ الأجنبية، أو لازمة لإحداهما ؛ وهي في قوَّةِ المذكورة، والأوَّلُ كَمَا في قياس المساواة، والثاني كَقولنا : جزء الجوهر يوجب ارتفاعُه ارتفاع الجوهر، وكلَّ مَا ليس بجوهرٍ لا يُوجب ارتفاعُهُ ارتفاع الجوهر، فإنَّهُ يلزمُ منهُما أنَّ جزء الجوهر
Chapter: On Syllogism. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'It excludes incomplete induction' means: it excludes the syllogism based on incomplete induction, which is applying the ruling of most particulars to the universal. As for complete induction: it is applying the ruling of all particulars, enumerated, to the universal. End of Yasin. Al-Attar: The syllogism, because its particulars are enumerated, thus it is useful for certainty, like the enumeration of the particulars of the elements: water, fire, air, and earth. So if it is said: 'Every element is physically extended; there is no individual among the individuals except that this ruling is established for it.' And only incomplete induction and analogy are excluded because what is meant by necessity is the necessity in reality with respect to the form of the composed statement. The conclusion of induction and analogy is not necessary for them in this sense, even if it is necessary for them in terms of probabilistic knowledge absolutely, and in reality in some materials. This is because their conclusion in reality sometimes differs from their form in some materials, as in your saying: 'Most animals move their lower jaw when chewing.' Therefore, every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing. Because even if probabilistic necessity is realized here, necessity in reality has been contradicted, since this ruling does not apply to the crocodile. Al-Isam said: If you say: 'Induction and analogy, like the syllogism of equality, entail the conclusion by means of an extraneous premise. As for the first: because the fact that the human, horse, donkey, etc. move their lower jaw when chewing entails that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, by means of the premise that what has not been induced from the types of animals is like what has been induced from them. As for the second: because our saying: 'The world is like a house in composition, so it is originated' entails the conclusion by means of our saying: 'Every composite is originated.' Thus both are excluded by his statement: 'entails from them' – rather, by his statement: 'by itself.' I say: Induction and analogy are not based on these two premises, unlike the syllogism of equality, because from merely observing the state of the majority, probability arises regarding the state of the universal in induction, and from merely observing the world's sharing with the house in composition, probability arises regarding the state of the world. It cannot be said: 'When necessity is absent in them, how are they included in the evidence defined as: what entails by knowledge of it knowledge of something else?' Because we say: 'It is possible that the other thing may differ while knowledge of it entails knowledge of something else, because the known may differ from the knowledge.' And al-Sayyid answered that what is meant by necessity in the definition of evidence is the appropriate suitability for transition. And his statement: 'by itself' excludes... Al-Dasuqi: And his statement: 'which is applying...' etc. contains leniency, because this ruling is sought from induction, not from itself. It is as if they meant that establishing the sought by induction is a ruling on the universal. The correct is that it is examining particular matters to judge by their ruling on something that encompasses those particulars. As mentioned by some virtuous scholars, and it is supported by what will come in the text. Example of the induction syllogism: when you say: 'The horse is an animal that moves its lower jaw when chewing, and the donkey is an animal that moves its lower jaw when chewing...' and so on. For this syllogism is composed of two statements, but another statement does not follow from it, namely that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because it is intellectually possible for it to be contradicted. It has been reported to us that the crocodile moves its upper jaw. Example of the analogy syllogism – i.e., comparison: 'Nabidh is like wine due to the shared attribute of intoxication.' This is a statement composed of two statements, because his saying: 'due to the shared attribute of intoxication' is the predicate of a suppressed subject, i.e., and it is characterized by the shared attribute of intoxication. But it does not entail that nabidh is prohibited intellectually, because it is possible that prohibition is due to another cause besides this. But if we do not say: 'due to the shared attribute of intoxication' as the predicate of a suppressed subject, then it falls outside by his statement: 'composed of two statements'. In that case, induction and analogy are not called syllogism. Al-Attar: (His statement and his statement: 'by himself' excludes... etc.) For the meaning is that the necessary should be by virtue of the composed statement itself, i.e., not by means of an extraneous premise. Either it is not necessary for either of the two premises – and it is foreign – or it is necessary for one of them, and it is in the strength of the mentioned. The first is like the syllogism of equality, and the second like our saying: 'A part of a substance necessitates that its removal necessitates the removal of the substance, and everything that is not a substance does not necessitate that its removal necessitates the removal of the substance.' For from these two it follows that the part of the substance...
Source pages p. 767

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of qiyas (syllogism) from the matn. It first clarifies that qiyas, in its strict sense, requires complete enumeration of all particulars under a universal, yielding certainty. Incomplete induction (istiqra' ghayr tam) and analogy (tamthil) are excluded because they do not produce necessary conclusions in reality, even if they yield probabilistic knowledge. The example of 'most animals move their lower jaw' leading to the false universal (crocodile) illustrates the failure of incomplete induction. Similarly, the analogy between nabidh and wine based on intoxication does not guarantee the ruling of prohibition because the cause could be specific to wine. The passage also responds to an objection that induction and analogy, like the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat), could be made to entail conclusions via an extraneous premise. The response is that in induction and analogy, the conclusion is based on mere observation of the majority or resemblance, not on a logical middle term, so they lack the necessity required for qiyas. The final part discusses the phrase 'lizatihi' (by itself), stating that the conclusion must follow from the composed statement itself, not via an extraneous premise. Examples include the syllogism of equality (which uses an extraneous premise) and a syllogism about substance parts. The overall aim is to delimit the precise definition of qiyas in the logical tradition, distinguishing it from other forms of reasoning.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Definition and Structure of Qiyas al-Musawah
Definition and concrete example of qiyas al-musawah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two propositions and the transitive structure in a qiyas al-musawah and construct one using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
A syllogism of equality chains two equalities to link extremes through a common middle.

This passage introduces a specific type of syllogism called qiyas al-musawah, where the middle term appears as the object of the predicate in the first premise and as the subject in the second. Understanding its structure helps you see how equality relations are transmitted. The author gives a formal example using letters (a, b, c) and then unpacks it with concrete names (Zayd, Amr, Bakr) familiar from Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Find the two statements that compose the syllogism. They are: '(a) is equal to (b)' and '(b) is equal to (c)'.
Each statement is a proposition (qadiyya).
2
Locate the object of the predicate in the first proposition
In '(a) is equal to (b)', the predicate is 'equal to (b)'. Its object is '(b)' (the prepositional phrase's noun). This object becomes the subject of the second proposition.
The text calls this 'muta'allaq al-mahmul'.
3
Confirm the middle term transitivity
The middle term '(b)' appears in both propositions: first as object, then as subject. This creates a chain: (a) relates to (b), and (b) relates to (c), implying (a) relates to (c).
This is the essence of equality syllogism.
4
Map letters to concrete Islamic names
The commentary provides an example: (a)=Zayd, (b)=Amr, (c)=Bakr. Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr, so Zayd is equal to Bakr.
These names are common in fiqh scenarios.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ قياس المساواة، وهو ما يتركَّب من قضيَّتين، متعلّق محمول أولاهما يكون موضوع الأخرى، كقولنا : (أ) مساو ل (ب)، و(ب) مساو لـ(ج)، الدسوقي أي بالإطلاقِ، وإِنَّما يُسمَّى قياساً بالتَّقييد، فيقالُ : قياس الاستقراء، وقياس التمثيل، وكذا قياس المساواة، وَمَا بعدَهُ لا يُقالُ فيهِ قياس بالإطلاق . (قَوْلُهُ : الغَيْرُ الثَّام) أي : وأمَّا النَّامُ؛ فهو استقراء جميعها . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ مَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْن ... إلخ) أي : سواء (۱) عبر فيه بالمساواة أوْ لَا . (قَوْلُهُ : كَقَوْلِنَا : أ مُسَاوِلِ ب... إلخ) أي : زيد مساءٍ لعمرو، وعمرو مُساءٍ لبكر ، فـ (أ) عبارةٌ عن زيدٍ مثلاً ، و (ب) عبارةٌ عن عمرو مثلاً ، و (ج) عبارة عن بكر . (قَوْلُهُ: مُسَاءٍ) هذا هو المحمول. وقوله : ( لـ (ب) هو متعلّق المحمول، وقوله : (ب) بعض ذلك المتعلّق . العطار جوهر بواسطة عكس نقيض المقدّمةِ الثَّانيةِ وهو قولنا : كُلُّ مَا يوجب ارتفاعُهُ ارتفاع الجوهرِ فهو جوهر ؛ مع أنَّه ليس بقياس بالنسبة إلى هذه القضيَّةِ اللازمة، لا يُقالُ : هذا قياسٌ مِنَ الشَّكل الثَّاني ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : لم يتحقَّق فيه شرطُهُ، الاختلاف وهو بالكيف، قال عبد الحكيم: وَلَا تتوهَّمْ أنَّ الأشكال الثلاثةَ تخرج عن التعريف لإحتياجها إلى مقدِّماتٍ غريبةٍ يثبتُ بِهِ إنتاجها ؛ لأنَّ تلك المقدِّماتِ واسطة في الإثباتِ لا في الثَّبوتِ، والمنفي في التّعريف هوَ الثَّاني. (قَوْلُهُ : قِيَاسَ الْمُسَاوَاةِ) تَسميةٌ لِلكُلّيّ باعتبارِ ما يوجد في بعض أفرادِهِ، وإنَّما أخرجُوا قياس المساواة عَن التَّعريف ؛ لعدم إنتاجِهِ مُطَّرداً، واختلافِهِ بحسب اختلافِ المواد، كما أخرجُوا الضُّروبَ العقيمةً ؛ لعدم إطْرادِ نتائجها واختلافها في الإنتاج ؛ قالَهُ عبد الحكيم . Section: On Syllogism. The syllogism of equality, and it is what is composed of two propositions, the object of the predicate of the first one becomes the subject of the other, like our statement: (a) is equal to (b), and (b) is equal to (c). Al-Dasuqi: That is, absolutely, and it is only called syllogism with restriction, so it is said: inductive syllogism, analogical syllogism, and likewise syllogism of equality, and what comes after it is not called syllogism absolutely. (His statement: the other eighth) i.e., and as for the mature, it is the induction of all of them. (His statement: and it is what is composed of two propositions... etc.) i.e., whether (1) it is expressed with equality or not. (His statement: like our statement: a is equal to b... etc.) i.e., Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr, so (a) is an expression for Zayd for example, and (b) is an expression for Amr for example, and (c) is an expression for Bakr. (His statement: equal) this is the predicate. And his statement: to (b) is the object of the predicate, and his statement: (b) is part of that object. Al-Attar: A substance by means of contrapositive of the second premise, which is our statement: Everything whose destruction necessitates the destruction of substance is a substance, even though it is not a syllogism with respect to this necessary proposition, it is not said: this is a syllogism of the second figure, because we say: its condition, difference which is by quality, is not realized. Abd al-Hakim said: And do not imagine that the three figures fall outside the definition due to their need for foreign premises to prove their productivity, because those premises are intermediaries in proof, not in existence, and what is negated in the definition is the second. (His statement: syllogism of equality) is a naming of the universal by consideration of what exists in some of its individuals, and they only excluded the syllogism of equality from the definition because of its lack of consistent production and its variation according to variation of matters, just as they excluded the sterile moods due to lack of consistency of their results and their variation in production; said Abd al-Hakim. (His statement: object of the predicate of the first one... etc.) i.e., part of the object, for the object is the sum of the preposition and the noun governed by it, and that which is made subject is the governed noun only, or the intended is the object of operation, and the preposition is an object of connection indicating direction, because it signifies the verb's going to the governed noun. (1) (His statement: whether... etc.) i.e., it is a naming of the universal by the name of a particular of its particulars. h. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawah). It begins by defining it as a compound of two propositions where the object of the predicate of the first becomes the subject of the second. The example given is: (a) is equal to (b), and (b) is equal to (c). Al-Dasuqi immediately notes that this is not called absolute syllogism; rather, it is restricted and named 'syllogism of equality' by way of qualification. The commentary then explains the example in concrete terms: Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr, so (a) represents Zayd, (b) Amr, and (c) Bakr. The word 'equal' is the predicate, and the phrase 'to (b)' is its object. Al-Attar then discusses a subtle point: a substance can be inferred via contrapositive of the second premise, but this does not constitute a syllogism of the second figure because the condition of difference in quality is not met. Abd al-Hakim is quoted to clarify that the three figures are not excluded from the definition of qiyas because their need for foreign premises is only for proof, not for the essential entailment. The passage concludes by explaining why qiyas al-musawah is excluded from the definition: it does not produce conclusions consistently and varies with the subject matter. Al-Sharnubi's footnote adds that 'syllogism of equality' is a general term applied to a specific instance, like naming the universal after a particular.

قضية

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-musawah is a transitive chain of equalities where the middle term links the extremes.

Construct a qiyas al-musawah using Islamic names: e.g., 'Zayd is equal to Amr in strength', 'Amr is equal to Khalid in strength'. Write the conclusion in syllogistic form.

Identify the two propositions and the object of the predicate in the following: 'The blood money for a woman is half that of a man (a=b). The blood money for a man is twice that of a cow (b=c). What conclusion follows? Translate into the structure of qiyas al-musawah.'

The Syllogism

Why Qiyas al-Musawah is Not an Absolute Syllogism
Explanation of why qiyas al-musawah is excluded from absolute syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical reasons for excluding qiyas al-musawah from absolute syllogism, as presented in the commentary.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-musawah is excluded because its conclusions are not consistent across all subject matters.

Although qiyas al-musawah has a clear structure, it is not considered an absolute syllogism because its validity depends on the material (the specific equality relation). The passage explains that, like sterile moods in categorical logic, it lacks universal productivity. The commentary by Abd al-Hakim and others clarifies that the definition of absolute qiyas requires consistent production, which qiyas al-musawah fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about qiyas al-musawah
The passage states that qiyas al-musawah is excluded from the definition of absolute qiyas (al-qiyas bi'l-itlaq).
Al-Dasuqi says it is only called syllogism with restriction.
2
Find the reason for exclusion
The reason given is 'lack of consistent production' (adam itjarad al-intaj) and variation according to materials (ikhtilaf bihasab ikhtilaf al-mawad).
Compare with 'sterile moods' (al-durub al-aqima) which are also excluded.
3
Examine the commentary on the three figures
Abd al-Hakim warns not to think the three figures are excluded because they need foreign premises; those are only intermediaries in proof, not in the essential entailment (al-thubut).
The exclusion of qiyas al-musawah is different.
4
Understand the analogy with sterile moods
Just as sterile moods do not always produce a valid conclusion, qiyas al-musawah does not always lead to a true conclusion across different equality relations.
The commentary by Abd al-Hakim explicitly makes this comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ قياس المساواة، وهو ما يتركَّب من قضيَّتين، متعلّق محمول أولاهما يكون موضوع الأخرى، كقولنا : (أ) مساو ل (ب)، و(ب) مساو لـ(ج)، الدسوقي أي بالإطلاقِ، وإِنَّما يُسمَّى قياساً بالتَّقييد، فيقالُ : قياس الاستقراء، وقياس التمثيل، وكذا قياس المساواة، وَمَا بعدَهُ لا يُقالُ فيهِ قياس بالإطلاق . (قَوْلُهُ : الغَيْرُ الثَّام) أي : وأمَّا النَّامُ؛ فهو استقراء جميعها . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ مَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْن ... إلخ) أي : سواء (۱) عبر فيه بالمساواة أوْ لَا . (قَوْلُهُ: مُسَاءٍ) هذا هو المحمول. وقوله : ( لـ (ب) هو متعلّق المحمول، وقوله : (ب) بعض ذلك المتعلّق . العطار جوهر بواسطة عكس نقيض المقدّمةِ الثَّانيةِ وهو قولنا : كُلُّ مَا يوجب ارتفاعُهُ ارتفاع الجوهرِ فهو جوهر ؛ مع أنَّه ليس بقياس بالنسبة إلى هذه القضيَّةِ اللازمة، لا يُقالُ : هذا قياسٌ مِنَ الشَّكل الثَّاني ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : لم يتحقَّق فيه شرطُهُ، الاختلاف وهو بالكيف، قال عبد الحكيم: وَلَا تتوهَّمْ أنَّ الأشكال الثلاثةَ تخرج عن التعريف لإحتياجها إلى مقدِّماتٍ غريبةٍ يثبتُ بِهِ إنتاجها ؛ لأنَّ تلك المقدِّماتِ واسطة في الإثباتِ لا في الثَّبوتِ، والمنفي في التّعريف هوَ الثَّاني. (قَوْلُهُ : قِيَاسَ الْمُسَاوَاةِ) تَسميةٌ لِلكُلّيّ باعتبارِ ما يوجد في بعض أفرادِهِ، وإنَّما أخرجُوا قياس المساواة عَن التَّعريف ؛ لعدم إنتاجِهِ مُطَّرداً، واختلافِهِ بحسب اختلافِ المواد، كما أخرجُوا الضُّروبَ العقيمةً ؛ لعدم إطْرادِ نتائجها واختلافها في الإنتاج ؛ قالَهُ عبد الحكيم . (1) (قَوْلُهُ : سواء ... إلخ) أي : فهو من تسمية الكُلّي باسم جزئي من جزئياته. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Section: On Syllogism. The syllogism of equality, and it is what is composed of two propositions, the object of the predicate of the first one becomes the subject of the other, like our statement: (a) is equal to (b), and (b) is equal to (c). Al-Dasuqi: That is, absolutely, and it is only called syllogism with restriction, so it is said: inductive syllogism, analogical syllogism, and likewise syllogism of equality, and what comes after it is not called syllogism absolutely. (His statement: the other eighth) i.e., and as for the mature, it is the induction of all of them. (His statement: and it is what is composed of two propositions... etc.) i.e., whether (1) it is expressed with equality or not. (His statement: like our statement: a is equal to b... etc.) i.e., Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr, so (a) is an expression for Zayd for example, and (b) is an expression for Amr for example, and (c) is an expression for Bakr. (His statement: equal) this is the predicate. And his statement: to (b) is the object of the predicate, and his statement: (b) is part of that object. Al-Attar: A substance by means of contrapositive of the second premise, which is our statement: Everything whose destruction necessitates the destruction of substance is a substance, even though it is not a syllogism with respect to this necessary proposition, it is not said: this is a syllogism of the second figure, because we say: its condition, difference which is by quality, is not realized. Abd al-Hakim said: And do not imagine that the three figures fall outside the definition due to their need for foreign premises to prove their productivity, because those premises are intermediaries in proof, not in existence, and what is negated in the definition is the second. (His statement: syllogism of equality) is a naming of the universal by consideration of what exists in some of its individuals, and they only excluded the syllogism of equality from the definition because of its lack of consistent production and its variation according to variation of matters, just as they excluded the sterile moods due to lack of consistency of their results and their variation in production; said Abd al-Hakim. (His statement: object of the predicate of the first one... etc.) i.e., part of the object, for the object is the sum of the preposition and the noun governed by it, and that which is made subject is the governed noun only, or the intended is the object of operation, and the preposition is an object of connection indicating direction, because it signifies the verb's going to the governed noun. (1) (His statement: whether... etc.) i.e., it is a naming of the universal by the name of a particular of its particulars. h. al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawah). It begins by defining it as a compound of two propositions where the object of the predicate of the first becomes the subject of the second. The example given is: (a) is equal to (b), and (b) is equal to (c). Al-Dasuqi immediately notes that this is not called absolute syllogism; rather, it is restricted and named 'syllogism of equality' by way of qualification. The commentary then explains the example in concrete terms: Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr, so (a) represents Zayd, (b) Amr, and (c) Bakr. The word 'equal' is the predicate, and the phrase 'to (b)' is its object. Al-Attar then discusses a subtle point: a substance can be inferred via contrapositive of the second premise, but this does not constitute a syllogism of the second figure because the condition of difference in quality is not met. Abd al-Hakim is quoted to clarify that the three figures are not excluded from the definition of qiyas because their need for foreign premises is only for proof, not for the essential entailment. The passage concludes by explaining why qiyas al-musawah is excluded from the definition: it does not produce conclusions consistently and varies with the subject matter. Al-Sharnubi's footnote adds that 'syllogism of equality' is a general term applied to a specific instance, like naming the universal after a particular.

Key Terms
قياس المساواة
الإنتاج المطرد
الضروب العقيمة

According to Abd al-Hakim, why is qiyas al-musawah excluded from absolute qiyas?
A) It has only two premises.
B) It uses foreign premises.
C) It does not produce consistent conclusions and varies by material.
D) It is a type of inductive syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-musawah is excluded from absolute qiyas because its conclusions are not universally consistent; validity depends on the material.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why is the first a valid qiyas al-musawah for age but the second is not necessarily valid for fatherhood? Use the commentary's criterion of consistent production.

The Syllogism

Why Qiyas al-Musawat is Excluded from the Definition of Syllogism
Commentators explain why qiyas al-musawat is excluded from the definition of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test any argument for the property of spontaneity (li dhatihi) and determine whether it qualifies as a valid syllogism (qiyas) according to the logicians.

Core Idea
A true syllogism's conclusion follows spontaneously; qiyas al-musawat fails because it hides an extra premise.

In this lesson, we examine a boundary case: the so-called 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat). The logicians deliberately excluded it from the definition of qiyas because its conclusion does not follow directly from its two premises; it covertly relies on an additional axiom. Understanding why this exclusion is made sharpens your grasp of what the logicians meant by 'spontaneous' (li dhatihi) entailment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of qiyas al-musawat
The argument has two premises: A is equal to B, B is equal to C. The apparent conclusion is A is equal to C.
This form is called musawat because it uses equality.
2
Check if the conclusion follows spontaneously
Ask: Does the conclusion necessarily follow from these two premises alone? In this case, it does not, because the inference depends on a hidden rule: 'Everything equal to the equal of something is equal to that thing.'
Spontaneity (li dhatihi) means no external premise is needed.
3
Identify the foreign premise
The missing premise is a universal statement that links equality transitively. The commentators call it a muqaddima ajnabiyya (foreign premise) because its terms are different from those in the original premises.
This premise must be supplied for the argument to be valid.
4
Conclude the exclusion
Because the conclusion is not spontaneous, qiyas al-musawat is excluded from the definition of qiyas. This also leads to discussions about other borderline cases like those clarified through conversion ('aks al-mustawi) or contrapositive ('aks al-naqid).
The condition of spontaneity is rigorous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
قياس المساواة، وهو ما يتركَّب من قضيَّتين، متعلّق محمول أولاهما يكون موضوع الأخرى، كقولنا : (أ) مساو ل (ب)، و(ب) مساو لـ(ج)،
الدسوقي: أي بالإطلاقِ، وإِنَّما يُسمَّى قياساً بالتَّقييد، فيقالُ : قياس الاستقراء، وقياس التمثيل، وكذا قياس المساواة، وَمَا بعدَهُ لا يُقالُ فيهِ قياس بالإطلاق . (قَوْلُهُ : الغَيْرُ الثَّام) أي : وأمَّا النَّامُ؛ فهو استقراء جميعها . (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ مَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ قَضِيَّتَيْن ... إلخ) أي : سواء (۱) عبر فيه بالمساواة أوْ لَا . (قَوْلُهُ : كَقَوْلِنَا : أ مُسَاوِلِ ب... إلخ) أي : زيد مساءٍ لعمرو، وعمرو مُساءٍ لبكر ، فـ (أ) عبارةٌ عن زيدٍ مثلاً ، و (ب) عبارةٌ عن عمرو مثلاً ، و (ج) عبارة عن بكر . (قَوْلُهُ: مُسَاءٍ) هذا هو المحمول. وقوله : ( لـ (ب) هو متعلّق المحمول، وقوله : (ب) بعض ذلك المتعلّق .
العطار: جوهر بواسطة عكس نقيض المقدّمةِ الثَّانيةِ وهو قولنا : كُلُّ مَا يوجب ارتفاعُهُ ارتفاع الجوهرِ فهو جوهر ; مع أنَّه ليس بقياس بالنسبة إلى هذه القضيَّةِ اللازمة، لا يُقالُ : هذا قياسٌ مِنَ الشَّكل الثَّاني ; لأنَّا نقولُ : لم يتحقَّق فيه شرطُهُ، الاختلاف وهو بالكيف، قال عبد الحكيم: وَلَا تتوهَّمْ أنَّ الأشكال الثلاثةَ تخرج عن التعريف لإحتياجها إلى مقدِّماتٍ غريبةٍ يثبتُ بِهِ إنتاجها ; لأنَّ تلك المقدِّماتِ واسطة في الإثباتِ لا في الثَّبوتِ، والمنفي في التّعريف هوَ الثَّاني. (قَوْلُهُ : قِيَاسَ الْمُسَاوَاةِ) تَسميةٌ لِلكُلّيّ باعتبارِ ما يوجد في بعض أفرادِهِ، وإنَّما أخرجُوا قياس المساواة عَن التَّعريف ; لعدم إنتاجِهِ مُطَّرداً، واختلافِهِ بحسب اختلافِ المواد، كما أخرجُوا الضُّروبَ العقيمةً ; لعدم إطْرادِ نتائجها واختلافها في الإنتاج ; قالَهُ عبد الحكيم . (قَوْلُهُ: مُتَعَلِّقُ مَحْمُوْلِ أَوْلَاهُمَا ... إلخ) أي: بعضُ مُتعلّق، فإنَّ المتعلّق مجموع الجار والمجرور، والذي مُجعِلَ موضوعاً المجرور فقط، أو المرادُ مُتعلّق المعمولية، والجارُ مُتعلّق تَعَلَّقَ إفضاء ; لأنَّهُ يُفضي بمعنى العامل إلى المجرور . (1) (قَوْلُهُ : سواء ... إلخ) أي : فهو من تسمية الكُلّي باسم جزئي من جزئياته. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
فإنَّه يستلزم أن يكونَ (أ) مساوياً لـ ( ج ) , لكن لا لذاته، بل بواسطة مقدّمةٍ أنَّ كلَّ مساوي المساوي مساو.
الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسَاوِي) الأولى أنَّ كلَّ مُساوي المساوي لشيء; مُساءٍ لذلك الشَّيء , فَقولُه : (أَنَّ مُساوي); أي: كَـ زيد).
العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ) فَسَّروها بما تكون حدودها مغايرة لحدودِ مُقدِّماتِ القياس ; حتّى يدخل فيه القياسات المبيَّنةُ بطريقِ العكس المستوي، ويخرج المبين بطريقِ عكس النَّقيض، وسبب ذلكَ أنَّهم اعتقدوا وجوب تكثر الحدّ الأوسط، وهو حاصل في المبيَّن بالعكس المستوي دونَ عكس النَّقيض ودون قياس المساواة، وهذا الوجوبُ مِمَّا لَا يَقتضيهِ تَعريفُ القياس ; قالَهُ المصنِّفُ، فعلى هذا ; لا وجهَ لإخراج القياس المبيَّنِ بعكس النَّقيض، ويؤيِّدُهُ مَا قالَهُ شارحُ المطالعِ : لَا وجهَ لإخراج الأوَّلِ , يعني : القياس المبيَّنَ بعكس النَّقيض عن تعريف القياس، معَ أَنَّهُ مِنَ الطَّرُقِ الموصلةِ، وَلَا فرقَ بينَهُ وبينَ الدَّليل المستلزم بواسطة العكس المستوي ا.هـ. ولذلك قال عبد الحكيم : والفرق بين الاستلزام بواسطة العكس وبينه بواسطة عكس النَّقيض ; تحكُمْ لمْ يَظْهَرْ لي إلى الآنَ وَجهُهُ ا.هـ. قال العصام : وهناك أدلَّةٌ أُخرى تخرج بقيد لِذَاتِهِ، مثل أنْ يحكم بالأكبر على أعمّ مِمَّا حكم بِهِ على الأصغر، فَيُقالُ : زيدٌ إنسان، وكُلُّ حيوان ماش، فإنَّهُ ينتجُ بِلَا اشتباه زيد ماش، لكنَّه بواسطة مُقدِّمةٍ أزمةٍ لِلكُبرى، وَهِيَ كُلُّ إنسانٍ ,ماش، ومثلُ أنْ يحكم بالأكبر على ما يساوي ما حكم بِهِ على الأصغر نحو : زيد إنسان، وكُلُّ ناطق حيوان، ينتج : زيد حيوان، ومثلُ أَنْ يُسلَبَ الأكبرُ عَن جميع أغيارِ مَا سُلِبَ عن كُلِّ الأصغرِ فيُقالُ : لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الإِنسانِ بفرس، ولا شيء من غيرِ الفرس بِصمَّالٍ، ينتجُ : لا شيء مِنَ الإِنسانِ بِصمَّالٍ، لكنَّهُ بواسطة أنَّ قولَنا : لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس ; يستلزم قولنا : كُلّ إنسانٍ غيرُ الفرس، ولا يخفى أنّه لا وجه لإخراج تلك الأدلَّةِ عَن حد القياس، وَهِيَ مُفيدة لليقين . (قَوْلُهُ : هِيَ أَنَّ كُلَّ ... إِلَخ) أي: المقدمة الأجنبية في هذا المثالِ, إلخ, هذه لا مطلقاً .
Section: On Syllogism
Syllogism of equality: it is composed of two propositions, the predicate of the first being the subject of the other, such as our saying: A is equal to B, and B is equal to C.
Al-Dasuqi: That is, absolutely, and it is only called syllogism by restriction, so it is said: inductive syllogism, analogical syllogism, and likewise the syllogism of equality. What comes after is not called syllogism absolutely. His saying: 'the non-perfect' means: as for the perfect, it is the induction of all of them. His saying: 'and it is composed of two propositions... etc.' means: whether it is expressed with equality or not. His saying: 'like our saying: A is equal to B... etc.' means: Zayd is equal to Amr, and Amr is equal to Bakr. So A stands for Zayd for example, B for Amr, and C for Bakr. His saying: 'equal' is the predicate. His saying: 'to B' is the complement of the predicate, and his saying: 'B' is part of that complement.
Al-Attar: ... (commentary on essence using the inverse of the contrapositive of the second premise, etc.) ... He said: Do not imagine that the three figures are excluded from the definition because they need foreign premises to establish their productivity, for those premises are a means in proof not in the actual ground, and what is negated in the definition is the second. His saying: 'syllogism of equality' is a naming of the universal by what is found in some of its individuals. They only excluded the syllogism of equality from the definition because of its lack of consistent productivity and its variation according to the variation of matter, just as they excluded the sterile moods due to the inconsistency of their conclusions and their variation in production. This was said by Abd al-Hakim. His saying: 'the complement of the predicate of the first... etc.' means: part of the complement, for the complement is the sum of the preposition and its object, and what is made the subject is only the object, or it means the complement of the object of the verbal noun, and the preposition is a complement of kind of completion (ifda'), because it conveys the meaning of the agent to the object. (1) (His saying: 'whether... etc.') means: it is a naming of the universal by the name of a particular from among its particulars. End of al-Sharnubi.
For it (the syllogism of equality) implies that A is equal to C, but not spontaneously, rather by means of a premise: that every equal of an equal is equal.
Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'that every equal' - the better form is: that every equal of an equal to a thing is equal to that thing. So his saying: 'that every equal' means like Zayd.
Al-Attar: His saying: 'rather by means of a foreign premise' - they interpreted it as one whose terms are different from the terms of the premises of the syllogism, so that syllogisms clarified by way of simple conversion are included, and those clarified by way of inverse of the contrapositive are excluded. The reason is that they believed in the necessity of multiplicity of the middle term, which occurs in the simple conversion clarification but not in the inverse of the contrapositive nor in the syllogism of equality. But this necessity is not required by the definition of syllogism, according to the author. So on this basis, there is no reason to exclude the syllogism clarified by inverse of the contrapositive. This is supported by the statement of the commentator of al-Mutali': There is no reason to exclude the first, meaning the syllogism clarified by inverse of the contrapositive, from the definition of syllogism, especially since it is among the methods leading to certainty, and there is no difference between it and the proof established by simple conversion. End quote. Therefore Abd al-Hakim said: The difference between implication by conversion and implication by inverse of the contrapositive is an arbitrary decision whose basis has not become clear to me until now. End quote. Al-Isam said: There are other proofs that are excluded by the condition 'spontaneously', such as judging the major term over a class wider than that over which it judged the minor term, e.g., Zayd is a human, and every animal is walking. This produces without doubt: Zayd is walking, but by means of a premise necessary for the major premise, namely: every human is walking. Another example: judging the major term over what is equal to what it judged over the minor, e.g., Zayd is a human, and every speaking creature is an animal; it produces: Zayd is an animal. Another example: negating the major from all the others of what was negated from every minor, e.g., nothing of the human is a horse, and nothing of what is not a horse is a mule (?), it produces: nothing of the human is a mule, but by means of the fact that our saying 'nothing of the human is a horse' implies 'every human is not a horse'. It is not hidden that there is no reason to exclude these proofs from the definition of syllogism, and they are useful for certainty. (His saying: 'it is that every... etc.') means: the foreign premise in this example is that every... etc., not absolutely.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section 'On Syllogism', specifically discussing the so-called 'syllogism of equality' (qiyas al-musawat) and its relation to the definition of a syllogism. The main claim is that this form is not a true syllogism (qiyas) in the technical sense because it does not produce its conclusion 'spontaneously' (li dhatihi); rather, it requires an additional external premise. The commentators expand on this by explaining that the syllogism of equality is composed of two propositions where the predicate of the first is the subject of the second, e.g., 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C', which implies that A is equal to C. However, this implication does not follow directly from the two premises alone; it depends on the premise 'Everything equal to the equal of something is equal to that thing', which is a foreign premise (muqaddima ajnabiyya) whose terms are external to the original premises. Therefore, it is excluded from the definition of qiyas. The commentators then discuss whether other types of arguments, such as those using the inverse of the contrapositive ('aks al-naqid), are also excluded. Some argue that such arguments should not be excluded because the necessity of having multiple middle terms is not required by the definition. Al-Isam gives additional examples of arguments that are excluded by the condition 'spontaneously', such as those where the major term is wider or where conversion is needed. The overall discussion aims to clarify the exact boundaries of what constitutes a valid syllogism according to the logicians.

Key Terms
قياس المساواة
مقدمة أجنبية
العكس المستوي
عكس النقيض
لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) excluded from the definition of qiyas?
A) Because it produces a false conclusion.
B) Because it requires a foreign premise to generate the conclusion.
C) Because it is only used in geometry.
D) Because it has four terms.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism (qiyas) is only valid if its conclusion follows spontaneously (li dhatihi) from its premises; qiyas al-musawat is excluded because it requires a foreign premise to link the terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'The Quran is the word of God; everything that is the word of God is true; therefore the Quran is true.' Does it satisfy the condition of li dhatihi? Why or why not? (Hint: Consider whether any foreign premise is needed.)
?
Take the argument: 'Zayd is a human; every human is mortal; therefore Zayd is mortal.' Is this a qiyas? Check if the conclusion follows spontaneously.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
The Lidhatihi Condition and Conversion/Contraposition Debate
Commentary on lidhatihi and extraneous premise with conversion/contraposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a syllogism satisfies the lidhatihi condition and distinguish arguments that require an extraneous premise from those that do not, particularly in the context of conversion and contraposition.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism needs no hidden premise; the conclusion must follow by itself.

In Islamic logic, the definition of qiyas requires that the premises entail the conclusion by themselves (lidhatihi). But what counts as 'by itself'? This lesson unpacks the condition through the example of equality and the controversy over whether arguments via simple conversion or contraposition really satisfy it. You will see how commentators debated the role of the middle term and the definition of an extraneous premise.

Guided Path
1
Understand the equality example
Given: (a) equals (b) and (b) equals (c). The conclusion (a) equals (c) does not follow directly; it needs the added premise 'every equal of an equal is equal'. This added premise is extraneous because it brings in a new relation not present in the original premises. The conclusion is entailed, but not by itself.
The extraneous premise is recognized because its terms are different from the original terms.
2
Define 'extraneous premise'
The commentators define an extraneous premise as one whose terms are different from the terms of the syllogism's premises. This definition is used to include arguments clarified by simple conversion (aks mustawi) and exclude those by contraposition (aks al-naqid), because the latter supposedly changes the middle term.
This definition is contested; see next step.
3
Analyze the controversy over contraposition
Some logicians argued that contrapositive arguments require a middle term that is not identical, violating lidhatihi. But the commentator (and others) reject this, stating that the definition of qiyas does not require multiple middle terms. The debate shows that the condition is about whether the conclusion follows from the given premises as they stand, not about the method of transformation.
Quotes from al-Attar, Abd al-Hakim, and al-Isam support the inclusive view.
4
Test the condition on a simple argument
Consider: Zayd is human, every human is mortal, therefore Zayd is mortal. Here the conclusion follows directly without an extra premise; it satisfies lidhatihi. In contrast, 'Zayd is human, every animal is walking' does not directly yield 'Zayd is walking' without the hidden premise 'every human is walking'.
The latter fails the lidhatihi condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أجنبية، هي الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : أَنَّ كُلَّ مُسَاوِي) الأولى أنَّ كلَّ مُساوي المساوي لشيء؛ مُساءٍ لذلكَ الشَّيء، فَقولُه : (أَنَّ مُساوي)؛ أي: كَـ زيد).
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ : بَلْ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أَجْنَبِيَّةٍ) فَسَّروها بما تكون حدودها مغايرة لحدودِ مُقدِّماتِ القياس؛ حتّى يدخل فيه القياسات المبيَّنةُ بطريقِ العكس المستوي، ويخرج المبين بطريقِ عكس النَّقيض، وسبب ذلكَ أنَّهم اعتقدوا وجوب تكثر الحدّ الأوسط، وهو حاصل في المبيَّن بالعكس المستوي دونَ عكس النَّقيض ودون قياس المساواة، وهذا الوجوبُ مِمَّا لَا يَقتضيهِ تَعريفُ القياس؛ قالَهُ المصنِّفُ، فعلى هذا؛ لا وجهَ لإخراج القياس المبيَّنِ بعكس النَّقيض، ويؤيِّدُهُ مَا قالَهُ شارحُ المطالعِ : لَا وجهَ لإخراج الأوَّلِ، يعني : القياس المبيَّنَ بعكس النَّقيض عن تعريف القياس، معَ أَنَّهُ مِنَ الطَّرُقِ الموصلةِ، وَلَا فرقَ بينَهُ وبينَ الدَّليل المستلزم بواسطة العكس المستوي ا.هـ. ولذلك قال عبد الحكيم : والفرق بين الاستلزام بواسطة العكس وبينه بواسطة عكس النَّقيض؛ تحكُمْ لمْ يَظْهَرْ لي إلى الآنَ وَجهُهُ ا.هـ. قال العصام : وهناك أدلَّةٌ أُخرى تخرج بقيد لِذَاتِهِ، مثل أنْ يحكم بالأكبر على أعمّ مِمَّا حكم بِهِ على الأصغر، فَيُقالُ : زيدٌ إنسان، وكُلُّ حيوان ماش، فإنَّهُ ينتجُ بِلَا اشتباه زيد ماش، لكنَّه بواسطة مُقدِّمةٍ أزمةٍ لِلكُبرى، وَهِيَ كُلُّ إنسانٍ ماش، ومثلُ أنْ يحكم بالأكبر على ما يساوي ما حكم بِهِ على الأصغر نحو : زيد إنسان، وكُلُّ ناطق حيوان، ينتج : زيد حيوان، ومثلُ أَنْ يُسلَبَ الأكبرُ عَن جميع أغيارِ مَا سُلِبَ عن كُلِّ الأصغرِ فيُقالُ : لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الإِنسانِ بفرس، ولا شيء من غيرِ الفرس بِصمَّالٍ، ينتجُ : لا شيء مِنَ الإِنسانِ بِصمَّالٍ، لكنَّهُ بواسطة أنَّ قولَنا : لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس؛ يستلزم قولنا : كُلّ إنسانٍ غيرُ الفرس، ولا يخفى أنّه لا وجه لإخراج تلك الأدلَّةِ عَن حد القياس، وَهِيَ مُفيدة لليقين . (قَوْلُهُ : هِيَ أَنَّ كُلَّ ... إِلَخ) أي: المقدمة الأجنبية في هذا المثالِ إِلَخ، هذه لا مطلقاً .
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
ولهذا لم يتحقق ذلك الاستلزام إلا حيث تصدقُ هذه المقدمة، وحيثُ لا فَلَا، كقولنا: (أ) نصف (ب)، و(ب) نصف (ج)، لم يلزم منه أن يكونَ (أ) نصفَ (ج)؛ لأنَّ نصفَ النِّصْفِ لا يكون نصفاً
Extraneous: that it entails that (a) is equal to (c), but not by itself, rather by means of a premise that every equal of an equal is equal. Extraneous, it is al-Dasuqi. (His saying: 'every equal of') the preferred is that every equal of an equal of something is equal to that thing. So his saying: (that equal of) i.e., like Zayd. Al-Attar. (His saying: 'rather by means of an extraneous premise') they explained it as having terms different from the terms of the premises of the syllogism, so that the syllogisms clarified by the method of simple conversion enter, and those clarified by the method of contraposition exit. The reason for that is that they believed in the necessity of the multiplicity of the middle term, and that is realized in the one clarified by simple conversion but not in contraposition nor in the equality syllogism. This necessity is not required by the definition of the syllogism; the author said it. So on this basis, there is no justification for excluding the syllogism clarified by contraposition. It is supported by what the commentator of al-Mutali' said: There is no justification for excluding the first, i.e., the syllogism clarified by contraposition, from the definition of the syllogism, while it is among the ways that lead [to truth], and there is no difference between it and the proof that entails by means of simple conversion. End quote. Therefore Abd al-Hakim said: The difference between entailment by means of conversion and by means of contraposition is an arbitrary one; its justification has not become apparent to me until now. End quote. Al-Isam said: There are other proofs that are excluded by the condition of 'by itself', such as predicating the major of a more general thing than what it was predicated of the minor. It is said: Zayd is human, and every animal is walking; then it produces without doubt: Zayd is walking, but it is by means of a premise necessary for the major, which is 'every human is walking'. And like predicating the major of what is equal to what it was predicated of the minor, as in: Zayd is human, and every rational is animal; it produces: Zayd is animal. And like negating the major of all others of what was negated of all the minor. It is said: Nothing of the human is a horse, and nothing other than the horse is a mule; it produces: Nothing of the human is a mule, but it is by means of the fact that our statement 'nothing of the human is a horse' entails our statement 'every human is other than the horse'. It is not hidden that there is no justification for excluding those proofs from the definition of the syllogism, and they are useful for certainty. (His saying: 'it is that every... etc.') i.e., the extraneous premise in this example etc., this not absolutely. Section: On the syllogism. And for this reason, that entailment is not realized except where this premise is true, and where it is not, then not, such as our saying: (a) is half of (b), and (b) is half of (c); it does not entail that (a) is half of (c), because half of half is not half.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of qiyas (syllogism), specifically the condition that the entailment of the conclusion must be 'by itself' (lidhatihi) and not through an extraneous premise (muqaddimah ajnabiyyah). The commentator begins by giving an example: if we have that (a) is equal to (b) and (b) is equal to (c), the conclusion (a) is equal to (c) does not follow by itself; it requires the additional premise 'every equal of an equal is equal'. This additional premise is extraneous because its terms (e.g., 'equal') are different from the terms of the original premises. He then cites the view that an extraneous premise is defined as one whose terms differ from the terms of the syllogism's premises. This view is used to include syllogisms clarified by simple conversion (aks mustawi) and exclude those via contraposition (aks al-naqid), because the latter keeps the same middle term? But the commentator argues that the necessity of multiple middle terms is not part of the definition of qiyas, so there is no reason to exclude contrapositive arguments. He quotes al-Attar, Abd al-Hakim, and al-Isam to support this. Al-Isam gives additional examples of valid arguments that are excluded by 'lidhatihi' but are still productive of certainty, such as when the major term is predicated of a more general class than the minor (e.g., Zayd is human, every animal is walking - entails Zayd is walking, but via the premise 'every human is walking'), or when major is predicated of an equal (e.g., Zayd is human, every rational is animal - entails Zayd is animal), or negative cases. The commentator insists there is no justification for excluding them. Then a new section 'On the syllogism' continues the point: the extraneous premise is only true in some cases; for example, if (a) is half of (b) and (b) is half of (c), the conclusion (a) is half of (c) does not follow because half of half is not half, so the extraneous premise 'every half of a half is half' is false. The passage thus refines the definition of qiyas by clarifying the condition of lidhatihi and showing which arguments are properly included.

Key Terms
مقدمة أجنبية
لذاته
قياس
عكس مستوي
عكس نقيض

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did some logicians exclude arguments via contraposition from the definition of qiyas?
A) Because the middle term is not multiple in contrapositive arguments.
B) Because contrapositive arguments are not useful for certainty.
C) Because the extraneous premise in contraposition has different terms.
D) Because the definition of qiyas explicitly forbids contraposition.

Key Takeaway
The lidhatihi condition ensures that a syllogism's conclusion is entailed directly by its premises, and the debate over contraposition shows that the condition must be applied consistently without arbitrary distinctions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Extraneous Premises Beyond Conversion: Al-Isam's Examples and the Half Case
Additional examples of extraneous premises and the half case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify extraneous premises in arguments that involve predication of a broader class, equality, or negation, and assess whether they satisfy the lidhatihi condition.

Core Idea
Many seemingly valid arguments hide an extraneous premise; identifying it is key to assessing syllogistic validity.

The lidhatihi condition is not only relevant to conversion and contraposition. Al-Isam provides several other examples where the conclusion follows only by means of an extraneous premise, such as when the major term is predicated of a more general class than the minor, or when negation is involved. He argues that despite requiring an extra premise, these arguments are still useful for certainty and should not be excluded from the definition of qiyas. This lesson examines these examples and the half case that shows when the extraneous premise is false.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first example: predication of a more general class
Consider: Zayd is human, every animal is walking. The conclusion 'Zayd is walking' follows only if we add the premise 'every human is walking'. This extra premise is extraneous because its term 'human' is not the same as 'animal'. The conclusion is entailed, but not by itself.
The argumen is valid in content, but fails the lidhatihi condition.
2
Examine the second example: predication of an equal class
Consider: Zayd is human, every rational is animal. Conclusion: Zayd is animal. Again, the premise 'every human is rational' (or 'every human is animal') is missing. The terms 'rational' and 'animal' are not directly connected to 'human' in the given premises.
This also requires an extraneous premise.
3
Examine the negative example
Consider: No human is a horse, and nothing other than a horse is a mule. Conclusion: No human is a mule. This follows but only if we first convert 'No human is a horse' to 'Every human is non-horse', which is an immediate inference. The conversion step acts as an extraneous premise.
Al-Isam notes that despite this, the argument is useful for certainty.
4
Analyze the half example as a counterexample
Consider: (a) is half of (b), (b) is half of (c). The conclusion (a) is half of (c) does not follow because half of half is not half. The needed extraneous premise 'every half of a half is half' is false. This shows that not all arguments with an extraneous premise are valid; the extraneous premise must be true.
The lidhatihi condition alone does not guarantee truth; it only ensures directness.

(قَوْلُهُ : هِيَ أَنَّ كُلَّ ... إِلَخ) أي: المقدمة الأجنبية في هذا المثالِ إِلَخ، هذه لا مطلقاً .
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ
ولهذا لم يتحقق ذلك الاستلزام إلا حيث تصدقُ هذه المقدمة، وحيثُ لا فَلَا، كقولنا: (أ) نصف (ب)، و(ب) نصف (ج)، لم يلزم منه أن يكونَ (أ) نصفَ (ج)؛ لأنَّ نصفَ النِّصْفِ لا يكون نصفاً
Extraneous: that it entails that (a) is equal to (c), but not by itself, rather by means of a premise that every equal of an equal is equal. Extraneous, it is al-Dasuqi. (His saying: 'every equal of') the preferred is that every equal of an equal of something is equal to that thing. So his saying: (that equal of) i.e., like Zayd. Al-Attar. (His saying: 'rather by means of an extraneous premise') they explained it as having terms different from the terms of the premises of the syllogism, so that the syllogisms clarified by the method of simple conversion enter, and those clarified by the method of contraposition exit. The reason for that is that they believed in the necessity of the multiplicity of the middle term, and that is realized in the one clarified by simple conversion but not in contraposition nor in the equality syllogism. This necessity is not required by the definition of the syllogism; the author said it. So on this basis, there is no justification for excluding the syllogism clarified by contraposition. It is supported by what the commentator of al-Mutali' said: There is no justification for excluding the first, i.e., the syllogism clarified by contraposition, from the definition of the syllogism, while it is among the ways that lead [to truth], and there is no difference between it and the proof that entails by means of simple conversion. End quote. Therefore Abd al-Hakim said: The difference between entailment by means of conversion and by means of contraposition is an arbitrary one; its justification has not become apparent to me until now. End quote. Al-Isam said: There are other proofs that are excluded by the condition of 'by itself', such as predicating the major of a more general thing than what it was predicated of the minor. It is said: Zayd is human, and every animal is walking; then it produces without doubt: Zayd is walking, but it is by means of a premise necessary for the major, which is 'every human is walking'. And like predicating the major of what is equal to what it was predicated of the minor, as in: Zayd is human, and every rational is animal; it produces: Zayd is animal. And like negating the major of all others of what was negated of all the minor. It is said: Nothing of the human is a horse, and nothing other than the horse is a mule; it produces: Nothing of the human is a mule, but it is by means of the fact that our statement 'nothing of the human is a horse' entails our statement 'every human is other than the horse'. It is not hidden that there is no justification for excluding those proofs from the definition of the syllogism, and they are useful for certainty. (His saying: 'it is that every... etc.') i.e., the extraneous premise in this example etc., this not absolutely. Section: On the syllogism. And for this reason, that entailment is not realized except where this premise is true, and where it is not, then not, such as our saying: (a) is half of (b), and (b) is half of (c); it does not entail that (a) is half of (c), because half of half is not half.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of qiyas (syllogism), specifically the condition that the entailment of the conclusion must be 'by itself' (lidhatihi) and not through an extraneous premise (muqaddimah ajnabiyyah). The commentator begins by giving an example: if we have that (a) is equal to (b) and (b) is equal to (c), the conclusion (a) is equal to (c) does not follow by itself; it requires the additional premise 'every equal of an equal is equal'. This additional premise is extraneous because its terms (e.g., 'equal') are different from the terms of the original premises. He then cites the view that an extraneous premise is defined as one whose terms differ from the terms of the syllogism's premises. This view is used to include syllogisms clarified by simple conversion (aks mustawi) and exclude those via contraposition (aks al-naqid), because the latter keeps the same middle term? But the commentator argues that the necessity of multiple middle terms is not part of the definition of qiyas, so there is no reason to exclude contrapositive arguments. He quotes al-Attar, Abd al-Hakim, and al-Isam to support this. Al-Isam gives additional examples of valid arguments that are excluded by 'lidhatihi' but are still productive of certainty, such as when the major term is predicated of a more general class than the minor (e.g., Zayd is human, every animal is walking - entails Zayd is walking, but via the premise 'every human is walking'), or when major is predicated of an equal (e.g., Zayd is human, every rational is animal - entails Zayd is animal), or negative cases. The commentator insists there is no justification for excluding them. Then a new section 'On the syllogism' continues the point: the extraneous premise is only true in some cases; for example, if (a) is half of (b) and (b) is half of (c), the conclusion (a) is half of (c) does not follow because half of half is not half, so the extraneous premise 'every half of a half is half' is false. The passage thus refines the definition of qiyas by clarifying the condition of lidhatihi and showing which arguments are properly included.

لذاته
قياس
أكبر

Key Takeaway
Arguments with extraneous premises can still be valid and certain, but they do not fit the strict definition of qiyas; the lidhatihi condition ensures logical directness, not necessarily truth.

Compound Propositions and the Definition of Qiyas
Objection that the definition of qiyas includes compound propositions that entail their converse, and the response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a compound proposition qualifies as a qiyas based on the definition's requirement of multiple propositions.

Core Idea
A compound proposition is one proposition, not two — so it does not fit the definition of qiyas.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle objection to the standard definition of qiyas. The objection claims that certain compound propositions, such as conditionals that entail their own converse, could misleadingly fit the definition. We then follow the commentator's refinement that distinguishes between a single compound proposition and multiple premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection says the definition of qiyas includes a compound proposition that entails its converse (e.g., a conditional that implies its converse). Find the exact Arabic phrase: 'بقي أنَّه يدخل في التَّعريف القضيَّةُ المركّبة المستلزمة لعكسها'.
This is the core of the objection.
2
Understand why this is a problem
Such a proposition, though compound, is still a single proposition. The definition of qiyas requires multiple propositions (qadaya) to produce a conclusion. If a single proposition could produce a conclusion, the definition would be too broad.
The definition must be exclusive.
3
Analyse the response
The response states that 'propositions' in the definition means more than one. A compound proposition, even if composed of two clauses, is conventionally called a single proposition, not two. Therefore, it is excluded.
Al-Attar quotes the author's explanation.
4
Examine the counter-response
Abd al-Hakim objects: if a compound proposition is composed of two propositions, then it is also a 'statement composed of two propositions', which might still satisfy the definition. This leaves a tension.
This shows ongoing refinement.
5
Synthesise the definitional refinement
The key refinement: the definition requires numerically multiple propositions, not just structurally compound. This preserves the exclusivity of the definition.
This protects the validity of the definition.

(His saying: 'and where not, then not') i.e., where it is not true, then that entailment is not realized. (His saying: 'half of b... etc.') i.e., one is half of two, and two is half of four. (His saying: 'it does not entail from it... etc.') i.e., that one is half of four. (His saying: 'because half of... etc.') the better is that half of the half of something is not half of that thing. (His saying: 'it remains that the compound proposition enters into the definition') like: Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is writing, not permanently. Al-Attar: (His saying: 'it remains that it enters into the definition') i.e., there remains an objection that is raised against it, that it is not exclusive due to the entry of the mentioned matter. Then what the commentator mentioned here is taken from the author's statement in the commentary on al-Asl: What is meant by 'propositions' (qadaya) is more than one, so the single proposition that entails its converse or its contrapositive comes out of the definition of qiyas. As for the simple proposition, its exclusion is obvious. As for the compound, it is because, conventionally, it is called a single proposition composed of two propositions, and it is not called two propositions. By this, the objection against the definition of qiyas that it includes the compound proposition that entails its converse or its contrapositive is repelled. End quote. And Abd al-Hakim objected to him that if it is true of it that it is a single proposition composed of two propositions, then it is true of it that it is a statement composed of two propositions.
Source pages p. 771

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the definition of qiyas. It raises an objection: the definition might include a compound proposition (qadiyya murakkaba) that entails its converse or contrapositive. For example, 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is writing, not permanently' is a compound conditional proposition that, by itself, entails something like 'If something is moving fingers as long as it is writing, then it is a writer'? Actually, the example is not fully specified, but it's a compound proposition that entails its converse. The objection is that such a compound proposition is just one proposition, but the definition of qiyas requires multiple propositions (qadaya) to produce a conclusion. So if this compound proposition is considered a single proposition, it should not be counted as a qiyas. Al-Attar explains the response: the definition says 'propositions' in the plural (qadaya) meaning more than one. A single compound proposition, even if composed of two clauses, is conventionally called one proposition, not two. Therefore, it does not fulfill the condition of being a composition of two propositions, so it is excluded from the definition. However, Abd al-Hakim objects: if it is true that it is a single proposition composed of two, then it can still be considered a statement composed of two propositions (since it has two parts). This leaves the issue somewhat unresolved. The passage thus refines the definition by addressing this potential counterexample.

Understanding 'Qadaya' in the Definition of Qiyas
Commentary clarifies that 'qadaya' means more than one proposition, including implicit ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify what qualifies as 'qadaya' in the definition of qiyas and distinguish cases where one premise is implicit.

Core Idea
Qadaya means at least two propositions, one may be implied.

The definition of qiyas (syllogism) includes the phrase 'qawl mu'allaf min qadaya' – a statement composed of propositions. But what exactly counts as a 'qadaya'? The commentator clarifies that it means more than one proposition in the linguistic sense, and goes further: even if one proposition is not explicitly stated but implied by context, it still qualifies. This expands the scope of qiyas beyond simple two-premise syllogisms, as long as the inference involves active reasoning (nazar).

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of qiyas
Find the phrase 'qawl mu'allaf min qadaya yalzimu lahu li-dhatihi qawl akhar' in the text. This is the core definition being discussed.
2
Understand the clarification of 'qadaya'
The commentator states that 'qadaya' means 'ma fawqa qadiyya wahida' (more than one proposition). This includes cases where one proposition is implicit (muqaddara).
The implicit premise is mentally supplied.
3
Examine the example given
The example 'Zaid is breathing, therefore he is alive' is provided. The premise 'Everything that breathes is alive' is not stated, but the mind arrives at it automatically.
This shows how implicit premises are accepted.
4
Distinguish qiyas from mere logical entailment
The commentator contrasts qiyas with the entailment of a proposition's converse. The latter occurs 'bi hasab nafs al-amr' (according to the matter itself), not through active reasoning (nazar), so it does not qualify as qiyas.
Active intellectual reflection is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي القِيَاسِ فَإِنَّ الْمُرَادَ بِالْقَضَايَا مَا فَوْقَ قَضِيَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، وَكَذَا كُلُّ جَمْعٍ يُسْتَعْمَلُ فِي هَذَا الْفَنِّ . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ الْمُرَادَ) علةٌ لقوله : ( أنه يدخل ... إلخ)؛ أي: وحينئذ؛ شمل التعريفُ القضيَّةَ المركَّبَةَ؛ لأنَّها مُركَّبَةٌ من قضيَّتين، وحينئذ؛ فالتعريف غيرُ مانع . العطار استلزام المقدِّمتَيْنِ النَّتيجةَ بطريقِ النَّظَرِ ؛ أي: الفكر، بحيثُ ينتقل من مقدِّمتي الدليل إلى النَّتيجة بالحركة الفكريَّةِ الواقعة في التَّرتيب، وهذا المعنى مفقود في لزوم العكس للقضيَّةِ، فإنَّ استلزام القضيَّةِ عكسها مُعتبر بحسب نفس الأمرِ، لَا أَنَّهُ ينتقلُ مِنَ العلم بالقضيَّةِ إلى عكسها كَمَا في الدَّليل؛ إذْ كثيراً ما نعلمُ قضايا، وَلَا يخطرُ بِبَالِنَا عكسها بل لا نعلمه، وإلا ; لزمَ أَنَّهُ عند حصولِ العلم بقضيَّةٍ مِنَ القضايا ; يحصلُ علوم غيرُ مُتناهية; لأنَّ عكسها قضيَّةٌ أُخرى ينتقل منها لعكسِهَا ، وَهَلُمَّ جَرَّا ، والاستدلال على عكس القضيَّةِ استدلال على الحكم بأنَّ هذه القضيَّةَ عكسها ، كَذَا فهو كالاستدلال على سائر الأحكام، فلا يفيد أن استلزام القضيَّةِ العكس بطريقِ النَّظَرِ بالمعنى الَّذِي فَهِمَهُ الباحث، بل معناه أنَّ هذا الاستلزام ثابت بالنَّظَرِ؛ لَا أَنَّهُ نفسُ النَّظَرِ، وفرَّقَ بين المستدلّ عليه والمستدلّ بِهِ، فالقضيَّةُ وَعكسها مِن قبيل الأوَّلِ، ومقدِّمات الدَّليل مِنْ قَبيل الثَّاني، والباحث لم يفرّق بينَهُمَا ، فوقع في الغلط الفاحش، وأنا لا أعجبُ إلَّا مِن تكثيرِ السَّوادِ في الأوراق بمثل هذه الأوهام. (قَوْلُهُ : فَإِنَّ الْمُرَادَ بِالْقَضَايَا ... إلَخ) تعليلٌ لِقولهِ : بقي؛ أي: وإذا كان المراد بها كَذَلِكَ ; تكون المركَّبةُ المذكورة داخلةٌ فى التَّعريف ; لأنَّه يصدق عليها أنَّها قول مؤلف من قضايا يلزمُهُ لِذَاتِهِ قولٌ آخَرُ . (قَوْلُهُ : مَا فَوْقَ قَضِيَّةٍ سواء كانتا مذكورتين أو إحداهما مُقدَّرةٌ نحو: فلانٌ مُتَنَفِّسٌ فَهُو حَيٌّ، وَلَمَّا كَانَتِ الشَّمس طالعة فالنَّهارُ مَوجودٌ، فَإِنَّ الذَّهَنَ يتأدَّى إلى المقدرةِ وإنْ لم تُذكر . (قَوْلُهُ : وَكَذَا كُلُّ جَمْعِ يُسْتَعْمَلُ فِي هَذَا الْفَنِّ) أي : كالقضايا في هذه الإرادة؛ أي : كُلُّ جمع يُستعمل في فنّ المنطقِ يُرادُ منه الجمعُ اللُّغوي، وهوَ مَا يشملُ مَا فوق الواحد ; لأنَّهُ في اللُّغةِ ما يكون أفراده مُتعدّدة; سواء كانت فوق فردٍ واحدٍ أو أكثر .
Chapter: On Syllogism. Indeed, what is meant by 'propositions' is more than one proposition, and likewise every plural used in this art. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'Indeed what is meant' is the cause for his statement: 'that it includes...' i.e., and then the definition includes the compound proposition, because it is composed of two propositions, and then the definition is not exclusive. Al-Attar: The entailment of the two premises of the conclusion by way of consideration, i.e., thought, such that one moves from the two premises of the proof to the conclusion by the intellectual motion occurring in the arrangement. This meaning is absent in the entailment of the converse of a proposition, for the entailment of a proposition's converse is considered according to the matter itself, not that one moves from knowledge of the proposition to its converse as in the proof; for often we know propositions and do not conceive their converse, rather we do not know it. Otherwise, it would follow that when knowledge of a proposition is obtained, infinite knowledge would be obtained, because its converse is another proposition from which one could move to its converse, and so on. And reasoning about the converse of a proposition is reasoning about the judgment that this proposition is its converse, so it is like reasoning about other judgments. So it does not entail that the entailment of the proposition's converse is by way of consideration in the sense understood by the researcher; rather, its meaning is that this entailment is established by consideration, not that it is the consideration itself. And he distinguished between what is sought by reasoning and what is used in reasoning. The proposition and its converse are of the first kind, while the premises of the proof are of the second kind. The researcher did not distinguish between them, so he fell into a gross error. And I am not surprised except at the increase of black on paper with such illusions. His statement: 'Indeed what is meant by propositions...' is an explanation of his statement: 'remaining'; i.e., if what is meant is thus, then the mentioned compound is included in the definition, because it is true that it is a statement composed of propositions from which another statement follows by itself. His statement: 'More than one proposition, whether both are mentioned or one is implicit, like: Zaid is breathing, therefore he is alive, and since the sun is rising, the day exists, for the mind arrives at the implicit one even if not mentioned. His statement: 'And likewise every plural used in this art' i.e., like 'propositions' in this intended meaning; i.e., every plural used in the art of logic, what is meant by it is the linguistic plural, which includes what is above one, because in language it is what has multiple individuals, whether they are above one individual or more.
Source pages p. 773

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of qiyas (syllogism) in Islamic logic. The commentator begins by clarifying that the term 'qadaya' (propositions) in the definition 'qawl mu'allaf min qadaya yalzimu lahu li-dhatihi qawl akhar' (a statement composed of propositions from which another statement follows by itself) means 'more than one proposition' in the linguistic sense, not necessarily exactly two explicit premises. He then discusses an objection: some had argued that if two propositions are merely mentioned together but not actually composed into a single argument (like when they entail their converse or contrapositive), they would still satisfy the definition. The commentator rebuts this by stating that the entailment in qiyas must be 'bi-tariq al-nazar' (by way of reflection/reasoning), i.e., a conscious intellectual movement from premises to conclusion. The entailment of a proposition's converse is not of this type; it is a matter of logical implication in itself, not a result of reasoning. He gives an example: we often know a proposition but do not think of its converse. If we had to know all converses, infinite knowledge would result. Thus, the definition requires the premises to be arranged and the conclusion to be derived through active reasoning. The commentator then provides examples of compound propositions that do qualify as qiyas, such as 'Zaid is breathing, therefore he is alive' where the premise 'everything that breathes is alive' is implicit. This is accepted because the mind automatically supplies the missing premise. Finally, he reiterates that all plural terms in logic (like 'qadaya', 'muqaddimat') are to be understood in the linguistic sense of 'more than one', not necessarily exactly two.

Key Terms
قضايا (qadaya)
قياس (qiyas)
مقدّرة (muqaddara)
نظر (nazar)
لزوم (luzum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is included in the definition of qiyas as 'qawl mu'allaf min qadaya'?
A) A single proposition
B) Two propositions that entail their converse
C) A compound statement like 'Zaid is breathing, therefore he is alive' where the second premise is implicit
D) A disjunctive syllogism without any implicit premise

Key Takeaway
In the definition of qiyas, 'qadaya' means more than one proposition, and one of them can be implicit as long as the inference involves active reasoning (nazar).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the statement: 'The sun has risen, so the day has come.' Identify the implicit premise and explain why this qualifies as a qiyas according to the commentator's conditions.
?
2. Explain why the inference 'This proposition is true, therefore its converse (not-B → not-A) is true' does NOT qualify as a qiyas based on the 'bi-tariq al-nazar' condition.

The Definition of Qiyas and the Problem of Compound Propositions
Commentator on whether compound propositions are included in the definition of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why compound propositions pose a problem for the classical definition of qiyas and evaluate the proposed solution of restricting the definition to independent propositions.

Core Idea
Definition must be exclusive; compound conditionals challenge this by containing multiple implicit propositions.

In this passage, the commentator al-Dasuqi examines a critical problem in the definition of qiyas (syllogism): does it include compound propositions like 'Zayd is breathing, so he is alive'? Such statements are composed of multiple judgments but are often expressed with dependent terms like 'non-permanence' and 'non-necessity'. The discussion reveals how subtle distinctions in the meaning of 'propositions' affect whether the definition remains exclusive (man').

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas under discussion
Find the definition in the passage: 'a statement composed of propositions which by itself entails another statement.' Note that 'propositions' (qadaya) is key.
The definition is the standard one.
2
Understand why compound propositions seem to fall under it
Compound propositions like 'Zayd is breathing, so he is alive' contain more than one proposition (the antecedent and consequent). In a linguistic sense, 'propositions' means more than one, so they would be included.
This makes the definition not exclusive (ghayr mani').
3
Examine the proposed answer: restrict 'propositions' to independent propositions
The answer suggests that 'propositions' in the definition means independent propositions (qadaya mustaqilla), where each judgment is expressed fully. Compound propositions use dependent expressions (ladawam, ladarurah) and thus are excluded.
This would make the definition exclusive.
4
Analyze the critique of this answer
Al-Dasuqi and al-Attar criticize the answer: the term 'independent' is not immediately obvious, and the link between compound propositions and syllogism premises is not necessary. Rajab Afandi's objection that 'non-permanence' would no longer be a proposition is also refuted.
The weakness lies in relying on an unintended interpretation.
5
Recognize the logical distinction between a proof and the entailment of a converse
The passage also distinguishes between a sylogism (a process of reasoning) and the mere entailment of a converse (a fact of the matter). The researcher confused them, leading to error.
This distinction helps clarify what counts as a proper qiyas.
